The Ruling on Iddah for Non-Standard Sexual Relations
Addresses iddah for non-standard sexual acts and rebuts a counterargument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the Hanafi ruling on iddah when semen is inserted without vaginal penetration, and identify the scholarly response to the objection from al-Nahr.

Core Idea
Semen insertion without full vaginal penetration triggers iddah because pregnancy can still occur, necessitating waiting to establish uterine purity.

This lesson examines a nuanced scenario: a husband engages in anal intercourse or inserts semen into his wife's vagina without penile penetration, then divorces her. The commentator argues that iddah is required, citing the need to ascertain purity. He also responds to a critique from al-Nahr, clarifying that the waiting period for pregnancy is not itself the iddah but a separate matter.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and the two opinions
The passage describes two acts: (1) vaginal intercourse (wat'i) in the anus, and (2) inserting semen into the vagina (idkhal al-mani) without penile penetration. The commentator notes that the Shafi'i school requires iddah in both cases, and he says it is not far-fetched to rule according to the second opinion (requiring iddah) from the Hanafi school as well.
The commentator is extending the Hanafi ruling to cover the case of semen insertion, which is not explicitly mentioned in earlier texts.
2
Understand the rationale for requiring iddah
The commentator argues that insertion of semen requires ascertaining uterine purity more than mere vaginal penetration because pregnancy can result. Thus, iddah is necessary to determine if the woman is pregnant or not.
This is based on the core purpose of iddah: to establish barra'at al-rahim (purity of the womb).
3
Analyze the objection from al-Nahr
Al-Nahr states that if pregnancy appears, her iddah is until delivery; otherwise, no iddah. The commentator responds that this waiting period for pregnancy is not itself the iddah; rather, the original iddah applies, and the pregnancy period is superimposed. The objection is flawed because it confuses the two.
The commentator argues that if she remarries during the waiting period and then no pregnancy is found, the remarriage is valid, proving that the waiting period is not iddah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثلاثة اقراء وثلاثة أشهر إن لم تحض وللموت أربعة أشهر وعشر والأمة قرآن ونصف هذا قوله : (عدة الحرة للطلاق أو الفسخ ثلاثة أقراء أي حيض ظاهر في أن العدة اسم للأجل المضروب كما في البدائع على إرادة مدة ثلاثة أقراء لأنه أوقع ثلاثة خبراً للعدة على تقدير الرفع فهو مخالف لما قدمناه من التحقيق، وأما على تقدير نصب «ثلاثة» فالمراد كون عدتها في مدة ثلاثة أقراء لأن الحرمات تتعلق في مدة الأقراء فكان ظرف زمان معرباً واقعاً خبراً عن اسم معنى نحو «السفر غداً لكنه على تقدير الرفع اعتبر فيه الإطلاق المجازي أعني إطلاق العدة على نفس المدة أطلق الطلاق فشمل البائن والرجعي ولم يقيد بالدخول بناء على أن الأصل في النكاح الدخول ولا بد منه حقيقة أو حكماً حتى تجب على مطلقة بعد الخلوة ولو فاسدة كما بيناه فيها، ولم أر حكم ما إذا وطئها في دبرها أو أدخلت منيه في فرجها ثم طلقها من غير إيلاج في قبلها. وفي تحرير الشافعية وجوبها فيهما ولا بعد أن يحكم على المذهب بالثاني لأن إدخال المني محتاج إلى تعرف البراءة أكثر من مجرد الإيلاج والأصل في النوع قوله تعالى والمطلقات يتربصن بأنفسهن ثلاثة قروء [البقرة: ٢٢٨] والمراد بهن المدخولات اللاتي يحضن وهو خبر بمعنى الأمر وأصل الكلام ليتربصن ولام الأمر محذوفة فاستغني عن ذكره، وإخراج الأمر في صورة الخبر تأكيد له وللإشعار بأنه مما يتلقى بالمسارعة إلى امتثاله نحو قولهم في الدعاء رحمك الله أخرج في صورة الخبر ثقة بالاستجابة كأن الرحمة وجدت فهو يخبر عنها. وبناؤه على المبتدأ يدل على زيادة التأكيد، ولو قيل يتربص المطلقات لم يكن بتلك الوكادة لأن الجملة الإسمية تدل على الدوام والثبات بخلاف الفعلية. وفي ذكر الأنفس تهييج لهن على التربص وزيادة تعب إذ نفوسهن طوامح إلى الرجال فأمرن أن يقمعن أنفسهن ويغلبنها على الطموح ويجبرنها على التربص وانتصب «ثلاثة» على الظرف أي مدة ثلاثة قروء وجاء المميز على جمع الكثرة دون القلة التي هي ! الأقراء لجواز استعمال أحد الجمعين مكان الآخر لاشتراكهما في الجمعية، ولعل القروء أكثر في جمع القرء من الأقراء فأوثر عليه تنزيلاً لقليل الاستعمال منزلة المهمل؛ كذا في المعراج. والقرء مشترك بين الحيض والطهر، وأوله أصحابنا في الآية بالحيض، والشافعي بالطهر وموضعه الأصول.
قوله : (ولا بعد أن يحكم على المذهب بالثاني الخ قال في النهر وأقول : ينبغي أن يقال إن ظهر حملها كان عدتها وضع الحمل وإلا فلا عدة عليها ا هـ . واعترضه بعض الفضلاء بأن الانتظار إلى ظهور الحمل وعدمه هو العدة التي فررت منها وإن جوزت تزوجها بعد إدخال المني احتجت إلى نقل ا هـ. وفيه نظر فإنا لا نسلم أن الانتظار المذكور هو العدة فإنه بناء على ما بحثه في النهر لو انتظرت ظهور الحمل في تلك المدة وكانت تزوجت في أثنائها ثم ظهر عدم الحمل صح النكاح وقول ذلك القائل وإن جوزت تزوجها الخ يقال عليه هذا طلاق قبل دخول فلا عدة له فالنكاح بعده صحيح، وعدم تصحيحه هو المحتاج إلى الدليل بإثبات أن إدخال المني موجب للعدة والنزاع إنما هو في
Three quru' and three months if she does not menstruate, and for death four months and ten days, and the slave woman two quru'. This is his statement: (The iddah of a free woman for divorce or annulment is three quru'), meaning menstrual cycles. It is apparent that iddah is a name for the prescribed term... and the original source for this type is the verse: 'And divorced women shall wait for three quru' (2:228). And quru' is ambiguous between menstruation and purity. Our scholars interpreted it in the verse as menstruation, while al-Shafi'i interpreted it as purity, and its detailed discussion is in usul.
Source pages p. 216

Commentary
This passage presents the core ruling on iddah durations: for a free woman divorced or annulled, three quru' (menstrual cycles); if she does not menstruate, three months; for death of husband, four months and ten days; for a slave woman, two cycles. The commentator then analyzes the text grammatically and theologically. He notes that the word 'iddah' can refer to the prescribed period itself or the time span. He also discusses a hypothetical case of anal intercourse or inserting semen into the vagina without penetration; the Hanafi school requires iddah if semen is inserted because it potentially indicates pregnancy, and the commentator favors this view. The central proof is Quran 2:228, which is analyzed rhetorically: it is in indicative form but has imperative meaning, giving emphasis. The key dispute is the meaning of 'quru'': Hanafis say it means menstruation (hayd), Shafi'is say purity (tuhr). The commentator then addresses a rebuttal from al-Nahr regarding the case of semen insertion, arguing that the waiting to see if pregnancy appears is not itself iddah.

Key Terms
إدخال المني (idkhal al-mani): Insertion of semen into the vagina without penile penetration.
براءة الرحم (barra'at al-rahim): Purity of the womb, i.e., confirmation that the woman is not pregnant from the previous marriage.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the commentator in this passage, what is the basis for requiring iddah for semen insertion without vaginal penetration?
A) It is directly mentioned in the Quran
B) The need to ascertain uterine purity is even greater than in normal intercourse
C) It is a punishment for the husband
D) The Shafi'i school requires it, so the Hanafi school follows

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, iddah is required even for non-standard sexual relations if they can cause pregnancy, and a waiting period for pregnancy does not replace the iddah.

6 min
The 'Idda of a Free Woman after Divorce is Three Quru'
Defining the 'idda and its proof from the Quran with the Hanafi-Shafi'i dispute on quru'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the 'idda of a free woman, cite the Quranic proof, and explain the dispute with the Shafi'i school over the meaning of 'quru''.

Core Idea
The 'idda of a free woman is three menstrual periods, based on the Quranic command in Surah al-Baqarah.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq defines the 'idda for a free woman after divorce or annulment as three quru'. The commentator explains the linguistic and grammatical features of the Quranic verse (2:228) and highlights the key dispute over the meaning of 'quru''. Understanding this ruling requires familiarity with the Hanafi interpretation of the verse and its legal implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is the 'idda (waiting period) for a free woman after divorce or annulment. The ruling is that it consists of three quru' (menstrual periods).
The term 'quru' is disputed; the Hanafi school defines it as menstruation (hayd).
2
Locate the dalil
The primary proof is the Quranic verse: 'And divorced women shall wait by themselves for three quru'' (Quran 2:228). This verse is the main textual evidence.
The verse is decisive in transmission (qat'i al-thubut) but its interpretation is ambiguous (zanni al-dalala).
3
Analyze the wajh al-dalalah
The imperative verb 'yatarabbasna' (they shall wait) indicates a command, signifying obligation (wujub) in Hanafi usul. The nominal construction emphasizes continuity and firmness of the command.
The phrase 'bi-anfusihinna' stresses self-control, urging women to restrain themselves.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
The Shafi'i school interprets 'quru' as periods of purity (tuhr), not menstruation. This changes the calculation of the 'idda: it ends after three tuhrs instead of three menstrual periods.
The dispute is rooted in linguistic polysemy of 'qara' and is resolved through preferred usage and purpose.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih
The Hanafi position is considered mufta bihi (the relied-upon opinion) within the school. It is based on predominant Arabic usage, hadith evidence, and the objective of the 'idda (ascertaining purity of the womb).
The thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence) is the timing of when the woman may remarry.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الطلاق باب العدة ثلاثة اقراء وثلاثة أشهر إن لم تحض وللموت أربعة أشهر وعشر والأمة قرآن ونصف هذا قوله : (عدة الحرة للطلاق أو الفسخ ثلاثة أقراء أي حيض ظاهر في أن العدة اسم للأجل المضروب كما في البدائع على إرادة مدة ثلاثة أقراء لأنه أوقع ثلاثة خبراً للعدة على تقدير الرفع فهو مخالف لما قدمناه من التحقيق، وأما على تقدير نصب «ثلاثة» فالمراد كون عدتها في مدة ثلاثة أقراء لأن الحرمات تتعلق في مدة الأقراء فكان ظرف زمان معرباً واقعاً خبراً عن اسم معنى نحو «السفر غداً لكنه على تقدير الرفع اعتبر فيه الإطلاق المجازي أعني إطلاق العدة على نفس المدة أطلق الطلاق فشمل البائن والرجعي ولم يقيد بالدخول بناء على أن الأصل في النكاح الدخول ولا بد منه حقيقة أو حكماً حتى تجب على مطلقة بعد الخلوة ولو فاسدة كما بيناه فيها، ولم أر حكم ما إذا وطئها في دبرها أو أدخلت منيه في فرجها ثم طلقها من غير إيلاج في قبلها. وفي تحرير الشافعية وجوبها فيهما ولا بعد أن يحكم على المذهب بالثاني لأن إدخال المني محتاج إلى تعرف البراءة أكثر من مجرد الإيلاج والأصل في النوع قوله تعالى والمطلقات يتربصن بأنفسهن ثلاثة قروء [البقرة: ٢٢٨] والمراد بهن المدخولات اللاتي يحضن وهو خبر بمعنى الأمر وأصل الكلام ليتربصن ولام الأمر محذوفة فاستغني عن ذكره، وإخراج الأمر في صورة الخبر تأكيد له وللإشعار بأنه مما يتلقى بالمسارعة إلى امتثاله نحو قولهم في الدعاء رحمك الله أخرج في صورة الخبر ثقة بالاستجابة كأن الرحمة وجدت فهو يخبر عنها. وبناؤه على المبتدأ يدل على زيادة التأكيد، ولو قيل يتربص المطلقات لم يكن بتلك الوكادة لأن الجملة الإسمية تدل على الدوام والثبات بخلاف الفعلية. وفي ذكر الأنفس تهييج لهن على التربص وزيادة تعب إذ نفوسهن طوامح إلى الرجال فأمرن أن يقمعن أنفسهن ويغلبنها على الطموح ويجبرنها على التربص وانتصب «ثلاثة» على الظرف أي مدة ثلاثة قروء وجاء المميز على جمع الكثرة دون القلة التي هي الأقراء لجواز استعمال أحد الجمعين مكان الآخر لاشتراكهما في الجمعية، ولعل القروء أكثر في جمع القرء من الأقراء فأوثر عليه تنزيلاً لقليل الاستعمال منزلة المهمل؛ كذا في المعراج. والقرء مشترك بين الحيض والطهر، وأوله أصحابنا في الآية بالحيض، والشافعي بالطهر وموضعه الأصول.
Book of divorce, chapter of 'idda: Three quru' and three months if she does not menstruate, and for death four months and ten days, and for a slave woman two quru'. This: He said: (The 'idda of a free woman for divorce or annulment is three quru', i.e., menstruations, apparent in that 'idda is the name for the prescribed period... the origin in this type is the saying of the Most High: 'And divorced women shall wait by themselves for three quru'' [al-Baqarah 2:228]. They are intended as those who have been entered upon and who menstruate; it is a report in the meaning of a command, and the original statement is 'let them wait', and the lam of command is deleted... And quru' is shared between menstruation and purity, and our companions interpreted it in the verse as menstruation, and al-Shafi'i interpreted it as purity, and its place is in usul.
Source pages p. 217

Commentary
This passage defines the 'idda for a free woman after divorce or annulment as three menstrual periods. The commentator explains that 'idda refers to the prescribed term, and the word 'three' can be analyzed grammatically as a predicate or an adverb. The verse from al-Baqarah is the primary proof, and its style (a nominal sentence) indicates emphasis and continuity, which strengthens the obligation. A key point is the meaning of 'qara''; the Hanafi school holds it signifies menstruation (hayd) while al-Shafi'i holds it signifies purity (tuhr). This difference affects how the 'idda is completed: in the Hanafi view, it ends after the third menstruation and subsequent purity; in the Shafi'i view, it ends after the third purity. The passage also touches on cases like anal intercourse and insertion of semen, noting that some Shafi'is require 'idda in such cases, but the Hanafi position is not finalized here.

Key Terms
العدة (al-'idda): The prescribed waiting period after the dissolution of marriage, during which the woman cannot remarry.
القرء (al-qara'): In this passage, interpreted by Hanafis as menstruation (hayd), and by Shafi'is as purity (tuhr).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what does the term 'quru'' in Surah al-Baqarah 2:228 refer to?
A) Menstruation (hayd)
B) Purity (tuhr)
C) Both menstruation and purity
D) A period of three months

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school rules that the 'idda of a free woman after divorce is three menstrual periods, based on the Quranic verse and the interpretation of 'quru'' as hayd.

Classification of Separations: Faskh vs Raf' in Hanafi Law
Defending a threefold division of marital separations

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between faskh and raf' and explain how the classification affects 'idda and the count of talaq.

Core Idea
Marriage after completion cannot be annulled (faskh); separation by ownership or kinship is raf', not talaq.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq confronts a critical intra-Hanafi dispute: are separations after consummation limited to talaq and faskh, or is there a third category called raf'? The text defends the threefold division, with raf' covering cases like one spouse purchasing the other. Understanding this classification is essential for correctly applying 'idda rules and tracking the number of available divorces.

Guided Path
1
Identify the type of separation
Determine whether the separation is by talaq, by annulment (faskh), or by lifting (raf'). For faskh and raf', identify whether the marriage was consummated (tamm) or not.
The text uses 'before completion' (qabl al-tamam) and 'after completion' (ba'd al-tamam) as the key distinction.
2
Apply the principle of completion
If the marriage is complete (consummated), the author states it does not admit of faskh. Any separation without talaq is raf'. If not complete, such separations are faskh.
The Hanafi principle: after dukhul, only talaq or judicial decree can dissolve the marriage; options like khiyar al-bulugh are no longer available.
3
Note the intra-Hanafi dispute
Some scholars (ahl al-dar) argue for a binary division: all separations are either talaq or faskh. They classify a kiss that causes suckling as faskh, not raf'. The author of al-Nahr defends the threefold division.
The practical difference: raf' does not reduce the number of talaqs, while faskh may affect 'idda differently.
4
Evaluate the praktical consequence (thamarah)
Consider a scenario where a husband kisses his wife's mother (establishing a suckling relationship). According to the threefold view, the wife becomes forbidden for marriage (raf'), but the husband retains his remaining talaqs. According to the binary view, it is faskh and may count as one talaq? (The text is unclear but the implication is that faskh reduces the count).
This directly impacts the number of divorces the husband can still issue and the 'idda period.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (ولا بعد أن يحكم على المذهب بالثاني الخ قال في النهر : وأقول : ينبغي أن يقال إن ظهر حملها كان عدتها وضع الحمل وإلا فلا عدة عليها ا هـ . واعترضه بعض الفضلاء بأن الانتظار إلى ظهور الحمل وعدمه هو العدة التي فررت منها وإن جوزت تزوجها بعد إدخال المني احتجت إلى نقل ا هـ. وفيه نظر فإنا لا نسلم أن الانتظار المذكور هو العدة فإنه بناء على ما بحثه في النهر لو انتظرت ظهور الحمل في تلك المدة وكانت تزوجت في أثنائها ثم ظهر عدم الحمل صح النكاح وقول ذلك القائل وإن جوزت تزوجها الخ يقال عليه هذا طلاق قبل دخول فلا عدة له فالنكاح بعده صحيح، وعدم تصحيحه هو المحتاج إلى الدليل بإثبات أن إدخال المني موجب للعدة والنزاع إنما هو في منه ومن هذا هذا وفائدة الخلاف تظهر فيما إذا طلقها في الطهر فإنه تنقضي العدة برؤية قطرة من الدم من الحيضة الثالثة عنده وعندنا لا تنقضي العدة ما لم تطهر منها؛ كذا في غاية البيان . وفي المبسوط : الحيضة الأولى لتعرف براءة الرحم، والثانية لحرمة النكاح، والثالثة لفضيلة الحرية وشمل جميع أسبابه من الفسخ بخيار البلوغ والعتق وملك أحد الزوجين صاحبه وردة أحدهما، وقدمنا في نكاح الأولياء جملة الفرق والإيراد على قولهم أنه لا يحتمل الفسخ بعد التمام، ثم رأيت في إيضاح الاصلاح هنا أنه لا فرق بين الطلاق أو الفسخ أو الرفع ثم قال : اعلم أن النكاح بعد التمام لا يحتمل الفسخ فكل فرقة بغير طلاق قبل تمام النكاح كالفرقة بخيار البلوغ والفرقة بخيار العتق والفرق بعدم الكفاءة فسخ، وكل فرقة بغير طلاق بعد تمام النكاح كالفرقة بملك أحد الزوجين الآخر والفرقة بتقبيل ابن الزوج ونحوه رفع وهذا واضح عند من له خبرة في هذا الفن اهـ. الكفاءة وعدم النوع ما إذا تزوج المكاتب بنت مولاه بإذنه ثم مات المكاتب بعد موت المولى لاعن وفاء فإن النكاح يفسد وتعتد بثلاث حيض إن كانت مدخولاً بها وسقط مهرها بقدر ما ملكت وإلا فلا عدة، وإن مات عن وفاء تعتد عدة الوفاة دخل بها أو لم يدخل، ولها الصداق والإرث لأنا حكمنا بعتقه في آخر جزء من أجزاء حياته، وقدمنا في فصل التحليل أن العدة لا تظهر في حق المطلق حيث كان دون الثلاث وهكذا في الفسخ، فلو اشترى زوجته بعد الدخول لا عدة عليها له وتعتد لغيره حتى لا يزوجها من الغير ما لم تحض حيضتين، ولهذا لو طلقها السيد في هذه العدة لم يقع طلاقه لأنها معتدة بالنسبة إلى غيره ولهذا تحل له بملك اليمين بخلاف ما إذا اشترت الحرة زوجها بعد الدخول وقد كان قال لها أنت طالق للسنة وهي حائض ثم ظهرت من حيضها وقع الطلاق لعدم ارتفاع عدة الطلاق بدليل حرمة وطئها ولا بد في انقضاء عدتها من الإقرار بالطلاق لأنه لو طلقها وأقام معها زماناً منكراً طلاقها لم تنقض عدتها ؛ هكذا اختاره المشايخ كذا في المحيط وسيأتي زيادة بيان له ولو اشترى المكاتب زوجته ثم مات فإن ترك وفاء فهو حر في آخر حياته وفسد نكاحه، فإن لم يكن دخل بها فلا عدة لوقوع الفرقة قبل الدخول وهي ذلك. هذا وفي قول المؤلف ولا بعد أن يحكم بالثاني مشعر بأن الأول ليس كذلك وفيه نظر، لأن العدة إن لم تجب باعتبار الوطء في الدبر تجب باعتبار الخلوة اللهم إلا أن يكون وطئها بحضرة أجنبي ولا يخفى بعده قوله : (وأصل الكلام ليتربصن كان الظاهر الإتيان بأو بدل الواو فإن على تقدير اللام يكون أمراً مثل محمد تفد نفسك كل نفس تأمل قوله : (ثم قال اعلم أن النكاح الخ) قال في النهر : هذا التقسيم لم نر من عرج عليه والذي ذكره أهل الدار أن القسمة ثنائية وأن الفرقة بالتقبيل من الفسخ كما قدمناه ا هـ. وفي حاشية أبي السعود على مسكين قال السيد الحموي : وأيضاً مقتضى كونه رفعاً أن يكون منقصاً للعدد إذ الطلاق يرفع القيد وليس كذلك . أمة، فإن

Source pages p. 217

Commentary
This passage deals with several intricate issues regarding 'idda. It starts with a discussion on whether 'idda is required in cases where semen is inserted without actual penetration; the author mentions a view that if pregnancy appears, the 'idda is delivery, otherwise none. This is debated. Then the text explains the benefit of the Hanafi-Shafi'i dispute on quru': if the husband divorces his wife during her pure period, according to Shafi'i the 'idda ends immediately upon seeing the first drop of the third menstruation, whereas according to Hanafi it ends only after she completes that menstruation and becomes pure. Next, the purpose of the three menstruations is outlined: the first to establish non-pregnancy, the second to make marriage prohibited, the third for the merit of freedom. The passage then shifts to a classification of separations: faskh (annulment) occurs before the marriage is complete (e.g., option of puberty, option of emancipation), while raf' (lifting) occurs after completion (e.g., by purchase of one spouse by the other, or by a kiss of the husband's son). However, the commentator notes that scholars of the school consider the division as binary (either talaq or faskh), and that a kiss is faskh, not raf'. Several case studies are presented: a mukatab (slave) marrying his master's daughter, a husband purchasing his wife, etc. The passage concludes with a grammatical note on the verse and a critique of the division. The text is dense and appears to be a compilation of comments from various sources.

Key Terms
الفسخ (al-faskh): Annulment of marriage before completion (e.g., option of puberty, option of emancipation, lack of equivalence).
الرفع (al-raf'): Lifting of the marriage after completion, typically by purchase of one spouse by the other or by actions that make the wife forbidden (e.g., a kiss that establishes suckling relationship).
التمام (al-tamam): Completion of the marriage, which in this context means consummation (dukhul) or valid seclusion (khulwa).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the classification of separation when a husband buys his wife after consummation?
A) Talaq
B) Faskh
C) Raf'
D) It is not considered a valid separation.

Key Takeaway
Marriage after consummation cannot be annulled (faskh); separations not by talaq are classified as raf' (lifting), which does not reduce the count of talaqs.

'Idda in Cases of Purchase of Spouse and Mukatab
Applying 'idda rules in ownership-based dissolution

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rulings on 'idda when a husband purchases his wife after consummation and when a mukatab (slave under contract) marries his master's daughter.

Core Idea
Ownership of a spouse after consummation removes 'idda for the owner but imposes it against remarriage to others.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq presents specific cases where marriage is dissolved by ownership (purchase or mukatab's death). The rulings on 'idda differ depending on whether the separation is before or after consummation and whether the husband or wife is the owner. Understanding these nuances is vital for correctly determining when a woman may remarry.

Guided Path
1
Identify the case and the spouse who owns
Determine which spouse acquires ownership and whether the marriage was consummated. The passage covers: (1) husband purchases wife after consummation, (2) wife purchases husband after consummation (with prior talaq), (3) mukatab marries master's daughter and dies.
Each case has unique rules on 'idda and the validity of the separation.
2
Apply the rule for purchase by husband
If the husband buys his wife after consummation, the marriage is dissolved (raf'). The text says 'no 'idda upon him' but she must observe 'idda for others (two menstruations).
This means the wife cannot remarry anyone else until two menstrual periods have passed, but the husband can remarry her (as owner) without an 'idda? Actually, once he owns her, she becomes his slave and he can have intercourse, but the text says 'la 'idda 'alayhi laha' meaning she does not observe 'idda for him, but for others she does.
3
Apply the rule for purchase by wife
If the free wife buys her husband after consummation, and he had pronounced a talaq during her menstruation, the divorce still takes effect after she becomes pure. Her 'idda for the talaq continues (three menstruations), and it is not interrupted by the purchase. However, if the purchase occurs before the talaq is complete, the marriage ends by raf' and the earlier talaq is invalid.
The text gives a scenario: he divorced her during menstruation (talaq al-sunnah), then she buys him after she becomes pure. The divorce is effective and she continues her 'idda.
4
Apply the ruling for mukatab
A mukatab (slave under contract) who marries his master's daughter with permission: if he dies without paying the kitaba (his freedom not completed), the marriage is annulled (fasid? Actually the text says 'فسد نكاحه'). If there was no consummation, no 'idda. If he dies after paying the kitaba (freedom established in his last moment), the marriage is valid, and she observes the 'idda of death (four months and ten days) even if not consummated, and she inherits.
This hinges on whether the mukatab died mawla (having fulfilled the kitaba) or not.

Distinguishing Faskh and Raf': The 'Iddah Dispute
Analyzes the distinction between faskh and raf' and their effect on 'iddah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between faskh and raf' in Hanafi law and state the Hanafi position on when 'iddah ends after a divorce in a state of purity.

Core Idea
Faskh occurs before marriage completion, raf' after; 'iddah ends only after full purity from the third menstruation.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq clarifies two important distinctions in Hanafi family law: the difference between faskh (annulment) and raf' (dissolution), and a dispute over when a divorced woman's 'iddah ends. The Hanafi school holds that 'iddah requires full purity from the third menstruation, not merely the first drop. Understanding these categories is essential for correctly applying rulings on marriage dissolution.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories of marriage dissolution
Recognize that faskh occurs only before the marriage is complete, while raf' occurs after completion. Examples from the text: option of puberty (faskh), one spouse owning the other (raf').
Faskh and raf' are not divorces; they do not count toward the three divorces.
2
Locate the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
The dispute: when does 'iddah end if a husband divorces his wife in a state of purity? Opponent says at the first drop of the third menstruation; Hanafi says only after complete purity.
This difference affects the timing of remarriage.
3
Trace the reasoning (ta'lil)
The three menstruations serve distinct purposes: first to clear the womb, second to prohibit marriage, third for virtue of freedom. The Hanafi view ensures the womb is definitively empty.
The Hanafi emphasis on certainty (yaqin) drives the position.
4
Distinguish the types of separation
List examples from the text: faskh (option of puberty, emancipation, lack of kafa'a), raf' (one spouse owning the other, stepson kissing stepmother). Note the timing relative to completion of marriage.
Completion of marriage likely refers to the marriage being fully established, often after consummation.
5
Apply the distinction
Confirm that both faskh and raf' are not talaq and do not count towards the three divorces. The 'iddah rules for each may differ, but the passage indicates no difference in the need for 'iddah.
The passage from Idah al-Islah states no difference between talaq, faskh, and raf' in terms of 'iddah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفائدة الخلاف تظهر فيما إذا طلقها في الطهر فإنه تنقضي العدة برؤية قطرة من الدم من الحيضة الثالثة عنده وعندنا لا تنقضي العدة ما لم تطهر منها؛ كذا في غاية البيان . وفي المبسوط : الحيضة الأولى لتعرف براءة الرحم، والثانية لحرمة النكاح، والثالثة لفضيلة الحرية وشمل جميع أسبابه من الفسخ بخيار البلوغ والعتق وملك أحد الزوجين صاحبه وردة أحدهما، وقدمنا في نكاح الأولياء جملة الفرق والإيراد على قولهم أنه لا يحتمل الفسخ بعد التمام، ثم رأيت في إيضاح الاصلاح هنا أنه لا فرق بين الطلاق أو الفسخ أو الرفع ثم قال : اعلم أن النكاح بعد التمام لا يحتمل الفسخ فكل فرقة بغير طلاق قبل تمام النكاح كالفرقة بخيار البلوغ والفرقة بخيار العتق والفرق بعدم الكفاءة فسخ، وكل فرقة بغير طلاق بعد تمام النكاح كالفرقة بملك أحد الزوجين الآخر والفرقة بتقبيل ابن الزوج ونحوه رفع وهذا واضح عند من له خبرة في هذا الفن اهـ .
And the benefit of the dispute appears in the case where he divorces her in a state of purity: according to him (the opponent) the 'iddah ends with seeing a drop of blood from the third menstruation, while according to us the 'iddah does not end until she becomes pure from it; as in Ghayat al-Bayan. And in al-Mabsut: the first menstruation is to know the emptiness of the womb, the second for the prohibition of marriage, the third for the virtue of freedom. And it includes all its causes from faskh by the option of puberty, emancipation, one of the spouses possessing the other, and apostasy of one of them. And we have presented in the chapter on the marriage of guardians the summary of the difference and objection to their statement that faskh cannot occur after completion. Then I saw in Idah al-Islah here that there is no difference between talaq, faskh, or raf'. Then he said: Know that marriage after completion does not admit faskh. So every separation without talaq before the completion of marriage, like separation by the option of puberty, separation by the option of emancipation, and separation due to lack of compatibility (kafa'a), is faskh. And every separation without talaq after the completion of marriage, like separation by one of the spouses possessing the other and separation by the son of the husband kissing (his stepmother) and similar, is raf'. And this is clear to those who have experience in this art. End.
Source pages p. 218

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq discusses the distinction between faskh (annulment) and raf' (dissolution) in the context of 'iddah. The author first presents a practical consequence of a disagreement: if a husband divorces his wife in a state of purity, according to some the 'iddah ends with the first drop of blood of the third menstruation, but according to the Hanafi school it does not end until she completes the menstruation and becomes pure. He then cites al-Mabsut explaining the purposes of each of the three menstruations. The main focus is on categorizing types of separation: faskh occurs before the marriage is complete (e.g., due to an option or lack of equivalence), while raf' occurs after completion (e.g., through one spouse owning the other or certain prohibited acts). The author quotes from Idah al-Islah that there is no difference between talaq, faskh, and raf' in that they all end the marriage, but they differ in their effect on 'iddah and other rulings. The passage ends by asserting that this division is clear to those knowledgeable in the subject.

Key Terms
فسخ (faskh): Annulment of the marriage contract before its completion due to a defect or option.
رفع (raf'): Dissolution of the marriage after its completion due to grounds like one spouse possessing the other (milak al-yamin) or prohibited affinity.
عدة ('iddah): The waiting period a woman must observe after separation from her husband before remarrying.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, when does the 'iddah end for a woman divorced in a state of purity?
A) When she sees the first drop of the third menstruation
B) When she completes the third menstruation and becomes pure
C) After three lunar months from the divorce
D) After one complete menstrual cycle

Key Takeaway
Faskh and raf' are not divorces; they have different rules, and Hanafi 'iddah requires complete purity from the third menstruation.

The 'Iddah of a Slave Woman with Children (Umm Walad)
Ruling on 'iddah of a slave woman with children from her master

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling for the 'iddah of a slave woman who is an umm walad upon her master's death, including the application of tadakhul and ihdad.

Core Idea
The 'iddah of an umm walad is three menstruations due to overlap, with ihdad only for the first two.

This lesson addresses a specific case in Hanafi fiqh: a slave woman who has a child from her master (umm walad). When her master dies, two causes of separation coincide—divorce (or faskh) and death. The general principle of tadakhul (overlap) applies, meaning the longer 'iddah period suffices for both. However, the mourning obligation (ihdad) is not required for the entire period. Understanding this ruling helps clarify how the Hanafi school handles overlapping legal causes and their effects on women's waiting periods.

Guided Path
1
Identify the woman's status
She is an umm walad (a slave woman who has given birth to her master's child). This status determines the default 'iddah periods.
An umm walad is automatically freed upon her master's death, which triggers the 'iddah of separation.
2
Recognize the dual cause of separation
The separation is due to both the master's death (which frees her) and the dissolution of the marriage-like bond (furqa). The text mentions 'two due to separation and three due to death'.
The two causes occur simultaneously.
3
Recall default 'iddah periods for a slave woman
For a slave woman: divorce 'iddah = two menstruations; death 'iddah = three menstruations (or three months if she does not menstruate).
Free women have longer periods: 3 months for divorce, 4 months 10 days for death.
4
Apply tadakhul (overlap)
Since both causes operate at the same time, the longer period (three) suffices for both. The total 'iddah is three menstruations, not five.
Tadakhul is a Hanafi principle that prevents double counting when multiple causes coincide.
5
Determine ihdad requirement
Ihdad (mourning) is required for the death 'iddah but not for the divorce 'iddah. Due to overlap, she observes ihdad for the first two cycles (the portion attributable to death) and not the third.
The third cycle is considered the remaining part of the divorce 'iddah, which does not require ihdad.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كانت ولدت منه تعتد بثلاث حيض حيضتان بالفرقة وثلاث بالوفاة إلا أنها تتداخل وتحد في الأوليين دون الثالثة ; كذا في المحيط.
If she had given birth from him, she observes 'iddah with three menstruations: two menstruations due to the separation and three due to death, except that they overlap (tadakhul), and she observes mourning (ihdad) in the first two but not in the third; as in al-Muhit.
Source pages p. 219

Commentary
This ruling addresses a slave woman (umm walad) who was separated from her master (who is also the father of her child) due to his death. The default 'iddah for a slave woman upon divorce is two menstruations, while upon death of the husband it is three menstruations. Since the separation here is both by divorce (or faskh) and death, the periods would be two and three respectively, but due to overlap (tadakhul), she observes the longer period of three menstruations. However, the mourning period (ihdad) applies only for the first two menstruations, which correspond to the 'iddah of divorce. After the third, she is free to adorn herself. The ruling is from al-Muhit.

Key Terms
تتداخل (tadakhul): The overlapping of two 'iddah periods so that the longer one suffices for both.
إحداد (ihdad): The mourning period during which a widow refrains from adornment and perfume.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Three
C) Four
D) Five

Key Takeaway
The 'iddah of an umm walad is three cycles due to tadakhul, with ihdad required only for the first two.

Iddah of Free Women: Muslim and Kitabiyya Wives
Ruling on 'iddah for free women under Muslim and dhimmī husbands

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state which free women must observe 'iddah and identify the exceptional case of a dhimmī woman according to Hanafi fiqh.

Core Idea
A free Kitabiyya wife of a Muslim observes 'iddah; a dhimmī wife generally does not.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq clarifies the 'iddah obligations for free women. The author of Kanz al-Daqa'iq uses the general term 'free woman', and the commentator explains that it covers both Muslim and Kitabiyya wives when married to a Muslim. For dhimmī wives, a nuanced rule applies, with a disagreement among the early Hanafi masters.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general ruling for free women under a Muslim husband
Note that the term 'free woman' includes both Muslim and Kitabiyya. Their 'iddah is identical to that of a free Muslim woman.
This is based on the unqualified term 'hurrah' in the matn.
2
Contrast with the ruling for a woman under a dhimmī husband
A dhimmī woman married to a dhimmī is not required to observe 'iddah unless the dhimmī community recognizes 'iddah. This is the default.
The basis is that dhimmīs follow their own religious laws.
3
Examine the exception for pregnancy
If the dhimmī woman is pregnant, according to Imam Abu Hanifa, she must wait until delivery if her religion prescribes 'iddah. The two disciples (Sahibayn) disagree, saying no 'iddah is required even if pregnant.
The relied-upon view is Abu Hanifa's, conditioned on the religion having 'iddah.
4
Understand the practical difference
The dispute affects whether a pregnant dhimmī woman can remarry immediately after divorce. According to Abu Hanifa, she is barred from remarriage until delivery if her religion has 'iddah.
This prevents confusion of lineage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And he (the author) used the unqualified term 'free woman' (al-hurrah), which includes the Muslim woman and the Kitabiyya under a Muslim husband. As for when she is under a dhimmī, there is no 'iddah upon her if they (the dhimmīs) do not believe in it (the 'iddah), except if she is pregnant, according to Imam Abu Hanifa, contrary to the two disciples (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad). And this has passed. And it is mentioned in al-Bada'i' here and in al-Walwaljiyyah: he said: unless she is pregnant, then she is prevented from marrying if that (i.e., the 'iddah) is in their religion. End. Source pages p. 219

Commentary
This passage addresses the 'iddah of a free woman. The author of Kanz al-Daqa'iq used the general term 'free woman' in the ruling, which the commentator explains includes both Muslim women and Kitabiyya (Jewish or Christian) women married to Muslim husbands. The 'iddah rules are the same: she observes the same 'iddah as a Muslim free woman. However, if the woman is married to a dhimmī (non-Muslim subject of the Islamic state), she is not required to observe 'iddah unless the dhimmī community recognizes 'iddah as a legal concept. There is a disagreement between Abu Hanifa and the two disciples (Sahibayn) regarding the pregnant dhimmī woman: Abu Hanifa says she must observe 'iddah until delivery, while the two say she does not need to. The author notes that the correct view is that if pregnancy is evident, she is prevented from remarrying until delivery if her religion imposes 'iddah.

Key Terms
ذمي (dhimmī): A non-Muslim living under the protection of the Islamic state, who is allowed to follow his/her own religious laws in personal matters.
كتابية (kitabiyya): A Jewish or Christian woman.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Only if she is married to a Muslim
D) Never, since she is not Muslim

Key Takeaway
A free Kitabiyya wife of a Muslim observes full 'iddah; a dhimmī wife does not unless pregnant and her religion prescribes a waiting period.

'Iddah of a Non-Menstruating Woman: Three Months
Ruling and proof for 'iddah of non-menstruating free woman

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the 'iddah duration for a free woman who does not menstruate and apply the rules for a mustahada (chronic bleeder) who has forgotten her habit.

Core Idea
Three months 'iddah for non-menstruating women: established by Quran and applied even when menstruation is uncertain.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains the 'iddah for a free woman who does not menstruate, whether due to age or a medical condition. The primary proof is Quran 65:4. The commentator expands the ruling to cover subtle cases like the mustahada who has forgotten her habit, and clarifies the definition of a young girl (saghirah). By the end, you will understand not only the basic rule but also the practical details that muftis need to apply when handling these nuanced scenarios.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and Its Ruling
The act is the 'iddah of a free woman who does not menstruate (due to young age, old age, or medical condition). The ruling is that it is wajib (fard) to wait three months.
This is a clear Quranic command.
2
Locate the Dalil
The dalil is Quran 65:4, which explicitly states three months for those who have despaired of menstruation and those who have not menstruated.
The verse is qat'i al-thubut and qat'i al-dalalah.
3
Analyze the Case of the Mustahada
A mustahada (woman with chronic irregular bleeding) who has forgotten her habit cannot determine her actual menstruation. She must wait three months to ensure she has completed three menstrual cycles with certainty.
If she remembers her habit, she calculates based on her previous cycle, which may be shorter than three calendar months.
4
Examine the Definition of Saghirah
The passage discusses the proper definition: a young girl who has not seen blood and has not reached the age of puberty (9 years). If she reaches 9 and still does not menstruate, she is not considered a saghirah for 'iddah purposes.
This affects whether her 'iddah is by months or by menstruations.

His statement: 'and three months if she does not menstruate' means the 'iddah of a free woman if she is not among those who menstruate due to young age or old age is a period of three months, due to the saying of Allah Most High: 'And those who have despaired of menstruation among your women, if you doubt, their 'iddah is three months' [65:4] regarding the post-menopausal woman, and His saying: 'And those who have not menstruated' [65:4] regarding the young girl and one who has reached majority but has not menstruated. And his statement 'if she does not menstruate' includes also the adult woman who does not see blood or sees it and it stops before completion, and one who reaches puberty as a mustahada (woman with chronic bleeding), and the mustahada who has forgotten her habit, which is a riddle: people say: a young woman who sees what qualifies as menstruation every month, and her 'iddah is by months. But in reality, when she has forgotten her habit, it is possible that it could be at the beginning of every month or the end, so when she waits for three months, it is known that she has had three menstruations with certainty. In contrast to the one who has not forgotten, she is referred to the days of her habit, so it is possible that her 'iddah could be at the beginning of the month, so she leaves 'iddah after five or six days of the third (month). And in Fath al-Qadir, taking from al-Zayla'i in al-Hayd: His statement: 'So the report of a single just person is accepted regarding his death' i.e., as will come at his statement 'and for death, four months and ten' clarifying. Commentary
This passage provides the ruling on the 'iddah of a free woman who does not menstruate, whether due to young age (saghirah), old age (a'isa), or a medical condition (mustahada). The primary evidence is from the Quran 65:4. The commentator explains that the term 'if she does not menstruate' covers several categories: the adult woman who never menstruates, the one whose menstruation stops prematurely, the mustahada (woman with irregular bleeding), and especially the mustahada who has forgotten her habit. For the latter, since it is unclear when her menstruation occurs, she waits three months because that is the maximum time in which three menstruations must have occurred. For a mustahada who remembers her habit, she calculates based on her previous cycle, so her 'iddah may be shorter than three calendar months. The passage also includes a critical note about the definition of 'saghirah' (young girl): the author of the matn defined her as one who has not reached the age of menstruation, but the commentator suggests a more precise definition: one who has neither seen blood nor reached the age of puberty (nine years). According to Imam al-Fadli, if a girl reaches nine, she may have reached the age of menstruation, so she should wait for three months only if she does not menstruate.

Key Terms
مستحاضة (mustahada): A woman who experiences chronic uterine bleeding outside the normal menstrual period, making it difficult to determine her actual menstruation.
آيسة (a'isa): A woman who has reached menopause and no longer menstruates.
صغيرة (saghirah): A young girl who has not reached the age of puberty and does not menstruate.

'Iddah for a Woman Who Gave Birth: Tadakhul, Free/Slave, and Kitabiyya/Dhimmi Rulings
Expansion on 'iddah for a woman who gave birth, with tadakhul and status clarifications.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the 'iddah for a woman who gave birth, explain the principle of tadakhul, and apply the ruling for free, slave, Muslim, and Kitabiyya women under a Muslim or dhimmi husband.

Core Idea
A woman who gave birth observes 'iddah with three haids, with tadakhul; her status as free/slave or Muslim/Kitabiyya under a Muslim does not change the ruling, but a dhimmi husband triggers ikhtilaf.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq expands on Kanz al-Daqa'iq, addressing a specific case: a woman who has borne a child from her husband. Her 'iddah for divorce is three haids, and for death also three, but they overlap (tadakhul) so only one three-periode period is needed. The commentator then clarifies that the general term 'free woman' includes Muslim and Kitabiyya under a Muslim husband—they have identical 'iddah. However, if the husband is a dhimmi, the default is no 'iddah unless the wife is pregnant, according to Imam Abu Hanifa, while Abu Yusuf and Muhammad hold otherwise. This lesson unpacks the ruling and the ikhtilaf.

Guided Path
1
Identify the woman's situation
The ruling begins with a woman who has given birth from her husband. Determine whether this affects the 'iddah.
She is not divorced or widowed yet; the 'iddah trigger is separate.
2
Determine the 'iddah length and overlap
For divorce, 'iddah is three haids; for death, also three. But they overlap (tadakhul), so she observes only one three-periode period total.
Tadakhul is a Hanafi principle: when two causes coincide, the shorter or overlapping period suffices.
3
Note the mourning (ihdad)
She performs ihdad for the first two periods but not the third.
Ihdad is specific to death; here it is partially applicable due to tadakhul.
4
Apply the general term 'free woman'
The text says 'free woman' absolutely, so it includes Muslim and Kitabiyya under a Muslim husband. Their 'iddah is identical.
This removes the misunderstanding that Kitabiyya have a different 'iddah.
5
Distinguish the dhimmi husband case
If the husband is a dhimmi, there is generally no 'iddah unless the wife is pregnant, according to Abu Hanifa. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad disagree.
The ikhtilaf is intra-Hanafi and rests on whether the dhimmis' religion imposes 'iddah.
6
Understand the ikhtilaf
Abu Hanifa conditions 'iddah on the dhimmi religion having 'iddah for pregnancy; the Sahibayn reject any 'iddah.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf within the school.

'Iddah of a Free Woman Who Does Not Menstruate: Three Months
Explains the three-month 'iddah for free women without menstruation with Quranic proof and detailed cases.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the basis for the three-month 'iddah and identify which categories of women are included, applying the ruling to cases of menopause, youth, istihadah, and forgotten habit.

Core Idea
Quran 65:4 commands three months 'iddah for women who do not menstruate; this applies to menopausal, young, and those with chronic bleeding whose habit is forgotten.

The Hanafi school, relying on the clear text of Quran 65:4, establishes that a free woman who does not menstruate—whether due to age, youth, or a medical condition—must observe an 'iddah of three months. But what exactly does 'does not menstruate' mean? In this lesson, we will unpack the categories included, the case of a woman with chronic bleeding who forgets her habit, and the definition of a young girl, along with the consensus on counting by days.

Guided Path
1
Identify the base ruling and its Quranic proof
The passage opens with the matn statement that the 'iddah for a free woman who does not menstruate is three months. It immediately cites Quran 65:4, which explicitly mentions two categories: those who have despaired of menstruation (al-ya'isa) and those who have not menstruated (al-saghira). This verse establishes the obligation (wujub) of a three-month 'iddah.
The verse is both qati' al-thubut and qati' al-dalalah, so the ruling is firm.
2
Trace the inclusion of additional categories
The commentator explains that the phrase 'if she does not menstruate' is broader than the two explicit categories. It also includes an adult woman who never saw blood, one whose blood stopped before completion, and a mustahadah (woman with chronic non-menstrual bleeding). The key is that any free woman who does not have a regular menstrual cycle falls under this ruling.
The expansion is based on the 'illah of not menstruating, which is common to all these cases.
3
Analyze the case of the mustahadah nasiyah
A woman with chronic bleeding who has forgotten her habit (mustahadah nasiyah) is a special case. Because her cycle is unpredictable, she cannot be sure when she menstruates. Therefore, she observes three months to guarantee that she has completed three menstruation periods. If she remembers her habit, she reverts to her usual days.
This is based on the principle of certainty: she must wait until she is certain of three purities.
4
Examine the definition of 'young girl' (saghira) and the suspension for puberty
The passage defines the young girl as one who has not reached the age of menstruation, with the preferred view being under nine years. If a girl is at the age of puberty (nine or above), according to Imam al-Fadli, her 'iddah is not by months; rather, her situation is suspended until it becomes clear whether she is pregnant from intercourse. If pregnant, 'iddah is by delivery; if not, then months are used.
This ensures that a possible pregnancy does not go unrecognized, protecting lineage.
5
Note the counting method and intra-Hanafi consensus
The passage affirms that months in 'iddah are counted by days, not lunar months, by consensus. The only difference between Abu Hanifa and his two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) is in the context of ijarah (hire contracts), not 'iddah. This clarifies that the three-month period is 90 days, not three lunar cycles.
This consensus simplifies calculation and ensures uniformity.

His statement: (and three months if she does not menstruate) meaning the 'iddah of a free woman, if she is not among those who menstruate due to youth or old age, is a period of three months, due to the Almighty's saying: 'And those who have despaired of menstruation from among your women, if you doubt, their 'iddah is three months' [65:4] regarding the menopausal woman, and His saying: 'And those who have not menstruated' [65:4] regarding the young girl and one who has reached age but not menstruated. And his statement 'if she does not menstruate' also includes the adult woman if she did not see blood, or saw it and it stopped before completion, and the one who reached adulthood with istihadah, and the mustahadah who forgot her habit, which is a riddle: they say a young woman sees what qualifies as menstruation every month but her 'iddah is by months; but in reality, since she forgot her habit, it is possible that it occurs at the beginning of each month or the end, so if she waits three months, she knows with certainty that she has had three menstruations, unlike if she had not forgotten—then she refers to the days of her habit, so it is possible her 'iddah begins at the start of the month, and she exits 'iddah after five or six of the third. And in Fath al-Qadir, taken from al-Zayla'i on menstruation: (but in reality, the summary is that her 'iddah in actual fact is not by months but by menstruation, but since she cannot be certain of three menstruations except in three months, it is said she should wait that period. And the meaning of 'young girl' is one who has not reached the age of menstruation, and the preferred corrected view is nine years. And from Imam al-Fadli: if she is at the age of puberty, her 'iddah does not expire by months; rather her situation is suspended until it becomes clear whether she has become pregnant from intercourse or not. If her pregnancy appears, she completes 'iddah by delivery; if not, then by months. And in al-Mujtaba: he made it subject to the difference like ijarah and debt; but the 'iddah of a man with impotence is counted by days by consensus. And in al-TatarKhaniyya: A woman reached adulthood and saw one day of blood, then the blood stopped from her until a year passed, then her husband divorced her: her 'iddah is by months. And he excluded by his statement 'if she does not menstruate' the young woman with extended purity, so she does not observe 'iddah by months; its example: if she saw three days and it stopped and a year or more passed, then she was divorced, her 'iddah is by menstruation until she reaches the limit of menopause, which is fifty-five years, according to the selected view; thus in al-Bazzaziyya. And among the strange things is what is in al-Bazzaziyya: al-'Allama said: the fatwa in our time follows Malik's saying on the 'iddah of the menopausal woman. And if a judge ruled that the 'iddah of the woman with extended purity expires after the passage of nine...
Source pages p. 219

Commentary
This passage is a detailed commentary on the matn phrase 'and three months if she does not menstruate'. It establishes that the 'iddah for a free woman who does not menstruate—whether due to youth, old age, or other reasons—is three months, citing Quran 65:4 for both the menopausal woman (al-ya'isa) and the young girl who has not yet menstruated. The commentator then expands on the meaning of 'if she does not menstruate' to include several categories: an adult woman who never saw blood, one whose blood stopped before completing a full menstruation, a mustahadah (woman with chronic non-menstrual bleeding), and specifically a mustahadah who has forgotten her habit. For the latter, there is a riddle: although she sees blood monthly, her 'iddah is calculated by months because with forgotten habit, her menstruation could occur at any time; by waiting three months, she is certain of three menstruations. If she remembers her habit, she refers to her usual days. The commentary then addresses the definition of 'young girl': the preferred view is that she is under nine years old; if she is at the age of puberty (nine or above), according to Imam al-Fadli, her 'iddah is not by months but is suspended until pregnancy is ruled out. The counting of months is by days (not lunar months) by consensus, though there is a difference between Abu Hanifa and his two companions regarding ijarah, not 'iddah. The passage also covers specific cases: a woman who saw blood once then stopped for a year before divorce counts by months; a young woman with extended purity (who sees three days of bleeding then stops for a year or more) counts by menstruation until menopause at 55. Finally, it notes a strange fatwa from al-Bazzaziyya following Malik's view for the menopausal woman's 'iddah, and discusses the minimum period for pregnancy claims.

Key Terms
آيسة (ya'isa): A woman who has reached the age of menopause and no longer menstruates.
مستحاضة ناسية (mustahadah nasiyah): A woman with chronic bleeding (istihadah) who has forgotten her usual menstrual pattern.
صغيرة (saghira): A young girl who has not reached the age of menstruation, preferred age under nine.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The Quranic command of three months 'iddah for non-menstruating women is clear, but its application varies by the woman's situation: menopause, youth, or chronic bleeding with forgotten habit, and must be counted by days by consensus.

The Iddah of a Prepubescent Girl
Clarifies the ruling for the waiting period of a prepubescent girl and whether she is addressed by the command

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school avoids saying iddah is 'wajib' upon a young girl, and identify the legal responsibility of the wali in ensuring the iddah is observed.

Core Idea
A prepubescent girl's iddah is established by khitab wad'i; the wali is addressed, not the girl.

The waiting period (iddah) for a young girl who has not yet menstruated is prescribed by the Quran. But a key Hanafi nuance is that she is not personally obligated—rather, the wali (guardian) is addressed to ensure she does not remarry during this time. This distinction touches on the difference between legally binding address (khitab taklifi) and declaratory rulings (khitab wad'i), a foundational usul principle.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is the iddah of a prepubescent girl. The Hanafi ruling is that she must observe a waiting period of three months, but the obligation is not personally upon her; it is upon the wali.
The term 'wajib' is avoided for the girl because she is not a mukallafa (legally responsible).
2
Locate the dalil
The primary proof is Surah al-Talaq (65:4), which prescribes three months for those who do not menstruate, and the hadith of Mu'adh explaining the revelation.
The verse is qat'i al-thubut and qat'i al-dalalah.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The verse explicitly states 'three months' for women who have not menstruated, which includes young girls. The hadith clarifies that the verse was revealed in response to a question about the iddah of those who do not menstruate.
The ruling is directly from the text, not derived by analogy.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Some early scholars debated whether the young girl is personally addressed with the command. The majority of Hanafi mashayikh avoid saying 'wajib' for her.
The dispute is about the nature of the address (khitab), not the duration of iddah.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih
The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that the girl observes iddah without being personally obligated; the wali is addressed to prevent her marriage during this period.
This view is supported by Fath al-Qadir and al-Mabsut.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قلت : لكنه مخالف لجميع الروايات لا یفتی به نعم لو قضى مالكي به نفذ. وفي فتح القدير : ثم أكثر المشايخ لا يطلقون لفظ الوجوب على هذه الصغيرة لأنها غير مخاطبة بل يقولون تعتد. وفي المبسوط قال بعض علمائنا : هي لا تخاطب بالاعتداد لكن الولي يخاطب بأن لا يزوجها حتى تنقضي مدة العدة مع أن العدة مجرد مضي المدة فثبوتها في حقها لا يؤدي إلى توجيه خطاب الشرع عليها، ولا يخفى أن القائل الأول قوله مبني على أنه يراها الحرمات أو التربص الواجب، فإن قلت على تقدير كونها مضي المدة أليس أن فيها يجب أن لا تتزوج أن يتعلق خطاب نهي التزوج بالولي فجعلها المدة كما قال شمس الأئمة لا يستلزم انتفاء قول الأول ويخاطب الولي بأن لا يزوجها، فالجواب لا يلزم فإنا إذا قلنا إنها المدة فالثابت فيها عدم صحة التزوج لا خطاب أحد بل وضع الشارع عدم الصحة لو فعل ا هـ . والحاصل أن الصغيرة أهل الخطاب الوضع وهذا منه كما خوطب الصغيرة والصغيرة بضمان المتلفات، ولو حاضت الصغيرة في الأشهر الثلاثة تستأنف العدة بالحيض، ولو حاضت الكبيرة حيضة ثم أيست استأنفت بالشهور تحرزاً عن الجمع بين الأصل والخلف، وقد فسر القاضي قوله تعالى إن ارتبتم شككتم وجهلتم ا هـ . وإذا كان هذا مع الارتياب ففي غيره بالأولى؛ كذا في غاية البيان وفي الفخر الرازي: إن ارتبتم في دم البالغات مبلغ الإياس أهو دم حيض أو استحاضة وروي أن معاذ بن جبل رضي الله عنه قال: يا رسول الله قد عرفنا عدة التي تحيض فما عدة التي لم تحض ؟ فنزلت واللائي يئسن) [الطلاق: ٤] فقام رجل فقال : ما عدة الصغيرة فنزل واللائي لم يحضن) [الطلاق: ٤] أى بمنزلة الكبيرة أي هي ولم ترد ما يحكم بإياسها حتى تمضي عدتها بعد ثلاثة أشهر . وروي عن ابن عمر رضي الله عنه مثله . فعلى هذا في ممتدة الطهر قبل بلوغها إلى الإياس فاعتدت بثلاثة أشهر بعد مضي نصف سنة وقضى القاضي جاز لأنه مجتهد فيه ويحفظ هذا لكثرة وقوعه ا.هـ. وبه ظهر أن قوله سابقاً «مدة الآيسة» المراد بها ممتدة الطهر لا من بلغت سن الإياس وإلا فهي تعتد بالأشهر بالنص
I said: But it contradicts all narrations; it should not be given as fatwa. Yes, if a Maliki judge ruled by it, it is carried out. In Fath al-Qadir: Then most of the mashayikh do not use the word wujub for this young girl because she is not addressed; rather they say 'she observes iddah'. In al-Mabsut, some of our scholars said: She is not addressed by the command of iddah, but the wali is addressed not to marry her until the period of iddah expires, even though iddah is merely the passage of time. So its establishment for her does not lead to directing the legal address (khitab) to her. And it is not hidden that the first speaker's statement is based on his view that she is subject to the prohibitions or the obligatory waiting. If you say: On the assumption that it is the passage of time, does it not require that she must not marry, and that the prohibition of marriage is attached to the wali? So making it the passage of time, as Shams al-A'imma said, does not negate the first statement, and the wali is addressed not to marry her. The answer: It does not necessitate; if we say it is the passage of time, then what is established is the invalidity of marriage, not the address to anyone; rather the Lawgiver has decreed invalidity if she does marry. End. The summary: The young girl is eligible for the legal ruling (khitab al-wad'i), and this is part of it, just as a young boy and girl are addressed regarding liability for damages. If a young girl menstruates during the three months, she begins the iddah anew by menstruation. If an older woman menstruates once then becomes menopausal, she begins anew by months, to avoid combining the original and the substitute... And if this is the case in doubt, then in other cases it is more so... It is narrated that Mu'adh ibn Jabal said: O Messenger of Allah, we know the iddah of the menstruating woman, so what is the iddah of the one who does not menstruate? Then was revealed: And those who have despaired of menstruation... (65:4). Then a man stood and asked: What is the iddah of the young girl? Then was revealed: And those who have not menstruated... (65:4), meaning she is like the older woman... And it is reported from Ibn Umar similar. Based on this, regarding the one with extended purity before reaching menopause, if she observed iddah by three months after half a year, and a judge ruled it valid, it is permissible because it is a matter of ijtihad... And it became clear that his earlier statement 'the period of the menopausal woman' means the one with extended purity, not the one who has reached the age of menopause; otherwise she observes iddah by months according to the text.
Source pages p. 221

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof. The main issue is whether a prepubescent girl (saghirah) is personally obligated to observe iddah. Most Hanafi mashayikh avoid the term wajib for her because she is not legally responsible (mukallafa). Instead, they say 'she observes iddah' (ta'tadd). The wali (guardian) is addressed with the prohibition of marrying her until the iddah period expires. The iddah here is merely the passage of time (mudiyy al-muddah), not an active duty. The author explains that the young girl is subject to khitab al-wad'i (declaratory address), like liability for damages. The text then provides the proof from Quran (Surah al-Talaq, 65:4) for both the menopausal woman and the young girl, via the hadith of Mu'adh. The passage also discusses the case of extended purity: if such a woman misses menses before menopause, her iddah is three months after half a year? The text notes that this is a area of ijtihad and even if a judge rules based on it, it is valid.

Key Terms
الخطاب الوضعي (al-khitab al-wad'i): The declaratory address of the Lawgiver that establishes a legal status (e.g., invalidity, condition) without requiring the subject's awareness or capacity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Because she is not a legal subject (mukallafa)
C) Because her iddah is not based on Quran
D) Because the wali performs the iddah for her

Key Takeaway
The iddah of a prepubescent girl is three months, but the obligation to ensure it is upon her wali, not upon her personally.

The Widow's Iddah: Days vs. Nights – Hanafi vs. al-Awza'i
Inter-madhhab dispute on widow's iddah duration

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school holds that a widow's iddah is four months and ten days and evaluate the argument of al-Awza'i for ten nights.

Core Idea
The widow's iddah is four months and ten days — the disputed 'ten' are days, not nights, determined by Arabic usage.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq addresses the Quranic verse on a widow's iddah (2:234). The Hanafi school interprets 'ashran' as ten days, while Imam al-Awza'i argued for ten nights. The dispute hinges on Arabic grammar and the practical calculation of the waiting period. In this lesson, we will trace the arguments and see how Hanafi scholars refuted al-Awza'i's view, and also explore an intra-Hanafi variant from al-Fadli.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position and its proof
Identify the ruling: four months and ten days. Cite the Quranic verse (2:234) and the hadith quoted in the passage. Note that the specifier of 'ashran' is understood as 'ayam' (days).
The verse uses the masculine 'ashran', which normally requires a masculine specifier. Hanafis argue that 'ayam' can be omitted because it is common.
2
Identify al-Awza'i's opposing view and his grammatical argument
Al-Awza'i held that 'ashran' refers to ten nights because nights (layal) are masculine, while days (ayyam) are feminine. He thus concluded the iddah is four months and ten nights.
This view would mean the iddah ends after the tenth night, allowing marriage on the tenth day.
3
Analyze the Hanafi response
The Hanafi rebuttal: In Arabic usage, when nights are mentioned, the corresponding days are automatically included. Moreover, the masculine form can be used for days when the noun is omitted. Thus, no contradiction.
The passage cites a historical analogy: dating by nights implies the days together.
4
Examine the practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf)
If al-Awza'i's view is followed, the iddah ends after the tenth night – meaning on the morning of the tenth day. The Hanafi view requires the full tenth day to pass. The difference amounts to a day shift depending on the time of death.
The text gives an example: death near dawn vs. near sunset affects whether the counting adds or subtracts a day.
5
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf and the mufta bihi
Some Hanafis like al-Fadli followed al-Awza'i for precaution (ihtiyat). However, the standard (mufta bihi) remains the Zahir al-Riwayah view of four months and ten days.
The passage shows that al-Fadli's view sometimes increases the iddah and sometimes decreases, so it is not always precautionary.

قوله : (وللموت أربعة أشهر وعشر) أي عدة المتوفى عنها زوجها بعد نكاح صحيح إذا كانت حرة أربعة أشهر وعشرة أيام لقوله تعالى والذين يتوفون منكم ويذرون أزواجا يتربصن بأنفسهن أربعة أشهر وعشرا [البقرة : ٢٣٤] أي عشرة أيام بناء على أنه إذا ذكر الأيام أو الليالي فإنه يدخل ما بإزائه من الآخر، وبه اندفع قول الأوزاعي إن العدة أربعة أشهر وعشر ليال أخذاً من تذكير العدد أعني العشر في الكتاب كما سمعت، وفي السنة في حديث لا حداد إلا على زوجها أربعة أشهر وعشراً». والحاصل أن الأوزاعي يقول بتسعة أيام وعشر ليال حتى لو تزوجت في اليوم العاشر جاز ; هكذا فرعه في معراج الدراية على قول الأوزاعي وتبعه في فتح القدير لكن في فتاوى قاضيخان حكي عن الفضلي كقول الأوزاعي فقال : وحكي عن الشيخ الإمام أبي بكر محمد بن الفضل أنه قال : تعتد أربعة أشهر وعشر ليال لأن الله تعالى ذكر العشر مذكراً وجمع الليالي بذكر لفظ التذكير وجمع الأيام بلفظ التأنيث فعلى قوله تزيد العدة بليلة واحدة وهذا أقرب إلى الاحتياط ا.هـ. فظاهره أن الليالي إنما زاد لا أنه نقص فإذا تزوجت في اليوم العاشر لم يجز اتفاقاً، وإنما يظهر الاختلاف من اعتبر قوله: (أي عشرة أيام) يعني أن تمييز عشراً هو الأيام لا الليالي لكن بناء ذلك على ما ذكره غير ظاهر لأنه يفيد أن المقدر في الآية الليالي لا الأيام ولهذا قال في الفتح في الجواب عن كلام الأوزاعي قلنا: الاستعمال في مثله أن بذكر عدة الليالي يدخل ما بإزائها من الأيام على ما عرف في التاريخ حيث يكتب بالليالي فيقال لسبع خلون مثلاً ويراد كون عدة الأيام كذلك ا هـ . فهذا كما ترى مبني على تسليم كون المقدر الليالي لا الأيام وما في النهر من قوله وتأنيث العشرة باعتبار الليالي لعل صوابه وتذكير العشر تأمل. ثم هذا إنما يحتاج إليه بناء على ما هو القياس وإلا فلا يجب عدم المطابقة حيث كان المعدود محذوفاً كما سيأتي قوله فظاهره أن من اعتبر الليالي الخ) أي ظاهر قول الخانية . فعلى قوله تزيد العدة بليلة وجعله إياه الاحتياط لكن لا يخفى أن هذا إنما يظهر فيما صوره المؤلف بما إذا مات قبيل طلوع الفجر، أما لو فرضنا موته بعيد الغروب وتربصت الأهلة الأربعة فإن عدتها تنقضي بمضي الليلة العاشرة من الشهر الخامس بناء على اعتبار الليالي أما على اعتبار الأيام فلا بد من مضي اليوم العاشر فالتحقيق أن القول باعتبار الليالي تارة تزيد فيه العدة بليلة وتارة تنقص بيوم وكان مراد الخانية بأنه أقرب إلى الاحتياط في صورة الزيادة فقط وأن الاحتياط في المشهور في
His statement: 'And for death, four months and ten' meaning the iddah of a woman whose husband has died after a valid marriage, if she is free, is four months and ten days, due to His statement: 'And those who are taken in death among you and leave wives behind – they shall wait concerning themselves four months and ten days' (2:234) – meaning ten days, based on the fact that if days or nights are mentioned, the corresponding part of the other is included. And with this, the statement of al-Awza'i is refuted, that the iddah is four months and ten nights, taking from the masculinity of the number, i.e., 'al-ashr' (ten) in the Book, as you heard, and in the Sunnah, in the hadith: 'No mourning except for a husband: four months and ten.' The summary is that al-Awza'i says nine days and ten nights, such that if she marries on the tenth day, it is permissible. This is the branch derived from al-Awza'i's statement in Mi'raj al-Dirayah, and it is followed in Fath al-Qadir. But in Fatawa Qadikhan, it is reported from al-Fadli like the statement of al-Awza'i. He said: It is reported from al-Shaykh al-Imam Abu Bakr Muhammad ibn al-Fadl that he said: She observes iddah four months and ten nights, because Allah mentioned 'al-ashr' in the masculine form and combined the nights by mentioning the masculine word, and combined the days by the feminine word. According to his statement, the iddah increases by one night, and this is closer to precaution. End. So apparent is that the nights are increased, not decreased. If she marries on the tenth day, it is not permissible by agreement. The difference only appears based on what is assumed. His statement: 'meaning ten days' – i.e., the specifier of 'ashran' is days, not nights. But the basis of this is not clear, because it implies that the implied (muqaddar) in the verse is nights, not days. Hence it was said in al-Fath in response to al-Awza'i's statement: We say: The usage in such cases is that by mentioning the number of nights, the corresponding days are included, as is known in dating where they write by nights and say 'for seven that have passed' and intend the count of days likewise. End. This, as you see, is based on conceding that the implied is nights, not days. And what is in al-Nahr: 'the feminization of al-ashara is due to the nights' – perhaps its correct form is 'the masculinization of al-ashr' – ponder. Then this is only needed based on what is the analogical principle; otherwise, conformity is not required when the counted noun is omitted... So the apparent meaning is that the one who considered nights (i.e., the apparent statement of al-Khaniyya) – according to his statement, the iddah increases by one night, and he made it precaution. But it is not hidden that this appears only in the case depicted by the author: when he dies just before dawn. But if we assume his death near sunset and she waits four moons, her iddah ends with the passing of the tenth night of the fifth month, based on considering nights. Whereas based on considering days, the tenth day must pass. The investigation is that the statement considering nights sometimes increases the iddah by a night and sometimes decreases by a day. And al-Khaniyya's intent was that it is closer to precaution only in the case of increase, and that precaution in the well-known [statement] is...
Source pages p. 222

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute. The author states the Hanafi ruling: a free widow's iddah is four months and ten days. The proof is Quran 2:234. The word 'ashran' is masculine, and its specifier is understood as ayam (days). Al-Awza'i argued that it is ten nights, leading to 9 days and 10 nights. The Hanafis respond that in Arabic usage, when nights are mentioned, the days are included, so the period actually covers 10 days and 10 nights? Actually the Hanafi position is that the specifier is days, so the period is 4 months + 10 days. The text delves into the grammatical debate. Some scholars like Abu Bakr al-Fadli followed al-Awza'i for precaution (ihtiyat), making the iddah 4 months and 10 nights (i.e., one extra night). But the author shows that this adds a night in some cases but subtracts a day in others, so it is not always precautionary. The Fatwa of Qadikhan reports al-Fadli's view. The discussion also mentions the timing of death (before dawn vs. near sunset) affecting the calculation. Ultimately, the Hanafi mufta bihi is the standard view of 4 months and 10 days.

Key Terms
عشر ('ashr): Ten (masculine form); in the verse, it is the amount added to four months for the widow's iddah.
عدة (iddah): The waiting period a woman must observe after the death of her husband (or after divorce) before she can remarry.
أيام (ayyam): Days (feminine plural). In the dispute, this is the specifier that Hanafis assume for 'ashran'.
ليال (layal): Nights (masculine plural). This is the specifier al-Awza'i assumes for 'ashran'.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, how long is the waiting period for a free widow whose husband dies?
A) Four months and ten nights
B) Four months and ten days
C) Four months and nine days
D) Four months exactly

Key Takeaway
The widow's iddah is four months and ten full days; the dispute with al-Awza'i over nights vs. days does not change the Hanafi fatwa.
