The End of a Widow's Idda: Ten Days or Ten Nights?
Dispute about the precise end of a widow's idda

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on when the idda of a widow ends if the husband dies before fajr on the last day of a lunar month, and explain the linguistic proof from the hadith 'six from Shawwal'.

Core Idea
A widow's idda requires ten nights, not just ten days, because Arabic drops the feminine marker when the counted noun is omitted.

This passage addresses a subtle but important point: does the idda of a widow whose husband dies before dawn end at sunset on the tenth day of the fifth month, or must the tenth night also pass? The Hanafi school, relying on linguistic principles and a Prophetic hadith, holds that 'ten' in such contexts includes ten nights. This lesson unpacks the proof and the ikhtilaf.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core ruling
Determine what is being ruled: when does the idda of a widow (whose husband dies before fajr) expire?
The idda for a widow is four lunar months and ten days according to the Quran (2:234).
2
Understand the dispute
Two views: al-Fadli and al-Awza‘i require the tenth night to pass; the general view says sunset on the tenth day suffices.
The author notes the first is more precautionary.
3
Examine the Hanafi proof
The Hanafi view is cited from al-Mujtaba: 'the ten is ten days and ten nights.' The linguistic proof is that when the counted noun (e.g., days) is omitted, the feminine 'ta' is dropped from the number, so 'ten' can refer to nights. The hadith 'followed it with six from Shawwal' is evidence because 'sittan' (six) drops the 'ta' even though the counted noun 'days' is masculine.
Also, Ibn ‘Umar said 'ten nights and nine days,' implying the tenth night is part of the count.
4
Apply the ruling
Thus, if a husband dies before fajr on the last day of the fourth lunar month, the idda ends after the tenth night of the fifth month has passed, not at sunset.
This is the mufta bihi position for caution.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فيما إذا مات قبل طلوع الفجر وتربصت الأهلة الأربعة فإن عدتها لا تنقضي بمضي اليوم العاشر من الخامس بل لا بد من مضي الليلة التي بعد العاشر على قول الفضلي والأوزاعي، وعلى قول العامة تنقضي بغروب الشمس، ولا يخفى أن الأول أحوط. وفي المجتبى أن العشر عشرة أيام وعشر ليال من الشهر الخامس عندنا . وقال ابن عمر : عشر ليال وتسعة أيام وأكثر أهل العربية أن العدد إنما يكون عكس المعدود تذكيراً وتأنيثاً حيث كان المعدود مذكوراً، وأما إذا كان محذوفاً فإنه يجوز ترك التاء في العدد الذي معدوده مذكر كقوله عليه السلام من صام رمضان وأتبعه ستا من شوال كذا في بعض شروح الألفية وذكره الكرماني في شرح حديث بني الإسلام على خمس والنكتة في عدم الاتيان بالتاء ما ذكره الرازي هذه أيام الحزن والمكروه ومثل هذه الأيام تسمى بالليالي استعارة كقولهم خرجنا ليالي الفتنة وتمامه فيه .
Concerning when he dies before dawn and she waits for four crescent moons, her idda does not expire with the passing of the tenth day of the fifth month; rather, the night after the tenth must also pass, according to the view of al-Fadli and al-Awza‘i. According to the general view, it expires at sunset, and it is not hidden that the first is more precautionary. In al-Mujtaba: 'The ten is ten days and ten nights from the fifth month according to us.' Ibn 'Umar said: 'Ten nights and nine days.' Most of the Arabic scholars hold that the number is the opposite of the counted noun in regard to masculine and feminine when the counted noun is mentioned, but when it is omitted, it is permissible to drop the ta' from the number whose counted noun is masculine, like his statement, peace be upon him: 'Whoever fasts Ramadan and follows it with six from Shawwal.' So it appears in some commentaries on al-Alfiyyah, and al-Kirmani mentioned it in his commentary on the hadith 'Islam is built on five.' The nuance in not bringing the ta' is what al-Razi mentioned: 'These are days of grief and detestation, and such days are called by the nights metaphorically, like their saying: We went out during the nights of tribulation.' And its completion is there.
Source pages p. 223

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling with proof. It addresses the precise moment when the 'idda of a woman whose husband dies before dawn on the last day of a lunar month ends. Two views are given: al-Fadli and al-Awza'i hold that the 'idda does not end simply with the passing of the tenth day of the fifth month; rather, the following night must also elapse. The general view (al-'ammah) holds that it ends at sunset of that day. The author notes that the first view is more precautionary (ahwat). He then cites al-Mujtaba to confirm that 'the ten' means ten days and ten nights from the fifth month, which supports the precautionary view. Ibn 'Umar's statement 'ten nights and nine days' is also mentioned, indicating the inclusion of the tenth night. A linguistic proof is provided: Arabic scholars state that the number agrees with the gender of the counted noun when it is mentioned; but if the counted noun is omitted (as in 'ten' instead of 'ten nights'), the feminine 'ta' may be dropped from the number even when the counted noun is masculine. The Prophet's hadith 'followed it with six from Shawwal' is evidence, where 'six' (sittan) drops the 'ta' because the counted noun (days) is masculine. The reason al-Razi gives for using 'nights' metaphorically is that these days are considered ill-omened, so they are called 'nights' by metaphor.

Key Terms
العدد (al-‘adad): The numeral (e.g., ten, six) that agrees with the gender of the counted noun.
المعدود (al-ma‘dud): The noun being counted (e.g., days, nights) whose gender determines the form of the numeral.
التاء (al-ta'): The feminine marker (ta' marbutah) at the end of a numeral, dropped when the counted noun is masculine and omitted.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) At sunset on the tenth day of the fifth month
B) After the tenth night of the fifth month has passed
C) At the beginning of the ninth day
D) At the end of the ninth night

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that a widow's idda of four months and ten days requires the tenth night to pass if the husband died before dawn, based on linguistic evidence and the precautionary principle.

Counting Idda in Mid-Month Events: The Hanafi Intra-Madhhab Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on idda calculation and its extension to other rulings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the difference between Imam Abu Hanifa and the two companions (Sahibayn) on counting idda when divorce or death occurs in the middle of a lunar month, and apply the mufta bihi ruling.

Core Idea
When idda begins mid-month, Abu Hanifa counts by days (90/130); the companions count first and last months by crescents and middle by lunar months.

Counting the idda accurately is essential to determine when a woman becomes lawful for remarriage. But what if the divorce or death occurs not at the beginning but in the middle of a lunar month? Within the Hanafi school, Imām Abū Ḥanīfa and his two companions differ on the method. This lesson unpacks the dispute, its reasoning, and the relied-upon position, which also applies to other areas like ilā', oaths, and rental contracts.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the ruling of Imām Abū Ḥanīfa on counting idda when the event occurs mid-month.
He counts entirely by days: 90 for divorce, 130 for death.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
State the view of the two companions (Abū Yūsuf and Muḥammad al-Shaybānī).
They count the first and last months by crescents and the months in between by lunar months.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
Explain the reasoning behind the companions' view as given in the passage.
If the second and third months were counted by lunar months, they might be short (e.g., 28 days), leaving a deficiency that prevents proper expiration of idda.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
How does the text (al-Muhīṭ) resolve the dispute?
The companions' view is declared sahīḥ (correct) because it avoids the deficiency problem.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilāf
What is the practical difference in the number of days of idda?
Abū Ḥanīfa gives fixed days (90/130); the companions' method gives a slightly longer period (e.g., 91-92 days for divorce).

وفي المحيط : إذا اتفق عدة الطلاق والموت في غرة الشهر اعتبرت الشهور بالأهلة، وإن انقصت عن العدد، وإن اتفق في وسط الشهر فعند الإمام تعتبر بالأيام فتعتد في الطلاق بتسعين يوماً، وفي الوفاة بمائة وثلاثين يوماً. وعندهما يكمل الأول من الأخير وما بينهما بالأهلة ومدة الإيلاء واليمين أن لا يكلم فلاناً أربعة أشهر والإجارة سنة في وسط الشهر وسن الرجل متى ولد في أثنائه، وصوم الكفارة إذا شرع فيه من وسط الشهر على هذا الاختلاف اهـ. وقدمنا عن المجتبى تأجيل العنين إذا كان في أثناء الشهر فإنه يعتبر بالأيام إجماعاً، ويستثنى أيضاً من الخلاف لو طلق الحامل في وسط الشهر فإنه يفصل بين كل طلاقين بثلاثين يوماً فإذا طلقها الثالثة فقد بانت منه بثلاث وبقي من عدتها ثلاثون يوماً وهو غيرها . ثم رأيت في القهستاني ما نصه والأول أحوط لزيادة ليلة كما في النظم وغيره لكن زيادتها محل تأمل ا هـ . وكان مراده بالتأمل ما قلنا من الزيادة غير مطردة قوله : (فإنه يجوز ترك التاء في العدد الخ) اقتصر على ترك التاء لكون ما نحن فيه كذلك وإلا فكذلك يجوز إثباتها في العدد الذي معدوده مؤنث قال الشمس محمد الداودي في حواشي ابن عقيل واعلم أن الأستاذ الصفوي نقل في شرح كافية ابن الحاجب عن الإمام النووي أنه نقل عن العلماء أيضاً زيادة التاء للمذكر وتركها للمؤنث إنما يجب إذا كان المميز مذكوراً بعد اسم العدد، وأما إذا حذف أو قدم وجعل اسم العدد صفة فيجوز حينئذ في اسم العدد إلحاق التاء وحذفها مع كل من المذكر والمؤنث. وقال الصفوي : فاحفظها فإنها عزيزة وخرج عليها الشنواني في حواشي الآجرومية قول مؤلفها. والمضارع ما كان في أوله إحدى الزوائد الأربع والزوائد جمع زائدة فكان القياس أحد الزوائد. والعلامة الغنيمي قول الهداية فرائض الصلاة ستة وإلا فلا محل لقول الأكمل القياس أن يقول ست لأن الفرائض جمع فريضة . قوله : (لو طلق الحامل في وسط الشهر) كذا في النسخ ولعله الحائل بالهمز والمراد بها الآيسة لأن ذات الحمل عدتها وضعه في الطلاق والموت كما سيأتي تأمل. قول الكل وهو الصحيح لأن عندهما تعذر اعتبار الأهلة في جميع العدة لأنا لو اعتبرنا الشهر الثاني والثالث بالهلال في حق انقضاء العدة فربما ينقصان ،يومين فمتى اعتبرنا الفاصل بين الطلاقين ثلاثين يوماً يبقى بعد الطلقة الثالثة ثمانية وعشرون يوماً وذلك أقل من شهر ولا يجوز انقضاء العدة به؛ كذا في المحيط وفي الصغرى واعتبار العدة بالأيام اجماعاً إنما الخلاف في الإجارة ا ونقله عنها في التتارخانية.
In al-Muhit: If the 'idda of divorce and death coincide with the beginning of the month, the months are counted by lunar crescents, even if they are less than the number. If it occurs in the middle of the month, according to Imam (Abu Hanifa) it is counted by days: she observes 'idda in divorce as ninety days, and in death as one hundred and thirty days. According to them (the two companions), the first and last are completed by the crescent and what is between them by months. The period of ila' and the oath 'I will not speak to so-and-so for four months', and the rental contract for a year in the middle of the month, and the age of a man when born in the middle, and the fast of expiation if begun in the middle of the month, follow this difference. (End of quote). And we have previously presented from al-Mujtaba: The postponement for the impotent man (if it is during the month) is counted by days by consensus. An exception from the dispute is if one divorces a pregnant woman in the middle of the month: he separates between every two divorces by thirty days; when he has divorced her the third time, she has become irrevocably divorced by three and remains of her 'idda thirty days, which is different. Then I saw in al-Qahistani: 'The first is more precautionary due to the addition of a night, as in the Nazm and elsewhere, but its addition is subject to scrutiny.' And what he meant by scrutiny is what we said: the addition is not consistent. His statement: 'Indeed it is permissible to drop the ta' in the number...' He limited himself to dropping the ta' because our case is like that, but otherwise it is also permissible to affirm it in the number whose counted noun is feminine. Al-Shams Muhammad al-Dawudi said in his glosses on Ibn 'Aqil: 'Know that al-Ustadh al-Safawi transmitted in his commentary on the Kafiya of Ibn al-Hajib from Imam al-Nawawi that he also transmitted from the scholars: the addition of the ta' for masculine and dropping it for feminine is obligatory only when the genitive is mentioned after the numeral; but if it is omitted or placed before and the numeral is made an adjective, then it is permissible to either add or drop the ta' for both masculine and feminine.' Al-Safawi said: 'Memorize it, for it is rare.' And al-Shanawani derived from it in his glosses on al-Ajrumiyyah the statement of its author: 'The present tense is what has at its beginning one of the four additions,' but 'additions' is plural of 'addition', so the analogy would be 'one of the additions'. And al-'Allama al-Ghunaimi [commented on] the statement of al-Hidayah: 'The obligatory acts of prayer are six,' otherwise there is no place for the statement of al-Akmal that the analogy is to say 'six' because 'obligatory acts' is plural of 'obligatory act'. His statement: (If he divorces a pregnant woman in the middle of the month) thus in the copies, and perhaps it is 'al-ha'il' with a hamza, meaning the menopausal woman, because the 'idda of a pregnant woman is delivery, in both divorce and death, as will come. Consider. The statement of all: It is the correct one, because according to them (the two companions) it is impossible to consider the crescents for the entire 'idda, because if we consider the second and third months by the crescent regarding the expiration of 'idda, perhaps they may be deficient by two days; so if we consider the interval between the two divorces as thirty days, after the third divorce there remains twenty-eight days, which is less than a month, and it is not permissible for the 'idda to expire by that. So it is in al-Muhit and in al-Sughra: 'Counting 'idda by days is by consensus; the dispute is only in the rental contract.' And he (al-Quduri) transmitted it from it in al-Tatar Khaniyya.

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute (intra_hanafi_dispute). It begins by quoting al-Muhit on how to count 'idda when the event (divorce or death) occurs at the beginning or middle of a lunar month. If at the beginning, lunar months are counted by crescents even if incomplete. If in the middle, Imam Abu Hanifa holds that counting is by days: 90 days for divorce (3 months * 30) and 130 for death (4 months * 30 + 10? Actually 4 months * 30 = 120, plus 10 days? The text says 130 days, which corresponds to the 'idda of death: four months and ten days, but if counting by days, 4*30=120 plus 10=130). The two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold that the first and last months are completed by the crescent (i.e., they count the remaining days of the current month and then count full lunar months for the middle, and then the final month is completed by the crescent as well, taking more days if the months are short). The same difference applies to the period of 'ila', oaths, rental contracts, age, and fasting of expiation. Exceptions are then listed: for the impotent man, counting by days is consensus; for a pregnant woman divorced in the middle of the month, each interval between divorces is 30 days, so after the third divorce, 30 days remain, which is different from the general rule. A comment from al-Qahistani is added about the first view being more precautionary due to the extra night, but this is scrutinized. Then follows a lengthy linguistic note about dropping the 'ta' from numerals, quoting numerous scholars. This note supports the use of 'thirty' (without feminine ending) in the context. Finally, a textual correction: perhaps the phrase 'if he divorces a pregnant woman' should read 'al-ha'il' (menopausal woman) since the idda of a pregnant woman ends with delivery. The passage concludes with an explanation of why the companions' view is considered correct: because if the second and third months were counted by lunar months, they might be deficient by two days, so after the third divorce only 28 days would remain, which is less than a month, so the 'idda cannot expire; thus the companions' method avoids this by counting the first and last months by crescents and the middle by days. The authority for this is al-Muhit and al-Sughra, and the dispute is only in the rental contract.

Key Terms
غرة الشهر (ghurrat al-shahr): The beginning of the lunar month (first day).
الإيلاء (al-īlā'): An oath taken by a husband not to have intercourse with his wife for a specified period (up to four months), after which divorce may be pronounced.
الحامل (al-hāmil): Pregnant woman. In the context, the text notes a possible textual corruption: it should be 'al-ha'il' (menopausal woman) because a pregnant woman's 'idda ends with delivery.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), how is the 'idda counted when divorce occurs in the middle of a month?
A) Entirely by days (90 days)
B) First and last months by crescents, middle months by lunar months
C) Entirely by lunar months
D) First month by crescent, remaining by days

Key Takeaway
The mufta bihi in the Hanafi school for mid-month idda is the companions' view: count the first and last months by crescents and the intervening months by lunar months.

Conditions for Acting on a Report of an Absent Husband's Death
Conditions and effects of a death report

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which a woman may remarry based on a report of her absent husband's death, and apply the rule for conflicting reports based on dates.

Core Idea
A single just informant suffices; later dated reports take precedence.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Raiq addresses the fiqh scenario of the absent husband. The Hanafi school sets clear conditions to protect the woman's right to remarry while managing contradictory reports. We will examine the key conditions and the interplay between different reports.

Guided Path
1
Identify the key parties
The woman has an absent husband; she receives a report of his death from one man and a report of his life from two men.
2
Examine the condition for acting on death report
The single informer must be just ('adl) and must have witnessed the death or seen the funeral procession.
Justice ('adala) is a standard condition for testimony in Hanafi fiqh.
3
Determine the effect of no dates
If the reports do not include dates, the woman may act on the death report, begin 'idda, and remarry.
4
Determine the effect of dates
If the reports are dated and the life report is later, the later testimony takes precedence, and the death report is disregarded.
Later testimony is considered more reliable because it is based on more recent information.
5
Understand the role of the woman's belief
According to al-Muntaqa, the woman's personal belief in the informer is not required; the condition is solely the justice of the informer.
This distinguishes the ruling from testimonial evidence in court, where belief is not formalized.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي التتارخانية امرأة الغائب إذا أخبرها رجل بموت زوجها وأخبرها رجلان بحياته فإن كان الذي أخبر بموته شهد أنه عاين موته أو جنازته وكان عدلاً وسعها أن تعتد وتتزوج، هذا إذا لم يؤرخا ، فإن أرخا وتاريخ شهود الحياة متأخر فشهادتهما أولى. وفي النسفية : سئل عن امرأ امرأة لها زوج غائب أخبرها رجل بموته فاعتدت وتزوجت ودخل بها فجاء آخر وأخبرها أنه حي في بلد كذا وأنا رأيته فهل يحل لها المقام مع الثاني ؟ فقال : إن كانت صدقت المخبر الأول لا يمكنها أن تصدق المخبر الثاني ولا يبطل النكاح الثاني ولهما أن يقرا على ذلك النكاح. وفي شهادات البزازية : قال رجل لامرأة سمعت أن زوجك مات لها أن تتزوج إن كان المخبر ،عدلاً، فإن تزوجت بآخر وأخبرها جماعة بأنه حي إن صدقت الأول صح النكاح ؛ كذا في فتاوى النسفي. وفي المنتقى : شرط عدالة المخبر ولا يشترط تصديقها. وفي النوازل لو عدلاً لكن أعمى أو محدوداً في قذف جاز، ولو شهد عندها عدل أن زوجها ارتد هل لها أن تتزوج؟ فيه روايتان : في رواية لسبر لا يجوز وفي الاستحسان يجوز
In al-Tatar Khaniyya: Regarding a woman of an absent husband: if a man informs her of her husband's death and two men inform her of his life: if the one who informed of his death testifies that he witnessed his death or his funeral and he is just, it is permissible for her to observe 'idda and marry. This is if they do not give a date. If they give a date and the date of the witnesses of life is later, then their testimony takes precedence. In al-Nasafiyya: He was asked about a woman who has an absent husband; a man informed her of his death, so she observed 'idda and married and he had intercourse with her; then another came and informed her that he is alive in such-and-such town and I saw him. Is it permissible for her to remain with the second? He said: If she believed the first informer, she cannot believe the second informer, nor does the second marriage become invalid, and they may continue in that marriage. In Shahadat al-Bazzaziyya: A man said to a woman, 'I heard that your husband died,' she may marry if the informer is just. If she marries another and a group inform her that he is alive, if she believed the first, the marriage is valid. So in Fatawa al-Nasafi. In al-Muntaqa: The condition is the justice of the informer, and her belief in him is not required. In al-Nawazil: If (the man) is just but blind or had been flogged for false accusation, it is permissible. And if a just man testifies before her that her husband has apostatized, may she marry? There are two narrations: in one according to al-Lays? It is not permissible, and in al-Istihsan it is permissible.
Source pages p. 224

Commentary
This passage presents a set of rulings (ruling_with_proof) regarding a woman whose husband is absent and she receives news of his death. Several scenarios are discussed. First, from al-Tatar Khaniyya: If one man (who is just and either witnessed the death or saw the funeral) reports death, and two men report that he is alive, the wife may act on the death report and begin 'idda and remarry. However, if the reports are dated and the life report is later, that takes precedence. Second, from al-Nasafiyya: If she actually believed the first report, remarried, and later another man says he saw the husband alive, the second marriage remains valid because her belief in the first report cannot be retroactively changed. Third, from Shahadat al-Bazzaziyya: The condition for her to remarry is that the informer is just; she need not personally believe him. Fourth, from al-Muntaqa: Justice of the informer is the condition, not her belief. Fifth, from al-Nawazil: Even if the just informer is blind or had been punished for false accusation (which normally impairs testimony), his report is still valid. Finally, regarding apostasy: if a just man testifies that her husband has apostatized, there are two narrations: one disallows remarriage (based on the rule of apostasy), and another allows it by istihsan. The text does not specify which is preferred, but the inclusion indicates the Hanafi position that istihsan may permit it.

Key Terms
المخبر (al-mukhbir): The informant who reports the death or life of the absent husband. His justice ('adala) is a condition.
العدالة (al-'adalah): Justice or uprightness; a condition for the informer. Includes being Muslim, adult, of good character, and not convicted of major sins.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to al-Muntaqa, what is the condition for a woman to remarry after receiving news of her absent husband's death?
A) The informer must be just and she must believe him
B) The informer must be just, regardless of her belief
C) Two witnesses must testify
D) The husband's body must be brought

Key Takeaway
A single just informant who witnessed the death suffices for a woman to remarry; her personal belief is not required.

Special Cases: Blind Informer, Flogged Informer, and the Apostasy Testimony Dispute
Special conditions and the apostasy dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the rulings for exceptional informers (blind, flogged for qadhf) and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute on remarriage based on testimony of apostasy.

Core Idea
Even a just but physically impaired or previously punished informer suffices; apostasy testimony contains a dispute resolved by istihsan.

The previous lesson covered the basic conditions. Now we delve into edge cases from al-Nawazil: what if the informer is blind or has been flogged for false accusation? And finally, a disputed scenario: can a woman remarry based on testimony that her husband apostatised?

Guided Path
1
Analyze the blind or flogged informer
The text says that even if the just informer is blind or has been flogged for qadhf, his report is still valid. This is because his justice is intact; physical impairment or past punishment does not affect the reliability of his information.
Flogging for qadhf normally disqualifies a person from testifying in court, but here it is overlooked due to necessity.
2
Identify the apostasy scenario
A just man testifies that the woman's husband has apostatised. Apostasy dissolves marriage. The question: can she remarry based on this testimony?
3
Understand the two narrations
There are two Hanafi narrations: one (attributed to 'al-Lays' possibly a scribal error) says it is not permissible; the other, based on istihsan, permits it.
The text does not resolve which is mufta bihi, but the mention of 'al-istihsan yajuz' suggests preference.
4
Apply the istihsan principle
Istihsan allows overriding qiyas (which might require stricter proof) for public interest. Here, permitting remarriage avoids hardship for the woman.

Source Text

General Ruling on Idda of a Free Woman for Death
General rule and exceptions regarding the idda of a free woman whose husband dies

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the general rule of 'idda for a free woman upon her husband's death and identify the conditions and exceptions, particularly for pregnant women and mukatab cases.

Core Idea
The 'idda of death for a free woman is four months and ten days, but pregnancy overrides it, and a fasid marriage triggers no 'idda.

In this passage, the commentator expands on the general rule of 'idda for a free woman whose husband dies. While the base text (Kanz al-Daqa'iq) simply states the period, the commentary here clarifies which women are included, what conditions must be met, and the specific exceptions. Understanding this will help you apply the correct waiting period in various marital statuses.

Guided Path
1
Identify the General Rule
The 'idda of a free woman for death is four months and ten days, covering all free women regardless of religion, age, or consummation.
The passage uses the phrase 'أطلق' (made absolute) to indicate generality.
2
Recognise the Exception for Pregnancy
A pregnant woman's 'idda ends at delivery, even for death. This overrides the general period.
The text notes that the author delayed mentioning the pregnant woman to indicate the general rule remains valid.
3
Verify the Condition for Obligation
The marriage must be valid (sahih) at the time of death. If the marriage was fasid (invalid), no 'idda is required.
This condition is cited from al-Bada'i: 'cause is death, condition is valid marriage'.
4
Apply the Ruling to Mukatab Cases
A mukatab (slave in manumission) who buys his wife and dies triggers different 'idda rules based on his ability to pay (wafa') and whether there was consummation or children.
Four scenarios: no consummation (no 'idda); consummation + child (three periods); consummation no child (two periods); insolvent (2 months 5 days).
5
Determine the Start of 'Idda
The 'idda begins from the time of death, not from the time the woman learns of it.
This is stated explicitly: 'ما بدؤها من وقت الوفاة لا من وقت العلم بها'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأطلق في عدة الحرة للموت فشمل المسلمة والكتابية تحت المسلم صغيرة كانت أو كبيرة أو آيسة سواء كان زوجها حراً أو عبداً، قبل الدخول أو بعده، ولم يخرج عنها إلا الحامل فإنها تعتد بالوضع في الوفاة أيضاً ولذا أخر عدة الحامل عن المتوفى عنها زوجها للإشارة إلى أنها باقية على عمومها كما سترى. وفي البدائع أن سببها الموت وشرط وجوبها النكاح الصحيح فلا تجب في النكاح الفاسد ا هـ . وسيأتي أن مبدأها من وقت الوفاة لا من وقت العلم بها ولا بد من بقاء النكاح صحيحاً إلى الموت فلو فسد قبله لم تجب عدة الوفاة ولهذا قدمنا أن المكاتب لو اشترى زوجته ثم مات عن وفاء لم تجب عدة الوفاء فإن لم يدخل بها فلا عدة أصلاً، وإن دخل بها فولدت منه صارت أم ولد له فعدتها ثلاث حيض، وإن لم تكن ولدت منه فعليها أن تعتد بحيضتين لفساد النكاح قبل الموت، وإن لم يترك وفاء تعتد بشهرين وخمسة أيام عدة الوفاء لأنهما مملو كان للمولى كما في الخانية، ولكن ذكر في المحيط أنها إذا ولدت منه وقلنا عدتها ثلاث حيض تحد في الأولين دون الثالثة، ولو تزوج المكاتب بنت مولاه فإن مات عند وفاء فعدتها عدة الحرة
And he (the author of Kanz al-Daqa'iq) made absolute concerning the 'idda of a free woman for death: it includes the Muslim woman and the Christian woman under a Muslim, whether young or old or menopausal, whether her husband is free or a slave, before consummation or after. Nothing is excluded from it except the pregnant woman, for she observes 'idda by delivery also in death. Therefore, he delayed the 'idda of the pregnant woman after the woman whose husband died to indicate that it remains on its generality, as you will see. In al-Bada'i': Its cause is death and the condition of its obligation is a valid marriage; it is not obligatory in a void marriage. (End quote). It will come that its beginning is from the time of death, not from the time of knowledge, and it is necessary that the marriage remains valid until death; so if it became void before, the 'idda of death is not obligatory. For this we have presented that if a mukatab buys his wife and then dies while able to pay (wafa'), the 'idda of death is not obligatory; if he had not consummated, there is no 'idda at all; if he had consummated and she gave birth to him, she becomes an umm walad for him, so her 'idda is three menstrual periods; if she did not give birth to him, she must observe 'idda by two menstrual periods due to the invalidity of the marriage before death. And if he left no wealth to pay (i.e., dies insolvent), she observes 'idda of two months and five days as the 'idda of a slave woman, because they are [considered] slaves of the master. As in al-Khaniyya. But it is mentioned in al-Muhit that if she gave birth to him and we say her 'idda is three menstrual periods, she is confined during the first two but not the third. And if the mukatab marries the daughter of his master, and then he dies while able to pay, her 'idda is that of a free woman.
Source pages p. 224

Commentary
This passage presents a general ruling (ruling_statement) about the 'idda of a free woman upon the death of her husband. It includes all free women regardless of religion (Muslim or Kitabiyya), age (young, old, menopausal), husband's status (free or slave), and whether the marriage was consummated. The only exception is a pregnant woman, whose 'idda ends with delivery, in both divorce and death. The text explains that the author delayed mentioning the pregnant woman's 'idda to indicate that the general rule remains applicable to others. The source (al-Bada'i) states the underlying cause (sabab) is death, and the condition for obligation is a valid (sahih) marriage; no 'idda for a fasid marriage. The beginning of the 'idda is from the time of death, not from the time she hears the news. The marriage must be valid until death; if it becomes invalid before death, the 'idda of death is not obligatory. Examples are given from the case of a mukatab (a slave in a contract of manumission) who buys his wife. If he dies while able to pay (wafa'), meaning the contract is in effect, the wife's 'idda is not four months and ten days; rather, if not consummated, no 'idda; if consummated and she bore him a child, she becomes an umm walad (mother of his child) and her 'idda is three menstrual periods; if she did not bear, her 'idda is two periods because the marriage was fasid (since a mukatab cannot own his wife properly?). If he dies insolvent (without wafa'), his wife is considered a slave of the master (since he is still a slave), so her 'idda is that of a slave: two months and five days. The text adds from al-Muhit that if she gave birth and her 'idda is three periods, she observes 'idda only for the first two periods, not the third? Actually it says 'she is confined in the first two but not the third' (tahiddu fi al-awwalayni duna al-thalitha), meaning her 'idda of three periods, the mourning (ihdad) applies only to the first two? But that is a detail. Finally, if a mukatab marries his master's daughter and dies while able to pay, her 'idda is that of a free woman (four months and ten days).

Key Terms
عِدَّة (‘idda): The waiting period a woman must observe after divorce or death of her husband before she can remarry.
أم ولد (umm walad): A female slave who has borne a child to her master, freeing her upon his death.
مكاتب (mukatab): A slave who has made a contract of manumission with his master, agreeing to pay a sum in installments.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the 'idda of a free Muslim woman whose husband dies before consummation?
A) Three menstrual periods
B) Four months and ten days
C) No 'idda because no consummation
D) Until she delivers if pregnant

Key Takeaway
A free woman's 'idda of death is four months and ten days, but only if her marriage was valid; pregnancy overrides, and mukatab cases have special rules.

Calculating 'Iddah by Days When Divorce Occurs Mid-Month
Clarifying 'iddah calculation method

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that 'iddah must be calculated by 30-day intervals when divorce occurs mid-month and explain why lunar months cannot be used.

Core Idea
When divorce occurs mid-month, 'iddah must use complete 30-day intervals, not lunar months.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq clarifies a critical detail in 'iddah calculation for the Hanafi school. When a husband divorces his wife using three talags with intervals, and the divorce occurs in the middle of a lunar month, the months may be incomplete. Therefore, the school relies on daily intervals to ensure the full 'iddah is observed. Let us understand the reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
The ruling applies when a woman is divorced with three talags with intervals, and the first divorce occurs in the middle of a lunar month.
The passage assumes the intervals are between each talaq.
2
Recognise the problem
Lunar months can be 29 or 30 days; if we use them, the second and third months might be deficient by two days, leading to an incomplete 'iddah.
The example in the text shows a deficiency of two days.
3
Understand the solution
Instead of lunar months, each interval between divorces is fixed at 30 days, ensuring the full 'iddah period.
This method guarantees at least three 30-day intervals.
4
Note the authority
The passage cites al-Muhit and al-Sughra, indicating this is the relied-upon view of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, declared correct.
The text says 'قول الكل وهو الصحيح' (the statement of all is correct).
5
Distinguish from other contexts
The passage notes that disagreement only exists in ijarah (leasing), not in 'iddah, where there is consensus.
Do not confuse the two areas.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قول الكل وهو الصحيح لأن عندهما تعذر اعتبار الأهلة في جميع العدة لأنا لو اعتبرنا الشهر الثاني والثالث بالهلال في حق انقضاء العدة فربما ينقصان يومين فمتى اعتبرنا الفاصل بين الطلاقين ثلاثين يوماً يبقى بعد الطلقة الثالثة ثمانية وعشرون يوماً وذلك أقل من شهر ولا يجوز انقضاء العدة به؛ كذا في المحيط وفي الصغرى واعتبار العدة بالأيام اجماعاً إنما الخلاف في الإجارة ا ونقله عنها في التتارخانية.
The statement of all is that it is correct because according to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) it is impossible to consider the lunar months in all of the 'iddah, for if we consider the second and third months by the crescent with respect to the expiry of the 'iddah, they might be deficient by two days. So when we consider the interval between the two divorces as thirty days, after the third divorce remains twenty-eight days, which is less than a month, and it is not permissible for the 'iddah to expire with that; thus in al-Muhit. And in al-Sughra: considering the 'iddah by days is by consensus; the disagreement is only in ijarah? and he transmitted it from her in al-Tatar Khaniyya.
Source pages p. 224

Commentary
This passage explains the correct ruling within the Hanafi school regarding the calculation of 'iddah when a husband divorces his wife in the middle of a month using three talags with intervals. According to the view of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani (the Sahibayn), it is not feasible to use lunar months consistently because they may be incomplete by two days. Therefore, the interval between each talaq is fixed at thirty days, ensuring the full 'iddah period. The text cites al-Muhit and al-Sughra, indicating consensus among the Hanafis on using days for calculation, with any difference being only in the context of ijarah (as mentioned in al-Tatar Khaniyya).

Key Terms
عدة (‘iddah): The waiting period a woman must observe after divorce or death to ensure the womb is clear.

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) Because the husband prefers it.

Key Takeaway
For 'iddah, when divorce occurs mid-month, the Hanafi school requires using 30-day intervals to ensure the full waiting period, as lunar months may be incomplete.

The 'Iddah of a Free Woman Due to Death: General Rule and Exceptions
Stating the general rule and its exceptions for the 'iddah of a free woman upon death.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the general rule for the 'iddah of a free woman upon the death of her husband and identify the exceptions and special cases involving a fasid marriage or a mukatib husband.

Core Idea
A free woman's 'iddah for her husband's death is required regardless of age, faith, or consummation; only pregnancy excepts her.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq expands on the basic ruling for the 'iddah of a free woman whose husband dies. It clarifies that the ruling applies universally to all free women, irrespective of their personal characteristics or the status of the husband. The text then delves into the cause and condition for this 'iddah, and illustrates with the complex case of a mukatib (slave under contract of manumission) to show how changes in the husband's status affect the wife's 'iddah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general rule and its scope
The passage begins by stating the general 'iddah of death for a free woman. Recognize that it covers all free women, Muslim or Kitabiyya, young or old, menopausal, regardless of the husband's status (free or slave), and whether or not the marriage was consummated.
2
Recognize the exception for pregnancy
The only exception is the pregnant woman, whose 'iddah ends with delivery.
3
Determine the cause and condition for the 'iddah of death
As per al-Bada'i', the cause is death, and the condition is a valid marriage at the time of death. If the marriage was fasid (defective) before death, no 'iddah of death is required.
Sabab and shart are key usul concepts.
4
Analyze the mukatib cases
The text presents several scenarios for a mukatib who buys his wife and then dies. Understand how the husband's ability to pay and the presence of a child affect the wife's 'iddah.
The mukatib's status as effectively free or slave is decisive.
5
Apply the ruling to the case of a mukatib marrying his master's daughter
The final scenario shows that if the mukatib dies with ability to pay, the wife (being free) observes the 'iddah of a free woman.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأطلق في عدة الحرة للموت فشمل المسلمة والكتابية تحت المسلم صغيرة كانت أو كبيرة أو آيسة سواء كان زوجها حراً أو عبداً، قبل الدخول أو بعده، ولم يخرج عنها إلا الحامل فإنها تعتد بالوضع في الوفاة أيضاً ولذا أخر عدة الحامل عن المتوفى عنها زوجها للإشارة إلى أنها باقية على عمومها كما سترى. وفي البدائع أن سببها الموت وشرط وجوبها النكاح الصحيح فلا تجب في النكاح الفاسد ا هـ . وسيأتي أن مبدأها من وقت الوفاة لا من وقت العلم بها ولا بد من بقاء النكاح صحيحاً إلى الموت فلو فسد قبله لم تجب عدة الوفاة ولهذا قدمنا أن المكاتب لو اشترى زوجته ثم مات عن وفاء لم تجب عدة الوفاء فإن لم يدخل بها فلا عدة أصلاً، وإن دخل بها فولدت منه صارت أم ولد له فعدتها ثلاث حيض، وإن لم تكن ولدت منه فعليها أن تعتد بحيضتين لفساد النكاح قبل الموت، وإن لم يترك وفاء تعتد بشهرين وخمسة أيام عدة الوفاء لأنهما مملو كان للمولى كما في الخانية، ولكن ذكر في المحيط أنها إذا ولدت منه وقلنا عدتها ثلاث حيض تحد في الأولين دون الثالثة، ولو تزوج المكاتب بنت مولاه فإن مات عند وفاء فعدتها عدة الحرة
And he (the author) made absolute (the ruling) regarding the 'iddah of a free woman due to death, so it includes the Muslim woman and the Kitabiyya under a Muslim husband, whether she is young, old, or menopausal, whether her husband was free or slave, before or after consummation. And nothing excepts her except the pregnant woman, for she observes 'iddah by giving birth in the case of death as well. Therefore, he delayed the (section on) 'iddah of the pregnant woman after the woman whose husband has died, to indicate that she remains under the general rule as you will see. And in al-Bada'i': The cause (sabab) of it is death, and the condition for its obligation is a valid marriage. So it is not obligatory in a fasid marriage. And it will come that its beginning is from the time of death, not from the time of knowledge. And it is necessary that the marriage remain valid until death. If it became fasid before death, the 'iddah of death is not obligatory. For this reason we previously stated that if a mukatib (slave under contract of manumission) buys his wife then dies with ability to pay, the 'iddah of death is not obligatory. If he did not consummate with her, then there is no 'iddah at all. If he consummated and she gave birth to a child from him, she becomes an umm walad, and her 'iddah is three menstruations. If she did not give birth from him, then she must observe 'iddah of two menstruations due to the fasid marriage before death. If he did not leave ability to pay, she observes 'iddah of two months and five days, the 'iddah of death, because they are slaves belonging to the master, as in al-Khaniyya. But it is mentioned in al-Muhit that if she gives birth from him and we say her 'iddah is three menstruations, she is confined ('iddah) in the first two but not the third. And if the mukatib marries his master's daughter, then dies with ability to pay, her 'iddah is that of a free woman.
Source pages p. 224

Commentary
This passage lays out the general rule for the 'iddah of a free woman whose husband dies. The 'iddah is four months and ten days (though that specific number is not stated here, it is implied from the context and from other parts of the school). The ruling applies universally to all free women, regardless of their marital status (young, old, menopausal, Muslim, Kitabiyya, etc.) and regardless of whether the husband was free or slave, and whether the marriage was consummated or not. The only exception is the pregnant woman, whose 'iddah ends with delivery. The text then discusses the cause (sabab) and condition for obligation: the cause is death, and the condition is a valid marriage at the time of death. If the marriage was fasid (defective) before death, then no 'iddah of death is required. To illustrate, the author brings the case of a mukatib (a slave who has contracted to purchase his freedom) who buys his wife (i.e., marries her, but a mukatib can marry with permission). If he dies with sufficient funds to complete his manumission (i.e., he is considered capable of paying), then the wife does not observe the 'iddah of death if the marriage was not consummated. If consummated and she bears a child, she becomes an umm walad and her 'iddah is three menstrual cycles (as per the 'iddah of a slave). If consummated but no child, her 'iddah is two menstruations because the marriage was fasid. If the mukatib dies without ability to pay, then the wife is considered a slave (since the mukatib himself is still a slave) and her 'iddah is two months and five days, like a slave's 'iddah for death. The text also notes a nuance from al-Muhit: if she gives birth and her 'iddah is three, she is confined only in the first two (meaning the third menstruation may not occur because of childbirth? Or that the 'iddah ends with the second menstruation? The text says 'تحدث في الأولين دون الثالثة' meaning her period occurs in the first two but not the third, implying that the third menstruation is not counted. This is a specific detail. Finally, if the mukatib marries his master's daughter and dies with ability to pay, the wife (the master's daughter) is free and her 'iddah is that of a free woman (four months and ten days).

Key Terms
عدة الوفاة (‘iddat al-wafāt): The waiting period observed by a woman after her husband's death, normally four months and ten days for a free woman.
مكاتب (mukātib): A slave who has entered into a contract of manumission with his master, agreeing to pay a sum in installments.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) After four months and ten days.
B) When she delivers her child.
C) After three menstrual cycles.
D) It never ends until she remarries.

Key Takeaway
A free woman's 'iddah upon her husband's death is universal, subject only to the condition of a valid marriage and the exception of pregnancy.

The 'Iddah of an Enslaved Woman and the Principle of Halving
Detailing the 'iddah rules for an enslaved woman and the general principle of halving

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the 'iddah periods for an enslaved woman in divorce and death, explain the Hanafi principle of tanṣīf, and defend the Hanafi proof for this ruling against objections from the general Quranic text.

Core Idea
Slavery halves certain rulings: an enslaved woman's 'iddah is two cycles, not three.

In the previous lesson, you learned the 'iddah of a free woman. Now we turn to the enslaved woman. The Hanafi school holds that her 'iddah is less than that of a free woman, based on a specific hadith. But this raises a critical question: how can a single-narrator hadith specify a general Quranic verse? In this lesson, you will not only learn the ruling but also how the Hanafi school defends its proof structure in response to objections.

Guided Path
1
Identify the 'iddah periods for an enslaved woman
State the specific durations for divorce (two menstruations or 1.5 months) and death (2 months 5 days), noting the condition of consummation for divorce.
Consummation is required for divorce 'iddah but not for death 'iddah.
2
Apply the principle of tanṣīf
Recognize that slavery halves certain legal consequences (hudud, marriage, divorce) but not others (worship, qisas). Explain why this principle applies here.
The principle is derived from the hadith and analogy, not all rulings are halved.
3
Examine the dalil: the hadith
The key evidence is the hadith 'and her 'iddah is two menstruations.' Determine its transmission level (khabar wahid) and its apparent meaning.
The hadith is reported by multiple compilers but is technically khabar wahid; however, it is considered mashhur.
4
Address the objection from the general Quranic text
The objection: the Quran says 'three menstrual periods' for all women (general), so how can a khabar wahid specify it? The Hanafi response: the hadith is mashhur, or the verse is specifically about free women due to context.
Specification of a general text by a well-known hadith is permissible in Hanafi usul.
5
Consider intra-Hanafi and external differences
Note that Abu Hanifa includes all slave categories, while the Sahibayn differ on the status of the mustas'a. Also, Abu Bakr al-Asamm (Mu'tazili) rejects the hadith and follows the general verse.
The practical difference: mustas'a is considered a slave by Abu Hanifa, free by the Sahibayn.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Regarding death, whether he consummated or not, otherwise she does not observe 'iddah for death. If he did not consummate, then no 'iddah. If he consummated, she observes 'iddah of three menstruations. His statement: (And for the enslaved woman is a Quran [i.e., prescribed portion] and a half of the specified amount) meaning: the 'iddah of an enslaved woman is two menstruations in divorce after consummation if she is of those who menstruate; otherwise, one and a half months in divorce, and two months and five days in death. He made it absolute, so it includes the absolute female slave (qinn), the umm walad, the mudabbara, the mukataba, and the mustas'a according to Imam Abu Hanifa, whether she is partially free or not, like the one emancipated in terminal illness if it does not exceed one-third, and the mudabbara after her master's death during the period of sa'yah (working for freedom). For the mustas'a is like the mukatab according to him, and according to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) he is a free man in debt. And the condition of consummation is necessary concerning the enslaved woman except for the one whose husband has died. The summary is that slavery halves benefit and punishment, but in prayer, fasting and purification they are equal. In fasting expiations they are equal. In the period of the impotent man (ajal al-'innin) they are equal, contrary to the ila of an enslaved woman, for it is half as we have mentioned. In hudud it is half, in marriage it is half, in divorce it is half (consideration being given to the woman), in qisas they are equal, contrary to limbs; it is half except in acts of worship and that which has the meaning of worship, ila, and qisas. The evidence for the halving in the 'iddah of an enslaved woman is the hadith: 'and her 'iddah is two menstruations.' It was objected in al-Kafi that this is opposed by the general categorical (Quranic) text, and specification of a general text initially is not permissible by khabar wahid and qiyas. For this reason Abu Bakr al-Asamm said that her 'iddah is three menstrual periods. And he (the author) answered that (the hadith) is from the well-known (mashhur) narrations accepted by the umma, or because the verse is only about free women due to the context: 'for what you have given them' [2:229] and 'until she marries' [2:230] and 'what she has ransomed herself with' [2:229]. And in Kafi al-Hakim: A man died leaving a woman who was owned (a slave). She observed 'iddah of two months and five days and declared that her 'iddah had expired. Then she gave birth more than six months after the day of her declaration, the child is not attributed to the husband. But if she did not declare, the child is attributed to him up to two years. And in al-Khaniyya: A woman said during the 'iddah of death, 'I am not pregnant,' then said the next day, 'I am pregnant,' her word is accepted. But if she said after four months and ten days, 'I am not pregnant,' then said, 'I am pregnant,' her word is not accepted. And it will come at the end of the chapter. Pregnant then: (1) , His statement: (except in acts of worship) meaning it [slavery] is not halving; rather they are equal in them, and likewise in that which has the meaning of worship like expiations. And his saying: 'and ila and qisas' is conjoined to 'acts of worship'. And it appears that the mention of ila is a slip of the pen because they are not equal in it as he mentioned earlier. So the correct is to replace it with 'ajal al-'innin'. Reflect on the author's statement: (And for the pregnant woman, her delivery). He said in al-Nahr: A branch: if the fetus dies in her womb and stays for a period, how does her 'iddah end? I have not seen the issue, and it is proper that she remains in 'iddah until it descends or she reaches the age of menopause. Some virtuous men said: his statement 'or she reaches the age of menopause' is contradictory to the verse (Quran 65:4) so reflect. And in the hashya of al-Ramli, quoting from Shafi'i books, it does not end with its existence. (1) Reported by Abu Dawud in Kitab al-Talaq, chapter 6; al-Tirmidhi in Kitab al-Talaq, chapter 7; Ibn Majah in Kitab al-Talaq, chapter 30; al-Muwatta in Kitab al-Talaq, hadith 69, 91; Ahmad in his Musnad (6/117). Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, vol. 4, p. 15.
Source pages p. 225

Commentary
This extensive passage begins with a continuation of the previous discussion on the 'iddah of a free woman, then focuses on the 'iddah of an enslaved woman (umma). The key ruling: For divorce after consummation, the 'iddah of an enslaved woman is two menstrual cycles (if she menstruates); otherwise, one and a half months. For death, it is two months and five days. This applies to all categories of enslaved women: absolute slave (qinn), umm walad, mudabbara, mukataba, and mustas'a according to Imam Abu Hanifa. The condition of consummation is required for the 'iddah in divorce, but not for death 'iddah. The passage then expounds a general principle: slavery 'halves' certain rulings (tanseef) but not others. In acts of worship (like prayer, fasting, purification) and in qisas and the period for impotence ('ajal al-'innin), the slave and free are equal. In hudud, marriage, divorce, and ila, the slave's ruling is half. The evidence for halving the 'iddah is a hadith: 'and her 'iddah is two menstruations.' The text then addresses an objection from al-Kafi: that this khabar wahid cannot specify a general Quranic verse (the verse on 'iddah, which mentions three menstrual periods). The response is that this hadith is well-known (mashhur) and accepted by the umma, or the Quranic verse specifically addresses free women due to context. The passage also includes two practical cases: one from Kafi al-Hakim about a slave woman who claims her 'iddah ended and then gives birth, and another from al-Khaniyya about a woman retracting her claim of not being pregnant during 'iddah. Finally, a discussion on the 'iddah of a pregnant woman if the fetus dies in the womb: the correct view (from al-Nahr) is that she remains in 'iddah until the fetus descends or she reaches menopause. The hashya of al-Ramli notes this contradicts some interpretations. The hadith reference at the end is for the 'iddah of an enslaved woman.

Key Terms
أمة (amah): An enslaved woman, including all categories of female slaves.
تنصيف (tanṣīf): The principle that many rulings for slaves are half of those for free persons (e.g., 'iddah, hudud, marriage).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the 'iddah of an enslaved woman for divorce after consummation if she menstruates?
A) Three menstrual cycles.
B) Two menstrual cycles.
C) Two months and five days.
D) One and a half months.

Key Takeaway
The 'iddah of an enslaved woman is half that of a free woman in divorce and death, based on a specific hadith, with the Hanafi school defending its applicability against the general Quranic verse.

Iddah of Slave Women: Half the Duration and the Evidence from Hadith
Commentary on the iddah of slave women with proofs and objections

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the iddah durations for a slave woman in divorce and death, explain the Hanafi evidence from the hadith, and evaluate the objection that a khabar wahid cannot specify the general verse.

Core Idea
The iddah of a slave woman is exactly half that of a free woman, established by the mashhur hadith: 'her iddah is two menstrual cycles.'

In Hanafi fiqh, the iddah of a slave woman is halved compared to a free woman. This lesson unpacks the specific durations—two menstrual cycles in divorce (or one and a half months if she does not menstruate), and two months and five days in death—and examines the proof from a well-known hadith. We also address a key objection: how can a single-transmission hadith specify the general Qur'anic command? The Hanafi response relies on the hadith's wide acceptance and contextual interpretation of the verse.

Guided Path
1
Identify the iddah durations
State the iddah for a slave woman in both divorce and death cases. Distinguish between those who menstruate and those who do not.
Divorce: two menstrual cycles (haydatān) or one and a half months. Death: two months and five days.
2
Locate the proof (dalil)
Find the hadith quoted in the text: 'وعدتها حيضتان' and note its source. Understand that this is a khabar wahid but considered mashhur.
The hadith is narrated by Abu Dawood, Tirmidhi, Ibn Majah, Malik, and Ahmad.
3
Understand the principle of halving (tanseef)
Recognize that slavery halves certain legal consequences (e.g., iddah, hudud, marriage) but not acts of worship (prayer, fasting). This principle supports the ruling.
The commentary lists areas where slavery halves and where it does not.
4
Address the objection to khabar wahid
Consider the objection from Abu Bakr al-Asamm that a khabar wahid cannot specify a general text. Then study the Hanafi response: the hadith is mashhur and accepted by the ummah, and the verse context indicates it applies only to free women.
This is a classic usul al-fiqh debate about specifying general texts with ahadith.
5
Apply the ruling to categories of slaves
Note that the ruling applies to all types of female slaves: qin, umm walad, mudabbara, mukataba, and mustas‘a according to Abu Hanifa.
The condition of consummation is required for divorce but not for death.

If he entered, she observes iddah with three menstruations. His saying: 'And for the slave woman, two Quranic months and half the prescribed period' meaning the iddah of a slave woman is two menstruations in divorce after consummation if she menstruates, otherwise one and a half months in divorce, and two months and five days in death. He made it general, including the female slave, umm walad, mudabbara, mukataba, and one who is earning according to Imam Abu Hanifa, whether she is partially free or not, like a woman freed during a mortal illness if she does not exit from one-third, and the mudabbara after the master's death during the period of earning; the one earning is like the mukatab according to him, and a free man in debt according to them. There must be a condition of consummation for the slave woman except for the one whose husband dies. The conclusion is that slavery is half in benefit and punishment, but in prayer, fasting, and purity they are equal; in fasting of expiation they are equal; in the term of impotence they are equal, unlike the ila of a slave woman, which is half as we mentioned; in hudud it is half; in marriage it is half; in divorce it is half, and it is considered by the woman; in retaliation they are equal, unlike limbs, so it is half except in acts of worship and what has the meaning of worship, and ila, and retaliation. The evidence for halving in the iddah of a slave woman is the hadith: 'and her iddah is two menstruations.' And it was objected in al-Kafi that it is opposed by the general meaning of the decisive text, and specification of the general ab initio is not permissible by khabar wahid and qiyas, so Abu Bakr al-Asamm said her iddah is three menstrual periods, and he answered that it is from the famous reports accepted by the ummah, or because the verse is only for free women, as indicated by context: 'and take what you have given them' [Baqara 229] 'until she marries [another]' [Baqara 230] 'what she ransoms herself with' [Baqara 229]. And in Kafi al-Hakim: A man died leaving a slave woman, she observed iddah for two months and five days and admitted the expiry of her iddah, then gave birth after more than six months from the day of admission—the husband is not liable, but if she did not admit, the child is ascribed to him within two years. In al-Khaniyya: A woman in the iddah of death said, 'I am not pregnant,' then the next day said, 'I am pregnant'—her word is accepted; but if she said after four months and ten days, 'I am not pregnant' then said 'I am pregnant,' her word is not accepted. And his saying 'and ila and retaliation' is connected to 'acts of worship.' The apparent is that mentioning of ila was a slip of the pen because they are not equal in it as he mentioned earlier, so the correction is to replace it with the term of the impotent man. Consider the author's statement: 'And for the pregnant, her placing [the child].' He said in al-Nahr: A branch: if the fetus dies in her womb and remains for a time, how does her iddah expire? I did not see the issue, and it should be that she remains in iddah until it is expelled or reaches the age of menopause. End. Source pages p. 225

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the matn of Kanz al-Daqaiq regarding the iddah of slave women. The matn states that for a slave woman the iddah is half of that for a free woman: two menstrual cycles if she menstruates, or one and a half months if she does not, and in case of death two months and five days. The commentator explains that this applies to various categories of slave women: the qin (full slave), umm walad, mudabbara, mukataba, and one who is earning (mustas‘a) according to Imam Abu Hanifa. He clarifies conditions such as that consummation is required for the iddah in divorce but not in death. He then discusses the principle that slavery halves certain legal consequences, but not in acts of worship like prayer, fasting, and purity, where slaves and free are equal. He provides the proof: a hadith stating the iddah of a slave woman is two menstruations. He addresses an objection from al-Kafi that this hadith is a khabar wahid and cannot specify the general Qur'anic command (which indicates three periods for all women), answering that the hadith is mashhur (well-known) and accepted by the ummah, or that the verse refers only to free women by context. The commentary also includes subsidiary rulings: a case from Kafi al-Hakim about a woman who claimed her iddah ended and later gave birth, and a case from al-Khaniyya about a woman's claim of pregnancy during death iddah. The commentator also corrects a potential error by noting that 'ila' (vow of abstinence) should be replaced with 'the term of the impotent man' in the list of exceptions. The passage ends with a footnote giving the hadith's sources.

Key Terms
العدة (al-iddah): The waiting period a woman must observe after divorce or death of her husband before she can remarry.
الأمة (al-ammah): A female slave who is legally owned (mamlukah) by another.
حيضتان (haydatān): Two menstrual cycles, meaning two menstruations and two purity periods in between.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the iddah of a slave woman in divorce if she menstruates?
A) One month
B) Two menstrual cycles
C) Three menstrual cycles
D) Four months and ten days

Key Takeaway
The iddah of a slave woman is half that of a free woman, supported by a mashhur hadith, overriding the general implication of the Qur'anic verse for free women.

Talaq — Divorce

Iddah of Pregnant Widows: Hanafi vs Shafi'i
Inter-madhhab dispute on pregnant widow iddah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on the iddah of a pregnant widow and explain why the school holds that delivery alone suffices, using Surah al-Talaq 4 and the principle of abrogation.

Core Idea
For a pregnant widow, Hanafi iddah ends with delivery; Surah al-Talaq 4 abrogates Baqara 234.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq addresses a key dispute over the iddah of a pregnant widow. The commentator presents the evidence from both sides and explains why the Hanafi school follows the later revelation, while the Shafi'i school relies on the hadith of Subay‘a al-Aslamiyya. Understanding this dispute clarifies the practical difference in rulings.

Guided Path
1
State the issue
Identify the contested ruling: does the iddah of a pregnant widow end with delivery alone, or must she also complete four months and ten days?
This is the core of the dispute between Hanafi and Shafi'i.
2
Identify Hanafi position and proof
Find the Hanafi position in the text: the iddah ends with delivery, based on Surah al-Talaq 4, and the testimony of Ibn Mas'ud that this verse is later and abrogates the earlier verse.
Look for the explicit statement: 'للحامل وضعه' and the quote of the verse.
3
Identify Shafi'i position and proof
Find the Shafi'i position: the iddah requires both delivery and four months ten days, based on Surah al-Baqara 234 and the hadith of Subay‘a al-Aslamiyya, as explained by al-Razi.
The text mentions al-Shafi'i's reliance on the sunnah rather than the Quran for this case.
4
Analyze Hanafi response and abrogation argument
Understand the Hanafi rebuttal: the verse of pregnant women (Talaq 4) is later and more specific, thus abrogates the general verse for widows (Baqara 234). This is supported by Ibn Mas'ud's testimony and the practice of many Companions.
The key is the chronology: the 'Qusra' (Surah al-Talaq) was revealed after the 'Tula' (Surah al-Baqara).
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
State the practical consequence: a pregnant widow who delivers before four months can remarry immediately in the Hanafi school, but must wait the remainder of the four months and ten days in the Shafi'i school.
This difference has real implications for the woman's life and marriage.

هاتين الآيتين أعم من الأخرى من وجه وأخص منها من وجه فإن الحامل قد يتوفى عنها زوجها وقد لا يتوفى، والمتوفى عنها زوجها قد تكون حاملاً وقد لا تكون فامتنع أن تكون إحداهما مخصصة للأخرى. الثاني أن قوله تعالى وأولات الأحمال أجلهن إنما ورد بعد ذكر المطلقات فربما كانت في المطلقة فلهذين السببين لم يعول الشافعي رحمه الله على القرآن وإنما عول على السنة وهو حديث سبيعة الأسلمية ا.هـ. وحاصل ما في التلويح The prescribed period, and for the pregnant woman, her placing [the child], and the wife of a fugitive is the longer of the two terms, and whoever is freed during a revocable divorce iddah. His saying: 'For the pregnant woman, her placing [the child]' meaning the iddah of a pregnant woman is the placing of the pregnancy, due to His statement: 'And those with pregnancies, their term is that they deliver their pregnancy' [Talaq 4]. He made it general, including the free woman, Muslim slave woman, Kitabi (People of the Book), whether divorced or separated in a defective marriage, or sexual intercourse with ambiguity, and the one whose husband died, due to the generality of the verse. Ibn Mas'ud said: 'Whoever wants I will appeal to him by oath that Surah al-Nisa al-Qusra was revealed after that which is in al-Baqara,' meaning by al-Qusra 'O Prophet, when you divorce women' [Talaq 1], and by al-Tula 'Those of you who die' [Baqara 234] the verse. It included mutual imprecation. In another narration: 'Whoever wants I will imprecate him,' and in another 'I will take an oath.' When they differed about a matter they said: 'The curse of Allah be upon the liar among us.' They said: This practice is legitimate in our time as in Ghayat al-Bayan and Fath al-Qadir. Umar said: 'If she delivers while her husband is on his bier, her iddah ends and she may marry.' From Ali and Ibn Abbas: 'The pregnant woman whose husband died is [subject to] the longer of the two terms,' meaning both delivery of the pregnancy and the passing of four months and ten days. That is the meaning of 'the longer of the two terms.' In al-Tafsir al-Kabir by Imam al-Razi, al-Shafi'i did not say that the Qusra verse specifies the Tula verse for two reasons: first, each of the two verses is more general from one aspect and more specific from another, as a pregnant woman may (have her husband) die or not, and a woman whose husband dies may be pregnant or not, so one cannot specify the other. Second, the verse 'and those with pregnancies, their term' came after mentioning divorced women, so it might be about the divorced woman. For these two reasons, al-Shafi'i did not rely on the Qur'an but on the sunnah, namely the hadith of Subay'a al-Aslamiyya. End. The summary of what is in al-Talwih: they contradict regarding the pregnant woman whose husband dies. According to the view of Ali, due to ignorance of the chronology, the ruling of contradiction applies to the extent of conflict, so we refer to the sunnah. According to Ibn Mas'ud, who held that the Qusra is later, the Qusra abrogated the Tula in the area of conflict, which is the pregnant widow only. End of al-Talwih here. And it is not as we said regarding the wife of a fugitive. The author of al-Mi'raj erred by interpreting the longer of the two terms reported from Ali as four months and ten days including three menstruations, and quoted it from the Fatawa of Qadi Khan; but this is only in the iddah of the wife of a fugitive, and menstruation has no role in the iddah of a pregnant woman at all. Hence al-Muhit says: from Ali, she observes iddah with the longer of the two terms, which are the months and delivery of the pregnancy. Thus in Fath al-Qadir: due to the generality of the verse. He said: No attention is paid to her harm by that, as in Sharh al-Minhaj al-Ramli and Hashiyat al-Minhaj by Ibn Qasim. Our Shaykh al-Tablawi said: A group of our time gave fatwa that the expiry of her iddah is conditional upon its exit (the fetus). What I say is that it is not conditional when there is no hope of its exit, due to harm from preventing her from marriage. End. Nothing from the principles of our madhhab rejects what they said, so know that. End. Summarizing his statement: 'And it is not its meaning as we said etc.' is connected to his earlier statement: this is the meaning of the longer of the two terms.
Source pages p. 226

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the matn's ruling for pregnant women in iddah: the iddah ends with delivery, based on Surah al-Talaq 4. The commentator notes the verse is general, covering free women, slave women, kitabi women, and those in various marital situations. He then presents the dispute concerning the pregnant widow: does her iddah end with delivery alone, or must she also observe the four months and ten days? Two views are attributed to the Companions: Ibn Mas'ud held that the 'Qusra' verse (Talaq 1-4) abrogated the 'Tula' verse (Baqara 234) for the pregnant widow, so her iddah ends with delivery. Ali and Ibn Abbas held she must observe the longer of the two terms, i.e., both delivery and four months and ten days. The Hanafi school follows Ibn Mas'ud, while al-Shafi'i follows the hadith of Subay‘a al-Aslamiyya (which also indicates delivery alone, but Shafi'i uses it differently). The commentator discusses al-Razi's explanation of why al-Shafi'i did not use the verses, and summarizes from al-Talwih that the two verses conflict; if the chronology is unknown, we refer to the sunnah; if Ibn Mas'ud's view is accepted, the Qusra abrogates. The commentator also corrects a misunderstanding: the 'longer of the two terms' from Ali is not to be confused with the iddah of a fugitive's wife. Finally, he addresses a modern fatwa issue: if the fetus dies in the womb and will not be expelled, the iddah does not expire until it is expelled or menopause, but some scholars argue for leniency to avoid harm.

Key Terms
أبعد الأجلين (aba‘d al-ajalayn): The longer of the two terms: for a pregnant widow, the view of Ali and Ibn Abbas that she must await both delivery and the passage of four months and ten days.
سورة النساء القصرى (sūrat al-nisā' al-qusrā): The shorter Surah of Women, a name used by Ibn Mas'ud for Surah al-Talaq (65), as opposed to the longer Surah al-Baqara.

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) Two menstrual cycles

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that the iddah of a pregnant widow ends with delivery alone, based on the later revelation of Surah al-Talaq 4 abrogating Surah al-Baqara 234 for this case.

The 'Iddah of a Pregnant Widow: Delivery Alone Suffices
Expounds the Hanafi ruling on pregnant woman's 'iddah and refutes opposing views

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the 'iddah of a pregnant widow, cite its Quranic and hadith evidence, and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument using the concept of naskh or takhsis.

Core Idea
Delivery alone ends the 'iddah for any pregnant woman, regardless of divorce or widowhood.

The 'iddah of a pregnant woman is a clear ruling in the Hanafi school: her waiting period ends with delivery, no matter the cause of separation. This lesson examines the primary evidence from Quran 65:4, the supporting hadith of Subay'ah, and the refutation of the Shafi'i position that would require both delivery and four months and ten days for a widow. Understanding this dispute reveals how Hanafi usul prioritizes a later, specific revelation over an earlier general one.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Hanafi ruling and its scope
The passage begins by stating that the 'iddah of a pregnant woman is terminated by delivery (waḍ‘ al-ḥaml). This applies to all pregnant women: free or slave, Muslim or kitabi, divorced or separated by void marriage or dubious intercourse, and widows.
The ruling is based on the absolute wording of Quran 65:4.
2
Locate the primary dalil and its evidentiary strength
The verse 'And those who are pregnant, their term is that they deliver their burden' (65:4) is the key proof. It is qat'i al-thubut and qat'i al-dalalah in the Hanafi view, indicating that delivery is the sole end of the 'iddah.
The Hanafis treat this verse as decisive and later-revealed, thus abrogating or specifying the earlier verse for widows (2:234).
3
Understand the inter-madhhab dispute
The Shafi'i school and Companions Ali and Ibn Abbas hold that a pregnant widow must observe the longer of two terms: delivery or four months and ten days. Their proof is the general verse 2:234 and caution.
The passage explains that this minority view arose from ignorance of the chronological order of revelation.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
The Hanafi response relies on: (1) the later revelation of Surah al-Talaq (65:4) which abrogates/specifies 2:234 for pregnant widows; (2) the hadith of Subay'ah al-Aslamiyyah, where the Prophet allowed her to marry after delivery; (3) the majority of Companions who knew the chronology upheld delivery alone.
The passage also delves into usul al-fiqh: whether the relationship is takhsis (specification) or naskh (abrogation). Hanafis lean toward naskh, but the practical effect is the same.
5
Apply the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence)
The difference is directly practical: Hanafis allow a pregnant widow to remarry immediately after delivery; Shafi'is require her to wait the longer of delivery or 4 months 10 days. In most cases, delivery occurs before 4 months 10 days, so the Shafi'i position delays remarriage significantly.
This underscores the real-world impact of usul differences.
