The predetermined [iddah] and for the pregnant woman, her delivery. That is, the iddah of a pregnant woman is the delivery of the pregnancy, due to His statement: 'And those who are pregnant, their term is that they deliver their burden' [65:4]. He made it general, so it includes the free woman, the slave woman, the Muslim, the Kitabi, whether divorced or separated in a void marriage or by dubious intercourse, and the one whose husband has died, due to the generality of the verse. Ibn Mas'ud (may Allah be pleased with him) said: 'Whoever wishes, I will imprecate him: verily Surah al-Nisa' al-Qusra was revealed after that in al-Baqarah.' He means by al-Qusra 'O Prophet, when you divorce women' [65:1], and by al-Tula 'And those who are taken in death among you' [2:234] the verse, and the mutual imprecation. In one narration: 'Whoever wishes, I will curse him,' and in another 'I will swear him.' They used to say when they disagreed on a matter: 'The curse of Allah be upon the liar among us.' They said: It is legislated in our time, as in Ghayat al-Bayan and Fath al-Qadir. 'Umar (may Allah be pleased with him) said: 'If she delivers while her husband is on his bier, her iddah is terminated and it is lawful for her to marry.' And from 'Ali and Ibn 'Abbas (may Allah be pleased with them) regarding the pregnant woman whose husband died: 'The longer of the two terms,' meaning she must deliver and complete four months and ten days. This is the meaning of 'the longer of the two terms.' In al-Tafsir al-Kabir by Imam al-Razi, that al-Shafi'i did not say that the verse of al-Qusra is a specifier of the verse of al-Tula for two reasons: first, each of these two verses is more general than the other in one aspect and more specific in another; the pregnant woman may have her husband die or not, and the woman whose husband died may be pregnant or not, so it is impossible that one be a specifier of the other. Second, the statement of Allah 'And those who are pregnant, their term' was revealed after mentioning the divorced women, so perhaps it is about the divorced woman. For these two reasons, al-Shafi'i (may Allah have mercy on him) did not rely on the Qur'an but relied on the Sunnah, namely the hadith of Subay'ah al-Aslamiyyah. End. The summary in al-Talwih is that the two are conflicting concerning the pregnant woman whose husband has died. According to the view of 'Ali, due to ignorance of the date, the ruling of conflict is established to the extent of their conflict, so we refer to the Sunnah. According to the view of Ibn Mas'ud who held that al-Qusra is later, al-Qusra abrogates al-Tula in what they conflict on, which is only the pregnant woman whose husband died. End of what is in al-Talwih here. It does not mean what we said regarding the wife of the fugitive. The author of al-Mi'raj erred when he interpreted 'the longer of the two terms' reported from 'Ali (may Allah be pleased with him) as four months and ten days including three menstrual cycles, and he transmitted it from Fatawa Qadikhan. This is only in the iddah of the wife of the fugitive, and menstruation has no place in the iddah of a pregnant woman at all. Hence al-Muhit said: From 'Ali, she observes iddah by the longer of the two terms, which are the months and delivery. Similarly in Fath al-Qadir. They only said that due to their ignorance of the date, so that was more cautious. The majority of the Companions (may Allah be pleased with them) when they knew the date, said it is by delivery because its verse is later. Al-Qadi said in his Tafsir: This is the ruling for divorced women and those whose husbands died, and preserving the generality of this is more appropriate than preserving the generality of His statement 'And those who are taken in death among you and leave behind wives' [2:234] because 'those who are pregnant' is essential and the generality of 'wives' is incidental, and the ruling is reasoned here otherwise. Then, because it is authentic that Subay'ah bint al-Harith delivered a few nights after her husband's death and mentioned that to the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) and he said: 'You have become lawful, so marry.' And because it is later in revelation, so giving it precedence is specification, and giving precedence to the other is building the general upon the specific, and the first is preponderant due to agreement upon it. End. In al-Durr al-Manthur from Ibn Mas'ud (may Allah be pleased with him) as a marfu' report: 'Surah al-Nisa' al-Qusra abrogated every iddah, and those who are pregnant, their term is that they deliver their burden, whether divorced or whose husband died.' And it is reported from Abu Sa'id al-Khudri (may Allah be pleased with him) that it was revealed seven years later. And it is transmitted from Ubayy ibn Ka'b, Abu Sa'id al-Khudri, 'Umar and his son, Abu Hurayrah, 'A'ishah, and al-Musawwar ibn Makhramah (may Allah be pleased with them). His statement: 'They only said that' meaning 'Ali and Ibn 'Abbas as previously reported from them. His statement: 'So giving it precedence in practice is specification' meaning giving precedence to His statement 'And those who are pregnant' over His statement 'And those who are taken in death among you' [2:234] and preferring to act by it to preserve its generality, and leaving action by this in what they cover would be building the general upon the specific. If we gave this verse precedence in practice and preserved its generality, that would be a specification of the generality of the other verse, because this verse is specific in one aspect just as that is specific in another. So acting by this later verse in the amount they cover, namely the pregnant woman whose husband died, would be a specification of it to beyond the pregnant woman whose husband died, and the later specific specifies the earlier general. This is according to the author's madhhab in the permissibility of a delayed specifier. According to the Hanafis, it is abrogation, not specification, and it is not carrying the general upon the specific that is not connected. Details are in the detailed works of usul. So his saying 'due to agreement upon it' has some consideration that is repelled by reflection, because his intent is agreement on acting by the later, whether we say it is a specifier or an abrogator, and there is no need for metaphor in specification as was said. It is supported, as in Sharh al-Tahrir, by what is in al-Bukhari from Ibn al-Zubayr that he said to 'Uthman (may Allah be pleased with them both): 'And those who are taken in death...' its sister verse abrogated it, so should we write it or leave it? He said: 'O son of my brother, I will not change anything from its place.' In it is 'Uthman's admission of abrogation and the precedence of the abrogating over the abrogated in the arrangement of verses is from the rare matters, so reflect. His statement 'building the general upon the specific' means if this is given precedence by acting by it, there would be specification of his saying 'wives' in that to non-pregnant women, and giving precedence to that in acting by it would necessitate building the general, which is 'and those who are pregnant' including divorced women and those whose husband died, upon the specific, which is the one whose husband died. The meaning of 'building' as some virtuous people said here is that the general is intended to mean the specific without a specifier, because the earlier cannot be a specifier for the later, and 'building' in this sense we have not seen from others, so it needs editing. So in Hashiyat al-Khafaji 'ala al-Baydawi.
Source pages p. 226

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute concerning the 'iddah of a pregnant woman, specifically whether her 'iddah ends with delivery alone or requires the longer of delivery and four months and ten days when she is widowed. The Hanafi position is clear: the 'iddah of any pregnant woman, whether divorced or widowed, ends with delivery. This is based on the absolute wording of Quran 65:4, 'And those who are pregnant, their term is that they deliver their burden.' The verse is general and covers all pregnant women regardless of the reason for separation. The author then cites Ibn Mas'ud who affirms that Surah al-Talaq (al-Qusra) was revealed later than the verse of 'iddah for widows in Surah al-Baqarah (2:234), thus abrogating it with respect to pregnant widows. The companions 'Umar and the majority held that delivery suffices. However, a minority view from 'Ali and Ibn 'Abbas required the longer of the two terms (delivery or four months and ten days) for a pregnant widow. The author explains that they held this only because they did not know the chronological order of revelation; once the later revelation was known, they would have agreed with the majority. The Shafi'i position is then discussed: Imam al-Shafi'i did not accept that 65:4 specifies 2:234 because each verse is general/specific in different aspects, and he relied on the hadith of Subay'ah al-Aslamiyyah, where the Prophet (peace be upon him) allowed her to marry after delivery. The Hanafi response is that the hadith actually supports the Hanafi view, and the later revelation (65:4) abrogates or specifies the earlier (2:234) for pregnant widows. The author delves into usul al-fiqh arguments about whether the relationship is specification (takhsis) or abrogation (naskh), ultimately upholding the Hanafi position that delivery ends the 'iddah.

Key Terms
العدة (al-‘iddah): The waiting period a woman must observe after separation from her husband due to divorce or death, before she can remarry.
الحامل (al-hāmil): The pregnant woman whose 'iddah ends with delivery.
الوضع (al-waḍ‘): Delivery of the child, which terminates the 'iddah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the primary evidence that the 'iddah of a pregnant widow ends with delivery alone?
A) The consensus (ijma) of the Companions that delivery ends the 'iddah.
B) The absolute wording of Quran 65:4, which does not mention any additional period.
C) The hadith of Subay'ah, which mentions a four-month waiting period.
D) The analogy (qiyas) between a widow and a divorced woman.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, the 'iddah of any pregnant woman ends with delivery, based on the clear text of Quran 65:4 and the Prophet's permission to Subay'ah.

Talaq — Divorce
intermediate

The 'Iddah of a Pregnant Widow: Hanafi Position
Debate on whether Surah al-Talaq specifies or abrogates Surah al-Baqarah for pregnant widows

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the 'iddah of a pregnant widow, cite the primary evidence, and explain the usul argument for specification over abrogation.

Core Idea
A pregnant widow's 'iddah ends with delivery, not four months and ten days.

This passage addresses a classical dispute: does a pregnant widow observe the general 'iddah of four months and ten days, or does her 'iddah end upon delivery? The Hanafi school, relying on Surah al-Talaq and the practice of the Companions, holds that delivery terminates the 'iddah immediately. The discussion also delves into usul al-fiqh, weighing whether the later verse specifies or abrogates the earlier one. Understanding this ruling clarifies an important family law scenario.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Two Positions
First, recognize that the Hanafi school says the 'iddah of a pregnant widow ends with delivery. The opposing view (attributed to 'Ali and Ibn 'Abbas) says she must wait the longer of four months and ten days or delivery.
The opposing view was based on caution due to uncertainty about the chronology of revelation.
2
Examine the Hanafi Evidence
The Hanafis rely on Quran 65:4 ('Ulatu al-ahmali ajaluhunna an yada'na hamlahunna') and the hadith of Subay'ah, where the Prophet told her she was permissible to marry after delivery. These texts are explicit and later in revelation.
The hadith is zanni al-thubut but qati' al-dalala.
3
Analyze the Usul Argument: Takhsis vs Naskh
Al-Qadi argues that the verse about pregnant women (65:4) is general by essence (bi al-dhat) while the widow verse (2:234) is general by accident (bi al-'arad). The later verse either specifies (takhsis) or abrogates (naskh) the earlier one for pregnant women. The Hanafis prefer takhsis because it preserves both texts.
Specification is more appropriate than abrogation when possible.
4
Consider the Practical Consequence (Thamarah al-Khilaf)
Under the Hanafi view, a pregnant widow may remarry immediately after delivery, even if less than four months have passed. Under the opposing view, she must wait until the longer period elapses.
This directly affects the woman's ability to remarry and resume normal life.
5
Review the Majority Companion Consensus
The passage notes that the majority of Companions, including 'Umar, Ibn Mas'ud, Abu Hurayrah, and 'A'ishah, held that delivery ends the 'iddah. This ijma' supports the Hanafi position.
The later revelation of Surah al-Talaq was known to them.

أولى من المحافظة على عموم قوله تعالى {والذين يتوفون منكم ويذرون أزواجاً} [البقرة: ٢٣٤] لأن «أولات الأحمال» بالذات وعموم أزواجاً بالعرض والحكم يتعلل ها هنا بخلافه . ثم ولأنه صح أن سبيعة بنت الحارث وضعت بعد وفاة زوجها بليال فذكرت ذلك لرسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم فقال : قد حللت فتزوجي. ولأنه متأخر النزول فتقديمه تخصيص وتقديم الآخر بناء العام على الخاص والأول أرجح للوفاق عليه ا هـ . وفي الدر المنثور أبي بن كعب وأبي سعيد الخدري رضي الله عنه وعمر وابنه وأبي هريرة وعائشة والمسور بن محزمة رضي الله عنهم كقول ابن مسعود ومعنى قول القاضي إن عموم أولات بالذات أن الموصول من صيغ العموم ، ومعنى قوله إن عموم أزواجاً بالعرض أن عمومه بدلي لا يصلح لتناول جميع الأزواج في حال واحد، ومعنى قوله إن الحكم يتعلل هنا أن الحكم هنا معلل بوصف الحملية بخلاف ذلك، وقوله والأول أرجح أي التخصيص أولى من النسخ لأنا إذا أخرنا آية الحمل عن آية الوفاة كانت مخصصة لآية الوفاة، وإذا قدمنا آية الحمل على آية الوفاة كانت رافعة لما في الخاص من الحكم وهو نسخ. وفي المعراج حمل أهل العلم آية البقرة على الحوامل تخصيصاً بآية القصرى والتخصيص أولى من دعوى النسخ ا هـ. وفي البدائع : إن كان بين نزول الآيتين زمان يصلح للنسخ فينسخ الخاص المتقدم بالعام المتأخر كما هو مذهب مشايخنا بالعراق ولا يبنى العام على الخاص أو يعمل بالنص العام على عمومه ويتوقف في حق الاعتقاد كما هو مذهب مشايخ سمرقند ولا يبنى العام على الخاص ا هـ. وذكر البقاعي في المناسبات : لما كان توحيد الحمل لا ينشأ عنه لبس وكان الجمع ربما أوهم أنها لا تحل واحدة منهما حتى تضع جمعاً قال حملهن ا هـ. وذكر الفخر الرازي أنه قرىء «أحمالهن» ثم قال «أن يضعن حملهن ولم يقل أن يلدن لأنه لو قاله لانقضت بولادة أحد الولدين ا هـ. يعني وهو بعض الحمل فلا تنقضي حتى تضع جميع ما في البطن لأن الحمل اسم لجميع ما في البطن ولهذا قال الأصوليون: لو قال إن كان حملك ذكراً فأنت حرة فولت ذكراً وأنثى لا تعتق لأنه اسم لجميع ما في البطن كقوله إن كان ما في بطنك ذكر. وفي البدائع : وشرط
And they only said that due to not knowing the date, so that was more precautionary. The majority of the Companions, may Allah be pleased with them, when they knew the date, said (the 'iddah ends) with delivery because its verse was later. Al-Qadi said in his Tafsir: This is the ruling for divorced women and those whose husbands die, and preserving its generality is more appropriate than preserving the generality of His statement, the Exalted, 'And those who die among you and leave behind wives' [al-Baqarah 2:234], because 'Ulatu al-ahmal' is general by essence and 'azwajan' is general by accident, and the ruling here is motivated by something else. Then, because it is authentically reported that Subay'ah bint al-Harith gave birth a few nights after her husband's death and mentioned that to the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him), and he said: 'You have become permissible, so marry.' And because it is later in revelation, giving it precedence is specification (takhsis), and giving precedence to the other is building the general upon the specific, and the first is more preponderant due to agreement upon it, end. In al-Durr al-Manthur, from Ibn Mas'ud (may Allah be pleased with him) as a marfu' hadith: 'Surat al-Nisa' al-Qusra abrogated every 'iddah, and the 'iddah of every pregnant woman—divorced or widowed—is until she delivers her child.' And it is narrated from Abu Sa'id al-Khudri (may Allah be pleased with him) that it was revealed after seven years. And it is narrated from Ubayy ibn Ka'b, Abu Sa'id al-Khudri, 'Umar and his son, Abu Hurayrah, 'A'ishah, and al-Miswar ibn Makhramah (may Allah be pleased with them all) as the saying of Ibn Mas'ud. The meaning of al-Qadi's statement that the generality of 'Ulatu al-ahmal' is essential is that the relative pronoun is among the forms of generality, and his statement that the generality of 'azwajan' is accidental is that its generality is substitutive (badali), not suitable to encompass all wives at one time. His statement that the ruling is motivated here means the ruling is motivated by the attribute of pregnancy, unlike the other. His statement 'the first is more preponderant' means specification is more appropriate than abrogation because if we postpone the verse of pregnancy after the verse of death, it would be a specifier for the verse of death; if we give precedence to the verse of pregnancy over the verse of death, it would remove the ruling of the specific, which is abrogation. In al-Mi'raj: The scholars applied the verse of al-Baqarah to pregnant women as a specification by the verse of al-Qusra, and specification is more appropriate than claiming abrogation, end. In al-Bada'i': If there is a time between the revelation of the two verses sufficient for abrogation, then the earlier specific is abrogated by the later general, as is the madhhab of our scholars in Iraq, and the general is not built upon the specific; or one acts upon the general text in its generality and suspends judgment in belief, as is the madhhab of the scholars of Samarqand, and the general is not built upon the specific, end. Al-Biqa'i mentioned in al-Munasabat: Since the singular of 'haml' does not lead to confusion, while the plural might give the impression that neither is permissible until they deliver both, He said 'hamlahunn' (their burden), end. Fakhr al-Razi mentioned that it was recited 'ahmalahunn', then He said 'an yada'na hamlahunn' and did not say 'that they give birth', because if He said so, it would end with the birth of one of the two children, end. Meaning, (the birth of) part of the pregnancy does not end it until she delivers all that is in the womb, because 'haml' is a term for all that is in the womb. For this reason, the usulis said: If he says, 'If your child is male, you are free,' and she gives birth to a male and a female, she is not free because it is a term for all that is in the womb, like saying 'If what is in your womb is male.' And in al-Bada'i': And the condition...
Source pages p. 227

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute concerning the 'iddah of a pregnant widow. The Hanafi position, based on Surah al-Talaq (65:4) and the hadith of Subay'ah bint al-Harith, holds that her 'iddah ends with delivery. The opposing view, attributed to Imam 'Ali and Ibn 'Abbas, states that she must observe the longer of two periods—four months and ten days or delivery—out of precaution, due to their uncertainty about which verse was revealed later. The majority of Companions, however, accepted the later verse as abrogating or specifying the earlier one. The discussion then delves into usul al-fiqh: whether the verse 'Ulatu al-ahmal' specifies the general verse in al-Baqarah or abrogates it. Imam al-Qadi argues that since the verse about pregnant women is specific in scope (it addresses all pregnant women, making its generality 'essential') while the verse about widows is general but 'accidental' in its application, the later specific verse should be applied to pregnant widows, overriding the earlier general verse for that subset. The Hanafis also rely on the hadith of Subay'ah, where the Prophet (peace be upon him) told her she was free to marry after delivery, and on reports that Surah al-Talaq was revealed later than the Baqarah verse. Later scholars like al-Biqa'i and Fakhr al-Razi clarify that the verse uses the word 'hamlahunn' (their burden) in the singular to avoid confusion that the 'iddah ends only after delivering all children in a multiple pregnancy.

Key Terms
أولات الأحمال (ulatu al-ahmal): A Quranic phrase meaning 'those possessing burdens/pregnancies' referring to pregnant women.
تخصيص (takhsis): Specification of a general text by a later specific text without abrogation.
عدة ('iddah): The waiting period a woman must observe after divorce or death of her husband before remarrying.
نسخ (naskh): Abrogation of a legal ruling by a later revelation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, when does the 'iddah of a pregnant widow end?
A) After four months and ten days, regardless of delivery
B) When she delivers her child, even if before four months
C) The longer of four months and ten days or delivery
D) One year after the husband's death

Key Takeaway
A pregnant widow's 'iddah ends upon delivery, as established by the later revealed Quranic verse and the practice of the Prophet and the majority of Companions.

Preferring Specification over Abrogation: The Pregnant Widow's 'Iddah
Discussion of preferring specification over abrogation for the two 'iddah verses

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the preferred Hanafi position on the 'iddah of a pregnant widow and explain why takhsis is preferred over naskh in this case.

Core Idea
Specification preserves both verses; abrogation discards one – takhsis is preferred.

In this lesson, we examine a key intra-Hanafi dispute regarding two Quranic verses on 'iddah: one for pregnant women (al-Baqarah 2:228) and one for widows (al-Baqarah 2:234). The apparent conflict is resolved by preferring specification (takhsis) over abrogation (naskh), as the preferred Hanafi view holds that the pregnancy verse specifies the widow's verse for pregnant women, allowing both verses to operate.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the preferred ruling: the pregnancy verse (2:228) specifies the widow's verse (2:234). So a pregnant widow's 'iddah ends with delivery.
2
Identify the opposing views within the Hanafi school
Note that some Hanafi scholars (Iraqi mashayikh) allow naskh if there is sufficient time between revelations. Scholars of Samarqand act on the general text and withhold in belief.
This is an internal ikhtilaf, not between madhahib.
3
Follow the opponents' proofs
The Iraqi scholars argue that if time allows for naskh, the earlier specific is abrogated by the later general. Samarqandis: the general is not built upon the specific.
Both views end up not applying the pregnancy verse to widows.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
The preferred view (al-rajih) is specification because it preserves both texts. Additionally, the ruling in the pregnancy verse has an effective cause (clearing the womb), making it rationally intelligible, whereas the widow's 'iddah is devotional. Therefore, the cause-based ruling should take precedence.
The text states 'al-awwal arjah' (the first is more preponderant).
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: under specification, a pregnant widow's 'iddah ends with delivery; under naskh, it ends after four months and ten days, regardless of delivery.
If she delivers before four months, the difference is stark.

And I say: The judge began by saying the subject is omitted, and the estimation is 'And the husbands of those who die among you', and it is clear that the definite plural by idafa is general, but its generality is also accidental due to the idafa. However, it remains to be said that the object of the ruling is 'dhat' (persons), and the claim that the generality is from the definite plural is prevented; rather, it is from the idafa of 'ulaatu' to it. Thus, it equals the verse of death with the aforementioned estimation. This difficulty I have not seen anyone address, and it is strong and needs an answer. The truth is that the judge's speech proceeds here on the basis that 'alladhina' (those who) is the subject, and the predicate is either 'yatarabbasna' (they wait) or omitted, meaning 'the ruling of those who is recited to you', so ponder it. And the ruling has an effective cause in that the woman in waiting has a pregnancy, so the clearing of the womb from the right of another is suitable to permit marriage to another, and this is rationally intelligible, unlike the other verse where it is not rationally intelligible that the fact that the woman's husband died has an effect on her waiting for four months and ten days; rather, it is devotional. And the cause-based ruling is stronger than others, but we have already quoted from the judge something indicating that it is not rationally intelligible either, except that it is a wisdom, not a cause. And when this is understood, what is in al-Bahr that the meaning of the generality of 'ulaatu' being essential and 'azwajan' accidental because the relative pronoun is from the forms of generality and the generality of 'azwajan' is substitutive, appears to me to be an error that became famous from the fact that 'ulaatu' is not a relative pronoun but a plural noun annexed to the sound feminine plural. He claims that.
Source pages p. 228

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the interpretation of two Quranic verses on 'iddah (waiting period): one for pregnant women (Quran 2:228) and one for widows (Quran 2:234). The issue is whether the widow's verse abrogates the pregnancy verse for widows or the pregnancy verse specifies the widow's verse. The preferred Hanafi view, as stated by the judge (al-Qadi), is specification (takhsis) over abrogation (naskh), because specification allows both verses to be applied—the pregnancy verse to pregnant widows and the widow's verse to non-pregnant widows. The passage also notes a difference among Hanafi scholars: the Iraqi mashayikh hold that if there was sufficient time between revelations for abrogation, the earlier specific ruling is abrogated by the later general; the Samarqandi mashayikh hold that the general is not built upon the specific and one acts on the general text while suspending judgment in belief. The passage then discusses linguistic points about the words 'ulaatu' and 'azwajan' and the meaning of 'haml' (pregnancy) as covering all contents of the womb. This is a technical usul al-fiqh discussion within a fiqh commentary, demonstrating how Hanafi scholars prioritize harmonizing verses through specification rather than abrogation when possible.

Key Terms
تخصيص (takhsis): Specification: restricting a general text to apply only to some of its potential referents, as opposed to abrogation.
نسخ (naskh): Abrogation: the complete removal of a previous ruling by a later revelation.
عام (aam): A general term that covers multiple individuals or types, such as 'azwaj' (husbands) in the verse.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) The widow's verse abrogates the pregnancy verse for widows.
B) The pregnancy verse specifies the widow's verse for pregnant women.
C) Both verses are considered abrogated by a third verse.
D) The pregnancy verse is general and the widow's verse is specific.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school prefers specification over abrogation when reconciling apparently conflicting texts, preserving the applicability of both.

The Condition of 'Iddah for a Pregnant Woman: Pregnancy Must Be from a Valid Marriage
Ruling that 'iddah is obligatory only if pregnancy is from a valid marriage, not from zina

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the condition for obligatory 'iddah for a pregnant woman, apply it to cases of pregnancy from zina, and distinguish the Hanafi position from other views.

Core Idea
A pregnant woman's 'iddah is only wajib if the pregnancy is from a valid marriage; zina does not create 'iddah.

This lesson examines a foundational Hanafi ruling: the waiting period ('iddah) is not required for a pregnancy resulting from zina. Only pregnancies from a valid marriage (sahih or fasid) trigger the obligation. The passage from al-Bada'i' and subsequent texts explore the implications, including cases of marriage during pregnancy, multiple births, stillbirths, and the famous case of the missing husband.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core condition for 'iddah obligation
Read the opening sentence from al-Bada'i': 'The condition for its obligation is that the pregnancy be from a marriage, whether valid or void.' State clearly that the source of pregnancy must be a marital relationship.
The term 'marriage' includes both sahih (valid) and fasid (void) marriages, but not zina.
2
Apply the condition to pregnancy from zina
The text explicitly says 'iddah is not obligatory for a woman pregnant from zina. Understand that zina does not create any marital bond, so no waiting period is needed. However, the marriage to such a woman is permissible.
This is a key distinction from schools that might require 'iddah for any pregnancy.
3
Note the ruling on intercourse with a pregnant-from-zina wife
According to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, the husband cannot have intercourse with his wife until she delivers, to avoid mixing lineages ('watering the crop of another'). Recognize this as a preventive measure.
This ruling applies even though the marriage is valid.
4
Understand how 'iddah ends for a divorced pregnant-from-zina woman
If she marries and is divorced, her 'iddah ends with delivery according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad. Note the term 'pregnancy' covers all contents of the womb; full delivery of all fetuses is required.
This is an intra-Hanafi view; the later fatwa follows this position.

And in al-Bada'i': The condition for its obligation is that the pregnancy be from a marriage, whether valid (sahih) or void (fasid), and it is not obligatory upon the woman pregnant from zina, because zina does not necessitate 'iddah. However, if a man marries a woman who is pregnant from zina, the marriage is permissible. And in Fath al-Qadir: If she marries after the months then gives birth to a child less than six months from the duration, the invalidity of the marriage appears and the child is attributed to the deceased, end. According to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, he is not permitted to have intercourse with her until she delivers, lest he be watering the crop of another. So it appears that the pregnant woman from zina has no 'iddah at all. As for the woman penetrated by shubha (doubtful intercourse), her 'iddah is by menstruations as will come, unless she is pregnant, in which case her 'iddah is by delivery of the pregnancy, as in the case of the pregnant woman from zina who then married and was divorced, then gave birth: her 'iddah ends by delivery according to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad). And in al-Bada'i': The 'iddah may end by delivery of the pregnancy from zina if the pregnant woman from zina marries, then he divorces her, and she gives birth; her 'iddah ends by delivery according to them. If she gives birth and another (fetus) remains in her womb, the 'iddah ends with the delivery of the other, because 'pregnancy' is a term for all that is in the womb. If she miscarries a stillbirth whose some limbs are formed, the 'iddah ends with it because it is a child; if some limbs are not formed, it does not end, because 'pregnancy' is a term for a changed sperm, evidenced by the fact that if the miscarried item is a clot or a lump of flesh, the 'iddah does not end with it because it has not changed, and its being changed is not known with certainty except by the formation of some limbs; as in al-Muhit. And in al-Tatarkhaniyya, he said: If he says, 'If you give birth to a child, you are divorced,' and she gives birth to a child, then she gives birth after six months, the lineage of the second is established, and the 'iddah ends with it, and no compensation is due. And in al-Kafi of al-Hakim: He said to her, 'Whenever you give birth to a child, you are divorced,' and she gives birth to twins in one pregnancy: she is divorced with the first, and the 'iddah ends with the other, and no divorce occurs with it. If she gives birth to triplets, two divorces occur and the 'iddah ends with the third. If there were six months between the two births and she did not acknowledge the end of the 'iddah, she is divorced thrice and observes 'iddah by menstruations after the third. And in al-Khaniyya: He divorced her revocably, then she married during the 'iddah, then the second divorced her, and she gave birth to a child more than two years from the first divorce and six months from the second divorce: then the child belongs to the second. And if she married the one whose husband was announced dead, then gave birth to children, then the first husband returned alive, Imam Abu Hanifa used to say: the children belong to the first; then he retracted and said: to the second, and upon that is the fatwa, end. Selected. Muhammad said in Nawadir ibn Rustam: If half the body emerges from the head side without the head, or half the body emerges from the feet side without the feet, the 'iddah ends with it; and he explained: the half is from the buttocks to the shoulders, and it is not considered from the body what is with the head nor with the feet. And he said in al-Haruniyyat: If most of the child emerges, the revocation is not valid and she becomes permissible for husbands. And our mashayikh said: She is not permissible for husbands either, because it has taken the place of the whole in terms of ending the revocation. His saying: (And in al-Mi'raj, the scholars interpreted the verse of al-Baqarah as referring to pregnant women. This appears in the copies as 'al-hawamil' with a mim, and the correct form is 'al-hawa'il' with a hamz, as the expression of al-Mi'raj: The scholars interpreted the verse of Surah al-Baqarah as referring to non-pregnant women (al-hawa'il) and the verse of the short Surah al-Nisa' as referring to pregnant women (al-hawamil), and specification is better than claiming abrogation.

Commentary
This passage presents a clear ruling from al-Bada'i' that 'iddah (waiting period) is only obligatory for a pregnant woman if the pregnancy originates from a valid marriage (sahih) or a void marriage (fasid). If the pregnancy is from zina (fornication), no 'iddah is required because zina does not create marital bonds. This ruling is consistently applied across multiple scenarios. The text then provides various cases: if a woman pregnant from zina marries, the marriage is valid but the husband cannot have intercourse until she delivers, to avoid mixing lineages. If she then divorces, her 'iddah ends upon delivery (according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad). The passage also discusses the termination of 'iddah with birth, including cases of multiple births, stillbirths, and partial delivery of the body. For a stillbirth, the 'iddah ends only if some limbs are formed; a mere clot or lump does not suffice. In the case of a missing husband (mafqud), if the wife marries again and then the first husband returns, the later fatwa attributes the children to the second husband. The passage ends with a textual correction regarding the word 'hawamil' vs. 'hawa'il' in a previous quote, affirming that specification (takhsis) is preferred over naskh.

Key Terms
عدة (iddah): The waiting period a woman must observe after divorce or death of husband before remarrying.
حمل (haml): Pregnancy; the term includes all contents of the womb; 'iddah ends only with complete delivery of all.
زنا (zina): Unlawful sexual intercourse; it does not create marital bonds and does not necessitate 'iddah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A woman becomes pregnant from zina and later marries a man. According to the Hanafi rulings in this passage, does she need to observe 'iddah for the zina pregnancy?
A) Yes, she must wait until delivery.
B) No, because zina does not create 'iddah.
C) Yes, but only if she was previously married.
D) No, but she must observe 'iddah of four months and ten days.

Key Takeaway
The obligation of 'iddah for a pregnant woman depends entirely on whether the pregnancy originates from a valid marriage; zina does not trigger 'iddah.

Ruling on Iddah for a Pregnant Woman: Marriage vs. Zina
Ruling on iddah for pregnant woman from valid/fasid marriage and from zina

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on iddah for a pregnant woman from a valid/invalid marriage and from zina, and apply the condition that prohibits intercourse until delivery in the case of zina.

Core Idea
Pregnancy from valid marriage triggers iddah; pregnancy from zina does not, but the new husband may not have intercourse until delivery.

This passage from Bahr al-Ra'iq clarifies the waiting period (iddah) for a pregnant woman. The key distinction is whether the pregnancy stems from a valid or invalid marriage (which obligates iddah) or from zina (which does not). The text also addresses the validity of marrying a woman pregnant from zina and the prohibition of marital relations until she gives birth to avoid lineage confusion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Determine what act is being ruled upon (iddah) and the hukm level for each scenario: pregnancy from marriage vs. pregnancy from zina.
The hukm for iddah from marriage is wajib; from zina it is not obligatory.
2
Locate the dalil
Although the passage does not explicitly cite a verse, the ruling is based on the Quranic principle that iddah ends with delivery (Surah al-Talaq 65:4). Zina does not create marital ties, so no iddah arises.
The dalil is implicit; the Hanafi school relies on the absence of a marital bond.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The verse 'and for those who are pregnant, their term is until they deliver' indicates that delivery ends the iddah only when pregnancy results from a valid sexual relationship (marriage). Zina does not fall under this ruling.
The cause (illah) is the existence of a valid or invalid marriage.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Other schools (e.g., Shafi'i) may require iddah for zina to protect lineage, but the Hanafi school distinguishes based on the absence of a marital contract.
The cross-madhhab status is mukhtalaf fih.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih
The Hanafi position is mufta bihi; the prohibition of intercourse until delivery is agreed upon by Abu Hanifa and Muhammad.
No intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf is mentioned.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وجوبها أن يكون الحمل من نكاح صحيحاً كان أو فاسداً ولا تجب على الحامل من الزنا لأن الزنا لا يوجب العدة إلا أنه إذا تزوج امرأة وهي حامل من الزنا جاز النكاح. وفي فتح القدير : لو تزوجت بعد الأشهر ثم جاءت بولد لأقل من ستة أشهر من المدة ظهر فساد النكاح وألحق بالميت ا.هـ. فعند أبي حنيفة ومحمد لا يجوز له أن يطأها ما لم تضع كيلا يكون ساقياً ماء زرع غيره فظهر أن الحامل من الزنا لا عدة عليها أصلاً
Its obligation (the iddah) is that the pregnancy be from a marriage, whether valid or invalid, and it is not obligatory for the pregnant woman from zina because zina does not necessitate iddah. However, if a man marries a woman who is pregnant from zina, the marriage is valid. In Fath al-Qadeer: If she marries after the months and then comes with a child less than six months from that period, the marriage is invalidated and the child is attributed to the deceased (i.e., the first husband). According to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, he may not have intercourse with her until she gives birth, lest he be watering another's crop. So it becomes clear that the pregnant woman from zina has no iddah at all.
Source pages p. 229

Commentary
This passage addresses the ruling on the waiting period (iddah) for a pregnant woman. The general rule is that iddah is obligatory for a woman who is pregnant from a valid or invalid (fasid) marriage. However, if the pregnancy is from zina (adultery), no iddah is required because zina does not create any marital rights or obligations. Nevertheless, if a man marries a woman who is already pregnant from zina, the marriage is valid. A quotation from Fath al-Qadeer adds that if the marriage occurs after the months (i.e., after the iddah of the first husband would have ended if she were not pregnant) and she gives birth less than six months from the marriage, then the marriage is invalidated because the child cannot be from the new husband, and it is attributed to the first husband. According to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, the new husband is prohibited from having intercourse with his wife until she delivers, to avoid mixing lineages. Thus, the pregnant woman from zina has no iddah at all.

Key Terms
العدة (al-iddah): The waiting period after divorce or death of husband during which a woman may not remarry.
الحمل من الزنا (al-haml min al-zina): Pregnancy resulting from adultery or fornication, not from a valid or invalid marriage.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The marriage is invalid because she is pregnant from zina.
B) She must observe an iddah of three months before the marriage.
C) The marriage is valid, but the husband cannot have intercourse until she delivers.
D) The child is attributed to the new husband regardless of timing.

Key Takeaway
Pregnancy from valid marriage obligates iddah; pregnancy from zina does not, but the new husband cannot have intercourse until delivery.

Iddah of a Woman from Dubious Intercourse and Multiple Fetuses
Ruling on iddah for woman from shubha and effect of delivering multiple children

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on iddah for a woman from shubha and explain when a stillbirth ends iddah.

Core Idea
For a woman in iddah from dubious intercourse, pregnancy ends only with delivery of a child showing discernible human features.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq clarifies the Hanafi position on iddah after dubious intercourse (shubha). While the baseline iddah is by menstrual cycles, pregnancy changes it to delivery. Critical is the distinction between a stillbirth that is a 'child' (with discernible parts) and one that is not—only the former ends the iddah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is iddah for a woman from dubious intercourse. The ruling: it is wajib, either by three menstrual cycles (aqra') or by delivery if pregnant.
The baseline is aqra'; pregnancy overrides.
2
Locate the dalil
The passage does not cite Quran or Hadith explicitly but uses rational reasoning: the term 'haml' denotes all contents of the womb, and a miscarried fetus ends iddah only if it shows discernible creation (istabana ba'd khalqihi).
This is aqli reasoning based on definition of child.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The proof: iddah ends with delivery of a child (walad). A stillbirth with discernible parts is a child; without, it is not. This is deduced from the criterion of recognizable humanity.
Certainty requires visible signs.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Within Hanafi school, the text says 'cindahuma' (according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad), implying agreement; but other schools may have different criteria.
This is an intra-Hanafi agreement but cross-madhhab difference.
5
Evaluate the tarjih
The relied-upon view is from al-Muhit: iddah ends only with delivery of a distinguishable stillbirth.
This is the mufta bihi position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الموطوءة بشبهة فعدتها بالاقراء كما سيأتي إلا إذا كانت حاملاً فعدتها بوضع الحمل كما في تزوج الحامل التي من الزنا ثم طلقها فولدت انقضت عدتها عندهما بالوضع. وفي البدائع : وقد تنقضي العدة بوضع الحمل من الزنا بأن تزوجت الحامل من الزنا ثم طلقها فولدت انقضت عدتها عندهما بالوضع. ولدت وفي بطنها آخر تنقضي العدة بوضع الآخر لأن الحمل اسم لجميع ما في البطن، وإذا أسقطت سقطاً استبان بعض خلقه انقضت به العدة لأنه ولد، وإن لم يستبن بعض خلقه لم تنقض لأن الحمل اسم لنطفة متغيرة بدليل أن الساقط إذا كان علقة أو مضغة لم تنقض به العدة لأنها لم تتغير فلا يعرف كونها متغيرة بيقين إلا باستبانة بعض الخلق؛ كذا في المحيط.
As for the woman who is subjected to dubious intercourse (shubha), her iddah is by menstrual cycles, as will come, unless she is pregnant, then her iddah is by delivery of the child, as in the case of marrying a woman pregnant from zina and then divorcing her; she gives birth and her iddah ends by delivery according to them (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad). In al-Bada'i: The iddah may end by delivery of a pregnancy from zina when a woman pregnant from zina is married and then divorced, she gives birth and her iddah ends by delivery according to them. If she gives birth and there is another (fetus) in her womb, the iddah ends by delivery of the last, because 'pregnancy' (haml) denotes all that is in the womb. If she miscarries a stillbirth (sikt) in which some of its creation is discernible (istabana ba'd khalqihi), the iddah ends with it because it is a child. But if some of its creation is not discernible, it does not end because 'pregnancy' denotes a changed sperm-drop. The evidence is that if the miscarried thing is a clot (alaqah) or a lump (mudgha), the iddah does not end with it because it has not changed, and it is not known with certainty that it is changed except by discernibility of some creation. This is in al-Muhit.
Source pages p. 229

Commentary
This passage discusses the iddah for a woman who has engaged in intercourse under a dubious contract (shubha). In general, her iddah is by three menstrual cycles (aqra'), but if she is pregnant, her iddah ends with delivery, as in the case of a woman who was pregnant from zina, then married, then divorced: according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, her iddah ends when she gives birth. The passage further addresses the case of multiple fetuses: if she gives birth to one and there is another in her womb, the iddah ends only with the delivery of the last one, because the term 'haml' (pregnancy) refers to all the contents of the womb. As for a miscarriage (sikt), if the fetus has reached a stage where some part of its form is distinguishable (e.g., fingers or toes), then the iddah ends with that miscarriage because it is considered a child (walad). But if it is merely a clot or a lump without discernible features, the iddah does not end, because the pregnancy is not considered to have ended with something that could be a child. This is based on the principle that certainty about the existence of a child requires visible signs of creation.

Key Terms
الموطوءة بشبهة (al-mawtu'ah bi shubhah): A woman who has had intercourse based on a doubtful contract (e.g., mistaken marriage or invalid contract).
استبان بعض خلقه (istabana ba'd khalqihi): The stillborn fetus shows discernible body parts, e.g., fingers or limbs.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Yes, because any miscarriage ends iddah.
B) No, because the fetus must show some discernible creation.
C) Yes, if it was more than 120 days into pregnancy.
D) No, only a live birth ends iddah.

Key Takeaway
A woman's iddah from dubious intercourse ends only upon delivering a child that shows discernible human features, whether live or stillborn.

Conditional Divorce upon Childbirth: Single vs. Repeated Conditions
Rulings on conditional divorces tied to childbirth

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rulings for conditional divorces tied to childbirth when the condition is phrased as 'if you give birth' versus 'whenever you give birth' and apply them to multiple birth scenarios.

Core Idea
A divorce condition with 'if' triggers once; 'whenever' triggers with each birth, up to three.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq presents two key scenarios of conditional divorce tied to childbirth. The first, from al-Tatar Khaniya, deals with a one-time condition ('if you give birth'), while the second, from al-Kafi, deals with a repeated condition ('whenever you give birth'). The rulings differ in how many divorces occur and how the iddah is completed. Understanding the precise wording of the condition is essential in Hanafi law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the condition phrasing
In the first example, the condition is 'if you give birth to a child' (singular, one-time). In the second, it is 'whenever you give birth to a child' (repetitive). Note the key Arabic terms: 'إذا' (if/when) vs 'كلما' (whenever).
2
Determine the number of divorces
For the first, only one divorce occurs upon the first birth; subsequent births do not trigger additional divorces. For the second, each birth triggers a divorce, up to three (since only three divorces are possible).
In the second example, twins: one divorce with first, no divorce with second (iddah ends). Triplets: two divorces (first and second children), third ends iddah.
3
Understand iddah completion
In both scenarios, the iddah ends with the last birth. In the first, the second birth after six months ends the iddah. In the second, the last child ends the iddah, regardless of the number of prior divorces.
4
Handle the six-month gap and admission
If there is a six-month gap between births and the wife does not admit the iddah ended after the first birth, then three divorces occur and she begins a new iddah of three menstrual cycles after the third pronouncement.
This applies to the 'whenever' condition; the gap implies the condition repeated.
5
Note the rule on 'aqr
In the first example, no 'aqr (compensation) is required because the lineage of the second child is established, indicating valid marital relations. 'Aqr is only due in cases of dubious intercourse.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي التتارخانية قال : إذا ولدت ولداً فأنت طالق فولدت ولداً ثم ولدت لستة أشهر ثبت نسب الثاني أيضاً وانقضت به العدة ولا يجب به العقر. وفي الكافي للحاكم : قال لها كلما ولدت ولداً فأنت طالق فولدت ولدين في بطن واحد طلقت بالأول وانقضت العدة بالآخر ولا يقع به طلاق ولو ولدت ثلاثة في بطن وقعت طلقتان وانقضت العدة بالثالث، ولو كان بين الولدين ستة أشهر ولم تقر بانقضاء العدة طلقت ثلاثاً وتحتد بالأقراء بعد الثالث
In al-Tatar Khaniya: He said: If (a man says) 'when you give birth to a child, you are divorced,' then she gives birth to a child, then she gives birth after six months, the lineage of the second is also established and the iddah ends with it, and no 'aqr (compensation) is obligatory. In al-Kafi by al-Hakim: He said (to his wife): 'Whenever you give birth to a child, you are divorced.' She then gives birth to two children in one womb. She is divorced by the first, and the iddah ends with the last, and no divorce occurs by that last. If she gives birth to three in one womb, two divorces occur and the iddah ends with the third. If there is six months between the two children and she does not admit to the end of iddah, she is divorced thrice and begins iddah by menstrual cycles after the third.
Source pages p. 229

Commentary
This passage addresses conditional divorces tied to childbirth. In the first example from al-Tatar Khaniya, the condition is 'when you give birth to a child, you are divorced.' If she gives birth to one child and then another after six months, both children are attributed to the husband and the iddah ends with the second delivery. The 'aqr (a compensation) is not required because the lineage is established. In the second example from al-Kafi, if the husband says 'whenever you give birth to a child, you are divorced,' then she has twins (two children in one womb), the first divorce occurs with the first child, and the iddah ends with the second child; the second child does not cause another divorce because the iddah is already ongoing. If she has triplets, two divorces occur (first and second child), and the iddah ends with the third. If there is a six-month gap between the births and she does not confirm the iddah ending, then three divorces occur and she must observe a new iddah of three menstrual cycles after the third.

Key Terms
العقر (al-'aqr): A financial compensation paid by a man to a woman for having intercourse under a dubious contract.
إذا (idha): Conditional particle meaning 'if' or 'when', used for a one-time condition.
كلما (kullama): Conditional particle meaning 'whenever' or 'every time', used for repeated conditions.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A man says to his wife: 'Whenever you give birth to a child, you are divorced.' She gives birth to twins. What is the ruling?
A) One divorce occurs with the first child, and the iddah ends with the second.
B) Two divorces occur, one for each child.
C) No divorce occurs until both are delivered.
D) She is divorced thrice immediately.

Key Takeaway
The wording of a conditional divorce—'if' vs 'whenever'—determines whether the divorce occurs once or repeatedly with each birth.

Paternity When Missing Husband Returns: Abu Hanifa's Retraction
Intra-Hanafi dispute on paternity with a retraction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the final Hanafi ruling on paternity when a missing husband returns and explain why Abu Hanifa retracted his initial view.

Core Idea
Abu Hanifa retracted: children born after the wife's remarriage belong to the second husband, not the first.

This passage from al-Khaniya records a famous intra-Hanafi dispute. Imam Abu Hanifa initially ruled that children born to the wife of a missing husband who remarried belong to the first husband, but later retracted and held they belong to the second. Understanding why he changed his view reveals the school's practical approach to certainty and lineage preservation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
A woman's husband is missing (man'iy). She remarries after the prescribed waiting period and has children. Then the first husband returns alive.
The first marriage is not automatically dissolved; a court or iddah completion ends it.
2
State Abu Hanifa's initial position
Imam Abu Hanifa initially said the children belong to the first husband, reasoning that the first marriage was never nullified and the husband is the legal father.
3
State the retracted position
Abu Hanifa later retracted and ruled the children belong to the second husband. This is the final mufta bihi view.
The author of al-Khaniya confirms 'upon it is the fatwa'.
4
Explain the basis for retraction
The second husband is present and likely the biological father. Attribution to the first would cause confusion and harm the child's lineage. Certainty of paternity is prioritized.
This is a rational ('aqli) argument based on maslaha.
5
Apply the ruling to the first scenario
Even in the first scenario (revocable divorce + remarriage in iddah), the child is attributed to the second husband if born more than two years from first divorce and six months from second divorce.
This shows consistency: attribution follows the second husband when conception is possible within his marriage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي الخانية : طلقها رجعياً فتزوجت في العدة ثم طلقها الثاني فجاءت بولد لأكثر من سنتين من طلاق الأول ولستة أشهر من طلاق الثاني فإن الولد للثاني، ولو تزوجت المنعي إليها زوجها ثم ولدت أولاداً ثم جاء الزوج الأول حياً كان الإمام أبو حنيفة يقول : الأولاد للأول ثم رجع عنه وقال للثاني وعليه الفتوى . ا هـ .منتقى . قال محمد في نوادر ابن رستم : لو خرج من قبل الرأس نصف البدن غير الرأس أو خرج من قبل الرجلين نصف البدن غير الرجلين انقضت العدة به وفسر فقال : النصف أليتيه إلى منكبيه ولا يعتد من البدن هو من بالرأس ولا بالرجلين.
In al-Khaniya: He divorced her revocably, she remarried during the iddah, then the second divorced her, and she gives birth to a child more than two years from the first divorce and six months from the second, then the child is for the second (husband). If the wife of a missing husband (man'iy) marries, then gives birth to children, then the first husband comes back alive, Imam Abu Hanifa used to say: the children are for the first, then he retracted and said: for the second, and upon it is the fatwa. End quote from Muntaka. Muhammad said in Nawadir Ibn Rustam: If from the head side half the body emerged except the head, or from the leg side half the body emerged except the legs, the iddah ends with it, and he explained: half is from the buttocks to the shoulders, and what is by the head or legs is not counted from the body.
Source pages p. 229

Commentary
This passage from al-Khaniya addresses two scenarios. First, a woman is divorced revocably (talaq raj'i), then remarries during her iddah (which is invalid without reconciliation). The second husband divorces her. She then gives birth after more than two years from the first husband's divorce and at least six months from the second husband's divorce. The child is attributed to the second husband because the timing aligns with a viable pregnancy from him. Second, the case of a missing husband (man'iy) whose wife remarries and has children, then the first husband returns alive. Initially, Abu Hanifa held the children belong to the first husband, but he retracted and ruled they belong to the second husband; this is the relied-upon position (mufta bihi). Then, a report from Muhammad in Nawadir Ibn Rustam discusses when half the body emerges (excluding head or legs) – the iddah ends with that emergence. He defines 'half' as from the buttocks to the shoulders, specifying that the head and legs are not counted in this half.

Key Terms
المنعي إليها زوجها (al-man'iy ilayha zawjuha): A woman whose husband is missing (presumed dead or lost).
رجوع (ruju'): Retraction; here, Abu Hanifa's change of opinion.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A woman's husband goes missing. After the legally prescribed period, she marries another man and has children. Then the first husband returns alive. According to the final Hanafi ruling, to whom are the children attributed?
A) The first husband, because the marriage was never dissolved.
B) The second husband, because that is the retracted view of Abu Hanifa and the fatwa.
C) Both husbands share paternity.
D) The children are fatherless.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school's final position attributes children to the second husband when a missing husband returns after the wife's remarriage and childbirth.

Completion of Iddah by Emergence of Half the Body
Ruling on completion of iddah by partial birth.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule that iddah ends when half the body emerges during childbirth, and define what constitutes 'half'.

Core Idea
Iddah ends when half the body emerges; head and legs are excluded from that half.

This excerpt from Muhammad's Nawadir Ibn Rustam provides a technical definition of when childbirth completes iddah. The ruling specifies that emergence of half the body (from buttocks to shoulders) without head or legs is sufficient to end the iddah period, clarifying a detail that can arise in complicated deliveries.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the context
The iddah of a divorced or widowed woman ends with childbirth, but there is a question of how much of the baby must emerge.
This is a supplementary ruling beyond the general principle.
2
Read the condition
Muhammad reports that if half the body emerges from the head side (excluding the head) or from the leg side (excluding the legs), the iddah ends.
The head and legs are specifically excluded.
3
Understand the definition of half
The half is defined as from the buttocks to the shoulders. The head and legs are not counted in this measurement.
This is a specific anatomical definition given by the report.
4
Apply the ruling
If only the head emerges, the iddah continues. If half the body (without head/legs) emerges, the iddah ends immediately.
The ruling covers both vertex and breech presentations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

In al-Khaniya: He divorced her revocably, she remarried during the iddah, then the second divorced her, and she gives birth to a child more than two years from the first divorce and six months from the second, then the child is for the second (husband). If the wife of a missing husband (man'iy) marries, then gives birth to children, then the first husband comes back alive, Imam Abu Hanifa used to say: the children are for the first, then he retracted and said: for the second, and upon it is the fatwa. End quote from Muntaka. Muhammad said in Nawadir Ibn Rustam: If from the head side half the body emerged except the head, or from the leg side half the body emerged except the legs, the iddah ends with it, and he explained: half is from the buttocks to the shoulders, and what is by the head or legs is not counted from the body.

Commentary
This passage from al-Khaniya addresses two scenarios. First, a woman is divorced revocably (talaq raj'i), then remarries during her iddah (which is invalid without reconciliation). The second husband divorces her. She then gives birth after more than two years from the first husband's divorce and at least six months from the second husband's divorce. Second, the case of a missing husband (man'iy) whose wife remarries and has children, then the first husband returns alive. Then, a report from Muhammad in Nawadir Ibn Rustam discusses when half the body emerges (excluding head or legs) – the iddah ends with that emergence. He defines 'half' as from the buttocks to the shoulders, specifying that the head and legs are not counted in this half.

Key Terms
نصف البدن (nisf al-badan): Half the body; here defined as from the buttocks to the shoulders, excluding head and legs.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) From the head to the waist.
D) The entire body except the head.

Key Takeaway

Emergence of Half or More of the Child: Legal Effects
Rulings on when half the child's body emerges, ending iddah and other effects

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the rule for when half or more of a child emerges and distinguish the effect on iddah, raj'a, and permissibility for other husbands.

Core Idea
Emergence of half or more ends iddah, but the cautious view prevents remarriage until full delivery.

When a woman gives birth, her iddah ends with delivery. But what if only part of the child emerges? The Hanafi madhhab provides detailed rules on what constitutes sufficient emergence to end iddah and affect other rulings. This lesson examines these rules from Nawadir Ibn Rustam and al-Haruniyyat as transmitted in al-Bahr al-Raiq.

Guided Path
1
Identify the types of emergence
The passage describes three scenarios: half the body from the head side (excluding head), half from the leg side (excluding legs), and majority (more than half). Also mentions head only.
Half is defined as from buttocks to shoulders.
2
Determine the effect on iddah
If half or majority emerges, the iddah ends. If only the head emerges (or head with less than half), the iddah does not end.
Iddah ends upon emergence, not upon full delivery, in these cases.
3
Determine the effect on raj'a
If majority emerges, the husband's right of revocation (raj'a) is cut off. However, according to the mashayikh, she does not become permissible for other husbands.
There is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on permissibility for others.
4
Understand the conclusion
The majority emergence is like full delivery for ending iddah and cutting raj'a, but not for making her permissible for other men according to the cautious view.
The mufta bihi is the cautious view of the mashayikh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي نوادر ابن رستم : لو خرج من قبل الرأس نصف البدن غير الرأس أو خرج من قبل الرجلين نصف البدن غير الرجلين انقضت العدة به وفسر فقال : النصف أليتيه إلى منكبيه ولا يعتد من البدن هو من بالرأس ولا بالرجلين. وقال في الهارونيات: لو خرج أكثر الولد لم تصح الرجعة وحلت للأزواج. وقال مشايخنا لا تحل للأزواج أيضاً لأنه قام مقام الكل في حق انقطاع الرجعة والحاصل أن خروج الأكثر كالكل في جميع الأحكام إلا في حلها للأزواج على قول المشايخ، وخروج الرأس فقط أو مع الأقل لا اعتبار به فلا تنقضي به العدة ولا يثبت نسب من المبانة إذا كان لأقل من سنتين والباقي للأكثر ، ولا قصاص بقطعهما
In Nawadir Ibn Rustam: If from the head side half the body emerged except the head, or from the leg side half the body emerged except the legs, the iddah ends with it, and he explained: half is from the buttocks to the shoulders, and what is by the head or legs is not counted from the body. And he said in al-Haruniyyat: If most of the child emerged, the revocation (raj'a) is not valid, and she becomes permissible for other husbands. And our scholars (mashayikh) said: she does not become permissible for other husbands either, because it has taken the place of the whole in the matter of cutting off the right of revocation. The conclusion is that emergence of the majority is like the whole in all rulings except in making her permissible for other husbands according to the saying of our scholars. And emergence of only the head or with the less is not considered, so iddah does not end with it, nor is lineage established for a woman separated by bain if the head emerges at less than two years and the rest after more, and no qisas for cutting them (the head and limbs).
Source pages p. 230

Commentary
This passage from Nawadir Ibn Rustam and al-Haruniyyat discusses the legal effect of a child's partial emergence during birth. The general rule is that if half of the child's body emerges (excluding head or legs), the iddah of the mother ends, just as if the whole child had emerged. The 'half' is defined as from the buttocks to the shoulders. However, there is a difference of opinion: some say that if most of the child emerges, the husband's right of revocation (raj'a) in a revocable divorce is cut off, and the woman becomes permissible for other husbands. But the mashayikh (later Hanafi scholars) hold that she does not become permissible for other husbands yet, because the child's emergence only takes the place of full delivery for ending iddah, not for making her halal for others. The ultimate ruling is that the emergence of the majority is equivalent to the whole in all rulings except in making her permissible for other husbands, where caution is exercised. Emergence of only the head or the head with a lesser part of the body is not considered significant: it does not end iddah, does not establish lineage if the mother is in bain, and there is no qisas for cutting those parts.

Key Terms
الرجعة (al-raj'ah): The right of a husband to take back his wife during the revocable divorce period without a new marriage.
النصف (al-nisf): Half of the child's body, defined as from the buttocks to the shoulders.
الأكثر (al-akthar): Majority, i.e., more than half of the child's body.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
During birth, only the child's head emerges. Does the mother's iddah end?
A) Yes, because birth has begun.
B) No, because emergence of the head alone is not considered significant.
C) Yes, if the child is alive.
D) No, because only full delivery ends iddah.

Key Takeaway
Emergence of half or more of the child ends iddah and cuts off raj'a, but does not allow remarriage until full delivery according to the cautious view.

Stillbirth Timing and Iddah from Shubha
Rulings on stillbirth timing and iddah for a woman in iddah from shubha who becomes pregnant

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on when a stillbirth ends iddah and explain the significance of 120 days for fetal development and lineage attribution.

Core Idea
A stillbirth with discernible creation after 120 days ends iddah; before that, it does not.

This lesson combines several rulings from Hanafi works on the timing of stillbirths and their effect on iddah. The central benchmark is 120 days (40+40+40) when the soul is breathed into the fetus. A stillbirth with discernible features after this period is considered a child and ends the iddah, while earlier miscarriages do not. The lesson also covers the attribution of the child to the husband and the oath procedure when a woman claims stillbirth.

Guided Path
1
Identify the key temporal benchmarks
Note that the passage sets 120 days (four lunar months) as the point when the soul is breathed. Before that, the fetus passes through nutfah (40 days), alaqah (40 days), mudghah (40 days). A stillbirth before 120 days does not have discernible creation and thus does not count as a child for iddah or lineage.
The hadith about creation stages is implicitly referenced.
2
Apply the 120-day rule to marriage and lineage
If a woman miscarries exactly at four months, the child is attributed to the husband. If earlier by one day, the marriage is invalid (if discernible features exist? Actually the text says 'if its creation was discernible' – meaning if features are present, the marriage is invalid because the child must be from the husband? There's nuance; follow the passage.) The calculation for parentage uses lunar months.
The example of tenth of the month shows how to compute five months and twenty days.
3
Understand the effect on iddah from dubious intercourse
A woman in iddah from shubha (dubious intercourse) who becomes pregnant and then delivers ends her iddah with the delivery. This is based on the principle that delivery terminates iddah regardless of the type of intercourse.
This applies whether the child is live or stillborn, as long as it is a child (i.e., discernible features after 120 days).
4
Analyze the oath requirement
If a woman claims she gave birth (or miscarried), her word alone is not accepted; she needs evidence. However, if she requests to take an oath that she miscarried a stillbirth with discernible creation, she is allowed to swear (by agreement). This protects against false claims.
The oath is only about the stillbirth having discernible features, not about the exact timing.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي المحيط أيضاً : تزوج بامرأة فجاء بسقط بعد أربعة أشهر إلا يوماً لم يجز النكاح إن كان قد استبان خلقه لأنه لا في مائة وعشرين يوماً أربعين يوماً نطفة وأربعين علقة وأربعين مضغة ثم ينفخ فيه الروح، وإن سقط لأربعة أشهر تامة فهو من الزوج والعمل على مائة وعشرين يوماً، وإن تزوجها في عشر من الشهر فخمسة أشهر بالأهلة وعشرين يوماً من السادس في لزوم الولد ا هـ. وفي الخانية : المتوفى عنها زوجها إذا ولدت لأكثر من سنتين من وقت الموت يحكم بانقضاء عدتها قبل الولادة بستة أشهر وزيادة فتجعل كأنها تزوجت بزوج آخر بعد انقضاء عدتها وحبلت من الثاني ا هـ . والحاصل أن السقط الذي استبان بعض خلقه يعتبر فيه أربعة أشهر وتام الخلق ستة أشهر ؛ كذا في المجتبى. وفي التتارخانية : المعتدة عن وطء بشبهة إذا حبلت في العدة ثم وضعت انقضت عدتها. وفي البزازية لو قالت المعتدة ولدت لا يقبل قولها بلا بينة فإن طلب يمينها بالله لقد أسقطت سقطاً مستبين الخلق حلفت اتفاقاً ا هـ.
In al-Muhit also: He married a woman and she came with a stillbirth after four months minus one day, the marriage is not valid if its creation was discernible because in one hundred twenty days: forty days a sperm-drop, forty days a clot, forty days a lump, then the soul is breathed into it. And if she miscarries at a full four months, it is from the husband, and the practice is based on one hundred twenty days. And if he married her on the tenth of the month, then five months by lunar months and twenty days of the sixth are considered for establishing parentage. In al-Khaniya: The wife whose husband died, if she gives birth after more than two years from the time of death, it is ruled that her iddah had ended before the birth by six months and more, so she is considered as if she married another husband after her iddah ended and became pregnant from the second. End quote. The conclusion is that a stillbirth in which some creation is discernible is considered at four months, and fully created at six months. This is in al-Mujtaba. In al-Tatar Khaniya: A woman in iddah from dubious intercourse, if she becomes pregnant during the iddah and then delivers, her iddah ends. In al-Bazzaziyya: If a woman in iddah says 'I gave birth,' her statement is not accepted without evidence. But if she requests an oath by Allah that she miscarried a stillbirth with discernible creation, she is sworn in accordance with the agreement (ittifaqan).
Source pages p. 230

Commentary
This passage brings together several rulings. First, from al-Muhit: if a man marries a woman and she miscarries at four months minus one day, the marriage is invalid if the fetus has discernible features, because the soul is not breathed until 120 days (40+40+40). If she miscarries at exactly four months, the child is attributed to the husband. The calculation for parentage is based on 120 days. An example: if the marriage occurred on the 10th of a month, then five full lunar months plus 20 days of the sixth are considered for establishing lineage. Second, from al-Khaniya: if a widow gives birth more than two years after her husband's death, it is assumed her iddah ended before that birth (by six months plus something), so she is considered to have married another man after her iddah. Third, a summary: a stillbirth with discernible features is considered a child after four months (120 days), and a fully formed child after six months. Fourth, from al-Tatar Khaniya: a woman in iddah from dubious intercourse who becomes pregnant during the iddah and then gives birth ends her iddah with that delivery. Finally, from al-Bazzaziyya: if a woman in iddah claims she gave birth, her statement is not accepted without witnesses. However, if she requests to take an oath that she miscarried a stillbirth with discernible creation, she is allowed to swear according to the agreement of scholars.

Key Terms
النطفة (al-nutfah): The sperm-drop; first stage of fetal development lasting 40 days.
العلقة (al-alaqah): The clot; second stage of fetal development.
المضغة (al-mudghah): The lump of flesh; third stage before the soul is breathed.
السقط (al-siqṭ): A stillbirth; a fetus that is born dead.
استبان خلقه (istabāna khalquhu): Its creation became discernible; having identifiable human features.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A woman in iddah from dubious intercourse becomes pregnant and gives birth. What effect does this have on her iddah?
A) Her iddah ends with the delivery, even if the child is stillborn.
B) Her iddah ends only if she delivers a live child.
C) Her iddah continues until she completes three menstrual cycles.
D) Her iddah ends only when she takes an oath.

Key Takeaway
A stillbirth ends iddah only if it has discernible creation, which occurs after 120 days (four lunar months) when the soul is breathed.
