Partial Birth: The Principle of Khuruj al-Akthar and Its Legal Effects
Compilation of rulings from Nawadir on partial childbirth outcomes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule that emergence of most of the child is treated as complete birth, and apply it to iddah, lineage, qisas, and emancipation cases.

Core Idea
Emergence of most of the child is like complete birth for all rulings except remarriage with the child.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Rā'iq compiles rulings from the Nawadir regarding the legal effect of partial birth. The key principle is that khurūj al-akthar (emergence of the majority) is equivalent to complete birth for most purposes, such as ending iddah, establishing lineage, and liability for diyah. However, for the permissibility of remarrying the child (if female), the majority rule does not apply due to precaution (ihtiyāṭ). We will walk through the detailed applications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the key principle
Recognize that the passage centers on the rule 'khuruj al-akthar ka al-kull' (emergence of the majority is like the whole). This is the central legal maxim.
Also note the exception for permissibility.
2
List the exceptions
Note that the only exception is for permissibility of remarriage (hullahā li'l-azwāj). All other rulings follow the majority rule.
The exception is due to ihtiyāṭ (precaution).
3
Apply to specific cases
Work through the examples: if head and half body emerge at less than two years, iddah ends; if only head, no effect; if legs emerge first, etc.
Consider the two-year maximum period for child's attribution.
4
Connect to stillbirth
Understand that stillbirth after 120 days (soul breathed) with distinct features establishes lineage and affects marriage validity based on the hadith of creation stages.
Before 120 days, stillbirth is not considered a child.
5
Distinguish emancipation rules
If a slave-girl is freed after partial birth, the fetus follows her in freedom only if the minority emerged; if majority or half emerged, the child remains a slave.
This ruling is from Nawadir Ibn Hisham.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الطلاق باب العدة احتياطاً ولا يقوم مقامه في حق حلها للأزواج احتياطاً. وفي نوادر ابن سماعة : لو جاءت المبانة المدخولة بولد فخرج رأسه لأقل من سنتين وخرج الباقي لأكثر من سنتين لم يلزمه حتى يخرج الرأس ونصف البدن لأقل من سنتين ويخرج الباقي لأكثر من سنتين أو يخرج من قبل الرجلين الأكثر من البدن لأقل من سنتين ويخرج ما بقي لأكثره، ولو خرج الرأس فقتله إنسان وجبت الدية ولا يجب القصاص، وكذلك في أذنيه، ولو قطع الرجلين قبل الرأس وجبت الدية. وفي نوادر ابن هشام قال الجاريته أنت حرة وقد خرج رأس الولد مع نصف البدن لا تعتق حتى يخرج النصف سوى الرأس. ا هـ . ما في المحيط. والحاصل أن خروج الأكثر كالكل في جميع الأحكام إلا في حلها للأزواج على قول المشايخ، وخروج الرأس فقط أو مع الأقل لا اعتبار به فلا تنقضي به العدة ولا يثبت نسب من المبانة إذا كان لأقل من سنتين والباقي للأكثر ، ولا قصاص بقطعهما ودليل مسألة العتق في المحيط محرفة من الكاتب. وحاصلها أن الحمل يتبع الأم في العتق فإذا أعتقت بعد خروج بعضه، فإن خرج الأكثر أو النصف لا يتبعها، وإن خرج الأقل يتبعها. وفي المحيط أيضاً : تزوج بامرأة فجاء بسقط بعد أربعة أشهر إلا يوماً لم يجز النكاح إن كان قد استبان خلقه لأنه لا في مائة وعشرين يوماً أربعين يوماً نطفة وأربعين علقة وأربعين مضغة ثم ينفخ فيه الروح، وإن سقط لأربعة أشهر تامة فهو من الزوج والعمل على مائة وعشرين يوماً، وإن تزوجها في عشر من الشهر فخمسة أشهر بالأهلة وعشرين يوماً من السادس في لزوم الولد ا هـ. وفي الخانية : المتوفى عنها زوجها إذا ولدت لأكثر من سنتين من وقت الموت يحكم بانقضاء عدتها قبل الولادة بستة أشهر وزيادة فتجعل كأنها تزوجت بزوج آخر بعد انقضاء عدتها وحبلت من الثاني ا هـ . والحاصل أن السقط الذي استبان بعض خلقه يعتبر فيه أربعة أشهر وتام الخلق ستة أشهر ؛ كذا في المجتبى. وفي التتارخانية : المعتدة عن وطء بشبهة إذا حبلت في العدة ثم وضعت انقضت عدتها. وفي البزازية لو قالت المعتدة ولدت لا يقبل قولها بلا بينة فإن طلب يمينها بالله لقد أسقطت سقطاً مستبين الخلق حلفت اتفاقاً ا هـ .
Kitab al-Talaq, chapter of 'Iddah: Precautionarily, and it does not take its place in the matter of her being lawful for husbands, precautionarily. And in the Nawadir of Ibn Samā'ah: If a woman divorced irrevocably (mubā'ana) and was consummated gives birth, and his head emerges at less than two years and the rest emerges at more than two years, it is not binding upon him until the head and half the body emerge at less than two years and the rest emerges at more than two years, or the majority of the body emerges from the legs at less than two years and the rest emerges at more, and if the head emerges and a person kills him, full blood-money (diyah) is obligatory but not retaliation (qisas), and similarly for his ears, and if the legs are cut off before the head, diyah is obligatory. And in the Nawadir of Ibn Hishām: He said to his slave-girl: 'You are free', and the head of the child emerged with half the body; she is not freed until the half besides the head emerges. End quote from al-Muhīt. The summary is that the emergence of the majority (al-akthar) is like the whole in all rulings except in her being lawful for husbands according to the saying of the mashāyikh, and the emergence of only the head or with the minority is not considered, so the iddah is not ended thereby, nor is lineage established from the mubā'ana if [the head] is at less than two years and the rest at more, and there is no qisas for cutting them off. And the evidence for the issue of emancipation in al-Muhīt is corrupted by the scribe. Its summary: the fetus follows the mother in emancipation; if she is freed after the emergence of part of it, if the majority or half emerges, it does not follow her, but if the minority emerges, it follows her. And in al-Muhīt also: He married a woman and she brought a stillbirth (siqṭ) after four months minus a day; the marriage is not valid if its creation is distinct, because in 120 days: 40 days a nutfah, 40 a alaqah, 40 a mudghah, then the soul is breathed into it. If the stillbirth is at a full four months, it is from the husband, and the action is based on 120 days. And if he married her on the 10th of the month, then five months by the lunar months and 20 days from the sixth regarding the necessity of the child. End quote. And in al-Khāniyyah: The widow (mutawaffā 'anhā zawjuhā) if she gives birth more than two years from the time of death, it is ruled that her iddah had ended before the birth by six months and some extra, so she is considered as if she married another husband after the completion of her iddah and became pregnant from the second. End quote. The summary is that a stillbirth whose some creation is distinct is considered at four months, and a complete creation at six months. Thus in al-Mujtabā. And in al-Tātārkhāniyyah: A woman observing iddah from intercourse with uncertainty (shubha), if she becomes pregnant during the iddah then gives birth, her iddah ends. And in al-Bazzāziyyah: If the woman observing iddah says 'I gave birth', her statement is not accepted without evidence; if he demands her oath by Allah that she miscarried a distinct miscarried fetus, she swears by agreement. End quote.
Source pages p. 230

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Rā'iq compiles several rulings from the Nawadir works regarding the effect of partial birth of a child on iddah, lineage, qisas, and emancipation. The key principle is that the emergence of the majority of the child's body (khurūj al-akthar) is treated as equivalent to complete birth for most rulings, except for the permissibility of the child for marriage (hullahā li'l-azwāj). For example, if only the head emerges, the iddah does not end, lineage is not established for a divorced woman, and no qisas is due for killing the child. The passage also discusses stillbirths: a miscarriage before four months (120 days) is not considered a child with a soul, so it does not establish lineage or affect marriage validity. The hadith of the creation stages (nutfah, alaqah, mudghah, then soul) is cited as evidence. Additionally, if a slave-girl is freed after part of the child emerges, the child is free only if the minority emerged; if the majority or half emerged, the child remains a slave. These rulings are from the Nawadir and are relied upon by the mashāyikh.

Key Terms
خروج الأكثر (khurūj al-akthar): The emergence of more than half of the child's body from the womb, treated as equivalent to complete birth in Hanafi fiqh for most rulings.
سقط مستبين الخلق (siqṭ mustabīn al-khalq): A miscarried fetus with distinct human features (e.g., limbs, features), considered a child for legal purposes after 120 days.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) When the child's head emerges
B) When the majority of the child's body emerges
C) When the entire child is born
D) When the child survives after birth

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi ruling equates emergence of the majority of the child (khuruj al-akthar) with complete birth for most legal effects, except for permissibility of remarriage.

Talaq — Divorce
intermediate
8 min
The iddah of the Irrevocably Divorced Terminally Ill Man's Wife
Intra-Hanafi dispute on the iddah of zawjat al-fār

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for the iddah of a woman irrevocably divorced by a terminally ill husband and explain the intra-madhhab disagreement between the Sahibayn and Abu Yusuf.

Core Idea
The wife observes the longer of the two iddahs – a precaution based on retained inheritance rights.

When a man on his deathbed divorces his wife irrevocably, she faces two possible iddahs: the divorce iddah of three cycles and the death iddah of four months and ten days. The Hanafi school is divided on which applies. This lesson examines the reasoning behind the dominant view of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad and the dissenting opinion of Abu Yusuf.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
A woman is irrevocably divorced (bā'in) by her husband while he is terminally ill; he later dies within her iddah. The question is which iddah she must complete.
This is the case of zawjat al-fār.
2
State the majority Hanafi position
Abu Hanifa and Muhammad (the Sahibayn) rule that she must observe the longer of iddat al-wafāt (4 months 10 days) and iddat al-talāq (3 cycles).
This is the muftā bihi position.
3
Identify the dissenting position
Abu Yusuf holds that she only observes iddat al-talāq (3 cycles) because the marriage ended at divorce, not at death.
He argues that the death iddah only applies when marriage ends by death.
4
Analyze the key proof
The Sahibayn argue that since the wife inherits from him, the marriage is considered ongoing for iddah as a precaution (ihtiyāṭ), thus combining both iddahs. Abu Yusuf counters that inheritance is a separate right and does not affect iddah.
The objection based on apostasy is addressed: a Muslim wife of an apostate inherits but her iddah is by cycles, not death iddah.
5
Determine the practical difference
Under the majority, a woman who completes three cycles before her husband's death still must wait out the four months and ten days. Under Abu Yusuf, she would be free after three cycles.
This is the thamarah al-khilāf.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

في العدة حتى تحيض ثلاثاً، ولو حاضت ثلاثاً قبل تمام هذه المدة لم تنقض حتى تتم كما ذكره في الخانية والعناية واعترضه في فتح القدير بأنه مقصر لأنه لا يصدق إلا إذا كانت الأربعة الأشهر وعشراً بعد من الثلاث حيض وحقيقة قوله : (لأنه لا يصدق إلا إذا كانت الخ أي وأما إذا امتد طهرها حتى مضت مدة الوفاة فإنه لا يصدق عليه لأن الحيض يكون خارجاً عنها لا واقعاً فيها قوله : قلت ويعتبر الحيض الخ) من الحال أنها لا بد ا.هـ. فعلى هذا قول من فسره بالأربعة الأشهر والعشر فيها ثلاث حيض مشكل لأنه يقتضي أنه لا بد أن تكون الحيض كلها في عدة الوفاة، وعلى ما في المجتبى لو حاضت حيضتين قبل وفاته ولم تحض بعد وفاته إلا واحدة ومضت عدة الوفاة كفى بخلاف ما في الخانية قيدنا بكونه بائناً لأنه لو طلقها رجعياً فعدتها عدة الوفاة، سواء طلقها في الصحة أو في المرض بطريق انتقال عدة الطلاق إلى عدة الوفاة وترث منه . وقيدنا بكونه في مرض موته لأنه لو طلقها بائناً في صحته لم تنتقل ولا ترث، وما ذكره المصنف قولهما. وقال أبو يوسف : عدتها ثلاث حيض لأن النكاح قد انقطع قبل الموت بالطلاق ولزمها ثلاث حيض، وإنما تجب عدة الوفاة إذا زال النكاح بالوفاة إلا أنه في حق الإرث لا في حق تغيير العدة بخلاف الرجعي لأن النكاح باقي من كل وجه. ولهما أنه لما بقي في حق الإرث يجعل باقياً في حق العدة احتياطاً فيجمع بينهما؛ كذا في الهداية. وأورد على قولهما لو ارتد زوج المسلمة فمات أو قتل على ردته ترثه زوجته المسلمة وعدتها بالحيض فقد بقي في حق الإرث ولم يبق في حق العدة فكذا في زوجة الفار. والجواب منع المسلمة بل يلزمها عدة الوفاة على ما أشار إليه الكرخي فهو على الاختلاف، وقيل عدتها بالحيض إجماعاً لأن النكاح ما اعتبر باقياً إلى وقت الموت في حق الإرث لأن المسلمة لا ترث الكافر فيستند استحقاقه إلى وقت الردة. وقد استفيد بما ذكرناه أن وضع المسألة فيما إذا لم تحض ثلاثاً قبل موته، أما إذا حاضت ثلاثاً قبل موته فقد انقضت عدتها ولم تدخل تحت حكم بقي كلام المجتبى وقيد بالحيض لأن الأربعة أشهر عشرة أيام عدة الوفاة ولا تكون إلا بعد الوفاة قوله : (قيدنا بكونه بائناً رسائله : فتفريعه على مقدر على حد قوله تعالى والذي أخرج المرعى فجعله غثاء أحوى [الأعلى : 4] إذ لا يصح أن يكون قوله «فعدتها» عدة الوفاة فرعاً لقوله طلقها» لأن المطلقة عدتها بالحيض أو ما يقوم مقامها بنص الكتاب والإجماع، ولأنه لو كان مفرعاً على قوله طلقها» لم يصح قوله بطريق انتقال عدة الطلاق إلى عدة الوفاة لأن المنتقل عنه غير المنتقل إليه اهـ . ثم إن التقييد المذكور غير لازم كما في الشرنبلالية لأن الكلام فيمن يموت زوجها الفار في عدتها والمطلقة رجعياً ليس زوجها فاراً. هذا وقد أقام الشرنبلالي النكير على صاحب الدرر وغيره حيث قال عدة امرأة الفار للبائن أبعد الأجلين وللرجعي ما للموت بأنه خطأ من وجوه : أحدها أنه يقتضي أنها إذا طلقت رجعياً وزوجها مريض فانقضى لها أربعة أشهر وعشر لا ترثه مع بقاء شيء من حيضها، وأنها إذا حاضت ثلاث حيض وهو حي ولم تمض أربعة وهو حي
His saying: (And the wife of the divorcing terminally ill man (zawjat al-fār) has the longer of the two terms) means the waiting period of a woman irrevocably divorced during her husband's terminal illness without her consent is the iddah of death and the iddah of divorce. So the meaning of 'the longer of the two terms' is the passage of four months and ten days including three menstrual cycles, so that if this period passes and she has not menstruated three times, she remains in iddah until she menstruates three times, and if she menstruates three times before the completion of this period, the iddah does not end until [the period] is completed, as mentioned in al-Khāniyyah and al-‘Ināyah. And al-Fatḥ al-Qadīr objected that it is deficient because it is only valid if the four months and ten days are after the three menstrual cycles... [continued]... This and Shurunblālī criticized the author of al-Durar and others who said that the iddah of the woman of the divorcing dying man for the irrevocably divorced is the longer of the two terms, and for the revocably divorced, it is that of death, saying it is wrong in several ways...
Source pages p. 230

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Rā'iq addresses the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the iddah of a woman who is irrevocably divorced by her terminally ill husband (zawjat al-fār). The key issue: her husband dies while she is in her iddah of divorce. The majority of the Hanafi school (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad al-Shaybani) hold that she must observe the longer of the two waiting periods: either iddat al-wafāt (four months and ten days) or iddat al-talāq (three menstrual cycles), whichever ends later. This is because the marriage is considered to have persisted for the purpose of inheritance, so out of precaution, it is also considered persistent for iddah, combining both. Abu Yusuf disagrees, arguing that since the marriage was already severed by the irrevocable divorce before death, only the divorce iddah applies; the death iddah only applies when marriage ends by death. The text also addresses an objection based on the case of an apostate husband, and later Shurunblālī critiques the wording of earlier scholars, distinguishing between revocable and irrevocable divorce. The relied-upon (muftā bihi) position is that of the Sahibayn: the longer of the two terms.

Key Terms
زوجة الفار (zawjat al-fār): A woman whose husband divorces her irrevocably while he is terminally ill (fār).
أبعد الأجلين (ab'ad al-ajalayn): The longer of the two terms: iddat al-wafāt (4 months 10 days) or iddat al-talāq (3 menstrual cycles).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the majority Hanafi position (Sahibayn), what is the iddah of a woman irrevocably divorced by her terminally ill husband?
A) Four months and ten days only
B) Three menstrual cycles only
C) The longer of the two waiting periods
D) The shorter of the two waiting periods

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that a woman irrevocably divorced by a terminally ill husband must observe the longer of the two iddahs, with Abu Yusuf dissenting; the majority view is relied upon for fatwa.

Ruling on the 'Iddah of a Woman Divorced Bā’inan by a Husband in Marad al-Mawt
Ruling and intra-madhhab dispute on 'iddah after bā’in divorce in marad al-mawt

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the 'iddah of a woman divorced bā’inan by a husband in marad al-mawt, and explain the dispute between Abū Ḥanīfa and Abū Yūsuf regarding the applicable 'iddah.

Core Idea
The 'iddah for a bā’in divorce in terminal illness is the longer of the death 'iddah or divorce 'iddah—not just three menses.

When a husband divorces his wife irrevocably during terminal illness and then dies during her 'iddah, which waiting period applies? The Hanafi school holds that she must observe the longer of the two periods: four months and ten days (death 'iddah) or three menstrual cycles (divorce 'iddah). This ruling stems from the principle that since she inherits from him, her marriage is considered persisting for 'iddah purposes as a precaution. However, Abū Yūsuf disagrees, maintaining that the marriage ended with the divorce and only the divorce 'iddah applies. This lesson unpacks the ruling, the proof, and the intra-madhhab dispute.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and core ruling
The scenario is a woman divorced bā’inan by a husband in marad al-mawt; the husband dies during her 'iddah. The ruling is ab‘ad al-ajalayn (the longer of the two 'iddahs).
2
Understand the conditions
The divorce must be bā’in (irrevocable) and the husband must be in marad al-mawt (terminal illness). If the divorce is raj‘iy, the 'iddah automatically becomes the death 'iddah.
The condition of marad al-mawt is crucial; a divorce in health does not trigger this ruling.
3
Examine the proof for the view of the two
The marriage persists for inheritance (she inherits), so it should also persist for 'iddah as a precaution (istiḥsān). This is a qiyās based on the persistence of marriage for inheritance.
The proof is not a direct text but derived from the principle of taking both benefits.
4
Analyze the opposing view of Abū Yūsuf
Abū Yūsuf argues that since the marriage ended by divorce, the 'iddah of death should not apply. His reasoning is that the 'iddah of death is only for marriages ending by death.
He sees the marriage as completely severed at the time of divorce.
5
Evaluate the tarjīḥ
The view of Abū Ḥanīfa and Muḥammad is the muftā bihī (operative fatwa) because it is more precautionary and harmonizes inheritance and 'iddah.
Al-Hidāyah and the author support this view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي المجتبى يعني بأبعد الأجلين عدة الوفاة إن كانت أطول وعدة الطلاق إن كانت أطول. قلت : ويعتبر الحيض من وقت الطلاق لا الوفاة ا.هـ. فعلى هذا قول من فسره بالأربعة الأشهر والعشر فيها ثلاث حيض مشكل لأنه يقتضي أنه لا بد أن تكون الحيض كلها في عدة الوفاة، وعلى ما في المجتبى لو حاضت حيضتين قبل وفاته ولم تحض بعد وفاته إلا واحدة ومضت عدة الوفاة كفى بخلاف ما في الخانية قيدنا بكونه بائناً لأنه لو طلقها رجعياً فعدتها عدة الوفاة، سواء طلقها في الصحة أو في المرض بطريق انتقال عدة الطلاق إلى عدة الوفاة وترث منه . وقيدنا بكونه في مرض موته لأنه لو طلقها بائناً في صحته لم تنتقل ولا ترث، وما ذكره المصنف قولهما. وقال أبو يوسف : عدتها ثلاث حيض لأن النكاح قد انقطع قبل الموت بالطلاق ولزمها ثلاث حيض، وإنما تجب عدة الوفاة إذا زال النكاح بالوفاة إلا أنه في حق الإرث لا في حق تغيير العدة بخلاف الرجعي لأن النكاح باقي من كل وجه. ولهما أنه لما بقي في حق الإرث يجعل باقياً في حق العدة احتياطاً فيجمع بينهما؛ كذا في الهداية. وأورد على قولهما لو ارتد زوج المسلمة فمات أو قتل على ردته ترثه زوجته المسلمة وعدتها بالحيض فقد بقي في حق الإرث ولم يبق في حق العدة فكذا في زوجة الفار. والجواب منع المسلمة بل يلزمها عدة الوفاة على ما أشار إليه الكرخي فهو على الاختلاف، وقيل عدتها بالحيض إجماعاً لأن النكاح ما اعتبر باقياً إلى وقت الموت في حق الإرث لأن المسلمة لا ترث الكافر فيستند استحقاقه إلى وقت الردة. وقد استفيد بما ذكرناه أن وضع المسألة فيما إذا لم تحض ثلاثاً قبل موته، أما إذا حاضت ثلاثاً قبل موته فقد انقضت عدتها ولم تدخل تحت حكم بقي كلام المجتبى وقيد بالحيض لأن الأربعة أشهر عشرة أيام عدة الوفاة ولا تكون إلا بعد الوفاة قوله : (قيدنا بكونه بائناً لأنه لو طلقها رجعياً) أي ومات وهي في العدة على حسب حالها أي بأن مات قبل مضي ثلاث حيض إن كانت من ذوات الحيض أو قبل مضي الأشهر إن كانت ممن لا تحيض، أو قبل وضع الحمل إن كانت حاملاً قال الشرنبلالي في بعض رسائله : فتفريعه على مقدر على حد قوله تعالى والذي أخرج المرعى فجعله غثاء أحوى [الأعلى : 4] إذ لا يصح أن يكون قوله «فعدتها» عدة الوفاة فرعاً لقوله طلقها» لأن المطلقة عدتها بالحيض أو ما يقوم مقامها بنص الكتاب والإجماع، ولأنه لو كان مفرعاً على قوله طلقها» لم يصح قوله بطريق انتقال عدة الطلاق إلى عدة الوفاة لأن المنتقل عنه غير المنتقل إليه اهـ . ثم إن التقييد المذكور غير لازم كما في الشرنبلالية لأن الكلام فيمن يموت زوجها الفار في عدتها والمطلقة رجعياً ليس زوجها فاراً. هذا وقد أقام الشرنبلالي النكير على صاحب الدرر وغيره حيث قال عدة امرأة الفار للبائن أبعد الأجلين وللرجعي ما للموت بأنه خطأ من وجوه : أحدها أنه يقتضي أنها إذا طلقت رجعياً وزوجها مريض فانقضى لها أربعة أشهر وعشر لا ترثه مع بقاء شيء من حيضها، وأنها إذا حاضت ثلاث حيض وهو حي ولم تمض أربعة أشهر وعشر ترث منه، وأنها لو تزوجت بعد مضي أربعة أشهر ولم تحض فيها يصح نكاحها وأنها لو حاضت ثلاث حيض وتزوجت ولم يصح وكل ذلك باطل فبطلت تلك العبارات المخالفة، وأنها لم تصدر عن صاحب المذهب ولا أصحابه والذي صدرت عنه ابتداء أراد غير ظاهرها وهو أنه أراد الانتقال عن عدة الطلاق الرجعي لعدة الوفاة حال حياته لترث بموته فيها ولا يفيد ما أراده من الانتقال تلك العبارات. وقد أردت بهذا إيضاح بطلانها لتجتنب فإنها وقعت في أجل كتب المذهب . هذا حاصل ما ذكره في رسالته وحاشيته على الدرر والذي يظهر أنهم تسامحوا في تسمية المطلق رجعياً في مرض موته فاراً اعتماداً على ما قرروه في موضعه وروماً للاختصار، وحينئذ فليس المراد إلا ما إذا مات وهي في العدة وكون المراد حينئذ الانتقال إلى عدة الوفاة ظاهر فدعوى أنه ليس في تلك العبارات ما يفيده ممنوعة، وما ذكره من أوجه البطلان فيما إذا كان حياً وعلى ما قلنا من
And in al-Mujtabā: It means by the longer of the two periods: the 'iddah of death if it is longer, and the 'iddah of divorce if it is longer. I said: And the menses are considered from the time of divorce, not death. End quote. So according to this, the statement of one who interpreted it as four months and ten days containing three menses is problematic, because it implies that all three menses must occur within the 'iddah of death. But according to what is in al-Mujtabā, if she experienced two menses before his death and only one after his death, and the 'iddah of death passed, it suffices—contrary to what is in al-Khāniyyah. We restricted it to being a bā’in divorce, because if he divorced her raj‘iyyan, her 'iddah becomes the 'iddah of death, whether he divorced her during health or illness, by way of the transfer of the 'iddah of divorce to the 'iddah of death, and she inherits from him. And we restricted it to being during his terminal illness, because if he divorced her bā’inan during his health, it does not transfer and she does not inherit. And what the author mentioned is the view of the two (Abū Ḥanīfa and Muḥammad). Abū Yūsuf said: Her 'iddah is three menses, because the marriage was severed before death by the divorce, and three menses became obligatory upon her. The 'iddah of death is only obligatory when the marriage ends by death, except that this is regarding inheritance, not regarding changing the 'iddah—contrary to the raj‘iy divorce, because the marriage persists in every respect. Their proof: Since it persisted regarding inheritance, it is considered as persisting regarding the 'iddah as a precaution, so the two are combined; as in al-Hidāyah. And an objection was raised against them: if a Muslim woman's husband apostatized and died or was killed in his apostasy, his Muslim wife inherits from him and her 'iddah is by menses—persistence in inheritance did not entail persistence in 'iddah. So too for the wife of the fugitive. The answer: The case of the Muslim woman is rejected; rather she must observe the 'iddah of death, as al-Karkhī indicated, so it is subject to difference of opinion. And it was said her 'iddah is by menses by consensus, because the marriage was not considered persisting up to death regarding inheritance—since a Muslim woman does not inherit from an unbeliever, so her entitlement is based on the time of apostasy. And from what we mentioned it is understood that the issue applies when she has not experienced three menses before his death. But if she experienced three menses before his death, her 'iddah has ended and she does not fall under the ruling... The rest of the passage continues with the commentary and critique.
Source pages p. 231

Commentary
This passage is a detailed ruling with proof and intra-madhhab dispute. It addresses the case of a woman divorced bā’inan by her husband who is in marad al-mawt (terminal illness), and who then dies during her 'iddah. The text first establishes that the woman must observe the longer of the two 'iddahs: either the 'iddah of divorce (three menses for a menstruating woman) or the 'iddah of death (four months and ten days). This is the view of Abū Ḥanīfa and Muḥammad (the two). The menses are counted from the time of divorce, not from the death. The author then clarifies that the divorce must be bā’in (irrevocable) and the husband must be in terminal illness. If the divorce is raj‘iy (revocable), then the 'iddah automatically becomes the death 'iddah and she inherits, because the marriage persists. If the divorce is bā’in during health, no transfer occurs. Abū Yūsuf disagrees, holding that her 'iddah is three menses because the marriage ended by divorce. The two argue that since the marriage persists for inheritance (she inherits), it should also persist for 'iddah as a precaution. An objection based on apostasy is rebutted. The latter part of the passage contains a critique by al-Shurunbulālī of later scholars who incorrectly phrased the ruling for the raj‘iy case, and explains that the intended meaning is valid despite imprecise wording.

Key Terms
أبعد الأجلين (ab‘ad al-ajalayn): The longer of the two 'iddah periods: either the 'iddah of divorce or the 'iddah of death, whichever is longer.
فار (fār): A husband who divorces his wife during terminal illness with intention to deprive her of inheritance (fugitive).
عدة الوفاة ('iddat al-wafāt): The waiting period upon husband's death: 4 months and 10 days for a free woman, or until delivery if pregnant.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abū Ḥanīfa and Muḥammad, what is the 'iddah of a woman divorced bā’inan by her husband during terminal illness when he dies after she has had only two menses?
A) Three menses only.
B) Four months and ten days (the 'iddah of death).
C) The longer of the two: three menses or four months ten days.
D) Two menses only.

Key Takeaway
The 'iddah of a woman divorced bā’inan by a terminally ill husband is the longer of the death 'iddah and divorce 'iddah, and she inherits.

Slave Woman Freed During Raj‘iy ‘Iddah
States the ruling and its proof

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the change in 'iddah for a slave woman freed during raj‘iy divorce and identify the underlying rationale.

Core Idea
A slave woman's 'iddah becomes that of a free woman if freed during raj‘iy divorce, because the marriage persists.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Rā’iq explains a key exception in Hanafi law: when a slave woman is emancipated while in her 'iddah from a raj‘iy divorce, her 'iddah changes to that of a free woman. This ruling highlights the principle that the persistence of marriage in raj‘iy divorce allows for changes in legal status. We will explore the ruling, its conditions, and the analogy with ilā’.

Guided Path
1
State the ruling and its conditions
State that if a slave woman is freed during her 'iddah from a raj‘iy divorce, her 'iddah becomes that of a free woman (3 menses or 3 months). The condition is that the divorce must be raj‘iy, not bā’in or death.
2
Identify the type of divorce
Distinguish raj‘iy (revocable) from bā’in (irrevocable) and death. Explain that only in raj‘iy does the marriage persist, so the change occurs.
In bā’in and death, the marriage has ended, so no change.
3
Explain the rationale
The marriage persists in raj‘iy divorce in every respect. Therefore, when the slave woman becomes free, the stricter 'iddah of a free woman applies because the marital bond continues.
This is a talīl (cause-based reasoning).
4
Examine the analogy with ilā’
The text extends the same logic to ilā’: if a slave woman is freed during an ilā’ oath, the waiting period transfers to that of a free woman because ilā’ does not immediately end the marriage.
This qiyas reinforces the principle.
5
Summarize the different 'iddah periods
Note the various scenarios: a slave woman's 'iddah is normally half (2 menses or 1.5 months), but changes upon emancipation during raj‘iy. Fatḥ al-Qadīr illustrates four possibilities.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (ومن عتقت في عدة الرجعي لا البائن والموت كالحرة) أي وعدة الأمة إذا معتدة عن طلاق رجعي كعدة الحرة في الابتداء فتتغير عدتها إلى عدة الوفاة، فإن كانت من ذوات الأقراء صارت عدتها ثلاثة حيض وإلا فثلاثة أشهر بخلاف ما إذا كانت عن بائن أو وفاة فإن عدتها لا تتغير لبقاء النكاح في الرجعي من كل وجه وزواله في البائن والموت. قيد بالعدة لأن الأمة لو آلى منها ثم أعتقت انتقل مدة إيلائها إلى مدة الحرائر لأن البينونة ليست من أحكام الإيلاء في الابتداء لأنها لا تثبت إلا بعد المدة فكانت الزوجية قائمة للحال فأشبه الطلاق الرجعي. وفي فتح القدير : وقد صور ا الانتقال إلى جميع كميات العدة البيسطة وهي أربعة صورتها أمة صغيرة منكوحة طلقت رجعياً فعدتها شهر ونصف
His saying: (And whoever is freed during the 'iddah of raj‘iy, not bā’in nor death, is like a free woman) meaning: And the 'iddah of a slave woman when she is in 'iddah from a raj‘iy divorce is like the 'iddah of a free woman from the beginning, so her 'iddah changes; if she is of menstruating age, her 'iddah becomes three menses, otherwise three months—unlike if it is from a bā’in divorce or death, for her 'iddah does not change, because the marriage persists in raj‘iy in every respect and ceases in bā’in and death. He restricted it to 'iddah, because if a slave woman's husband took an oath of ilā’ from her and then she was freed, the period of his ilā’ transfers to the period of free men, because separation is not a ruling of ilā’ from the beginning, as it is only established after the period, so the marriage exists presently, thus it resembles raj‘iy divorce. And in Fatḥ al-Qadīr: And he illustrated the transfer to all quantities of the simple 'iddah, which are four: its form is a young married slave woman divorced raj‘iyyan, so her 'iddah is a month and a half.
Source pages p. 232

Commentary
This is a ruling statement with proof. The text explains that if a slave woman (umm walad or regular slave) is freed while she is in her 'iddah from a raj‘iy (revocable) divorce, her 'iddah changes from that of a slave (normally half of a free woman's 'iddah) to that of a free woman. For a menstruating woman, it becomes three full menses; for a non-menstruating woman, three months. This change does not occur if the 'iddah is from a bā’in divorce or from the husband's death, because in those cases the marriage has already ended. The reason given is that in raj‘iy divorce, the marriage persists in every respect, so when she becomes free, the stricter 'iddah applies. The text then gives an analogy with ilā’: if a slave woman is freed during an ilā’ oath, the waiting period transfers to that of a free man because the marriage is still intact. Finally, a citation from Fatḥ al-Qadīr illustrates the various possible 'iddah periods for a slave woman in different scenarios.

Key Terms
أمة (amah): A female slave; her 'iddah is normally half that of a free woman (two menses or 1.5 months).
عدة الرجعي ('iddat al-raj‘iy): The 'iddah of a woman who has received a revocable divorce, during which the husband may take her back without a new marriage contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a slave woman is freed during her 'iddah from a raj‘iy divorce, what happens to her 'iddah?
A) It remains half of a free woman's 'iddah (2 menses or 1.5 months).
B) It changes to that of a free woman (3 menses or 3 months).
C) She must start a new 'iddah from the day of emancipation.
D) She is no longer required to observe any 'iddah.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, a slave woman's 'iddah converts to that of a free woman if she is freed during a revocable (raj‘iy) divorce because the marriage is still intact.

Iddah of a Slave Woman Emancipated During Revocable Divorce
Explanation of iddah transition upon slavery status change

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the change of iddah for a slave woman emancipated during revocable divorce, explain the reasoning behind it, and distinguish it from final divorce or death cases.

Core Idea
Emancipation during revocable divorce changes iddah; marriage remains, so status upgrades.

This passage addresses a key fiqh question: what happens to a slave woman's iddah if she is freed while in the waiting period? The Hanafi position is that her iddah changes to that of a free woman, but only if the divorce was revocable. The reasoning hinges on the nature of the marriage tie during revocable divorce. The commentary also warns against erroneous interpretations that appeared in some later texts, and lists several sub-cases of iddah transition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is the emancipation of a slave woman during her iddah. The ruling: if the divorce is revocable, her iddah becomes that of a free woman (three menstrual cycles or three months).
The matn says: 'من عتقت في عدة الرجعي لا البائن والموت كالحرة'
2
Trace the reasoning (ta'lil)
The change occurs because in revocable divorce, the marriage bond remains intact in all respects. In final divorce or death, the marriage is severed, so no upgrade. The analogy with ila' further supports this: if a slave is freed during ila', the period transfers to that of a free woman because the marriage still exists.
Ta'lil: بقاء النكاح في الرجعي من كل وجه وزواله في البائن والموت
3
Analyze the mistake and correction
Some texts mistakenly claim that if a man divorces revocably during death illness, the iddah changes to death iddah immediately. The author clarifies this only applies if death actually occurs during iddah; the expressions that suggest otherwise are false and not from the founding imams.
The passage calls these expressions 'باطل' and warns they occur in great books.
4
Review sub-cases of iddah transfer
The passage lists six scenarios where iddah changes due to change in status (e.g., minor attaining puberty, post-menopausal woman menstruating, etc.). These are analogous in principle: the iddah adapts to the current status of the woman.
These are found in al-Khaniyya and other sources.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (ومن عتقت في عدة الرجعي لا البائن والموت كالحرة) أي وعدة الأمة إذا معتدة عن طلاق رجعي كعدة الحرة في الابتداء فتتغير عدتها إلى عدة الوفاة، فإن كانت من ذوات الأقراء صارت عدتها ثلاثة حيض وإلا فثلاثة أشهر بخلاف ما إذا كانت عن بائن أو وفاة فإن عدتها لا تتغير لبقاء النكاح في الرجعي من كل وجه وزواله في البائن والموت. قيد بالعدة لأن الأمة لو آلى منها ثم أعتقت انتقل مدة إيلائها إلى مدة الحرائر لأن البينونة ليست من أحكام الإيلاء في الابتداء لأنها لا تثبت إلا بعد المدة فكانت الزوجية قائمة للحال فأشبه الطلاق الرجعي. وفي فتح القدير : وقد صور ا الانتقال إلى جميع كميات العدة البيسطة وهي أربعة صورتها أمة صغيرة منكوحة طلقت رجعياً فعدتها شهر ونصف أشهر وعشر ترث منه، وأنها لو تزوجت بعد مضي أربعة أشهر ولم تحض فيها يصح نكاحها وأنها لو حاضت ثلاث حيض وتزوجت ولم يصح وكل ذلك باطل فبطلت تلك العبارات المخالفة، وأنها لم تصدر عن صاحب المذهب ولا أصحابه والذي صدرت عنه ابتداء أراد غير ظاهرها وهو أنه أراد الانتقال عن عدة الطلاق الرجعي لعدة الوفاة حال حياته لترث بموته فيها ولا يفيد ما أراده من الانتقال تلك العبارات. وقد أردت بهذا إيضاح بطلانها لتجتنب فإنها وقعت في أجل كتب المذهب . هذا حاصل ما ذكره في رسالته وحاشيته على الدرر والذي يظهر أنهم تسامحوا في تسمية المطلق رجعياً في مرض موته فاراً اعتماداً على ما قرروه في موضعه وروماً للاختصار، وحينئذ فليس المراد إلا ما إذا مات وهي في العدة وكون المراد حينئذ الانتقال إلى عدة الوفاة ظاهر فدعوى أنه ليس في تلك العبارات ما يفيده ممنوعة، وما ذكره من أوجه البطلان فيما إذا كان حياً وعلى ما قلنا من فلو حاضت في أثنائها انتقلت إلى حيضتين، فلو أعتقت قبل مضيهما صارت ثلاث حيض، فلو مات زوجها انتقلت إلى أربعة أشهر وعشر ا هـ وفيه نظر لأن هذه الصورة لم يجتمع فيها جميع كميات العدة أي عددها البسيطة لأن عدة الآيسة من جملة كميات العدة البسيطة ولم يذكرها ولذا قال في الخانية : وقد يجب على المرأة أربع عدد ولو ذكر كذلك لسلم. وحاصل مسائل انتقال العدة مسائل : الأولى صغيرة اعتدت فبلغت في خلالها تستقبل بالحيض، مبتوتة كانت أو رجعية الثانية آيسة حاضت في أثناء الشهور أو حبلت تستقبل بالحيض أو بالوضع . الثالثة اعتدت بحيضة أو حيضتين ثم ارتفع حيضها لا تخرج من العدة ما لم تيأس فإذا أيست استقبلتها بالأشهر الرابعة آيسة اعتدت بالأشهر ثم حاضت وستأتي. الخامسة أعتقت الأمة بعد الطلاق أو الموت وقد قدمناها السادسة مات زوج الحرة المطلقة في عدتها وقد قدمناها في زوجة الفار .
His saying: (And whoever is freed during revocable divorce, not during final or death, is like a free woman). That is, the iddah of a slave woman when she is in iddah from a revocable divorce is like the iddah of a free woman in its beginning, so her iddah changes to the iddah of death. If she is of those who menstruate, her iddah becomes three menstrual cycles; otherwise three months. Unlike if it is from a final divorce or death, for her iddah does not change due to the marriage remaining in all respects in revocable divorce and its removal in final and death. He restricted it to iddah because if a slave woman is under ila' (vow of abstinence) then is freed, the period of her ila' transfers to that of free women because separation (baynuna) is not a ruling of ila' in the beginning, since it only establishes after the period, and so marital connection is present at the moment, resembling revocable divorce. In Fath al-Qadir: He has depicted the transition to all simpler quantities of iddah, which are four forms: a small slave woman married and divorced revocably, her iddah is one and a half months [??] months and ten, she inherits from him; and that if she remarried after four months and did not menstruate therein, her marriage is valid; and that if she menstruated three times and remarried, it is not valid; and all that is false, so those contradictory statements are invalid. And they did not originate from the master of the school nor his companions, and the one who originally stated them intended something other than their apparent meaning, namely that he intended transition from the iddah of revocable divorce to the iddah of death during his life so that she inherits by his death during it, and those expressions do not convey what he intended. I have intended by this to clarify their invalidity so that they be avoided, for they occur in the greatest books of the madhhab. This is the summary of what he mentioned in his treatise and his gloss on al-Durar. What appears is that they took liberties in calling a divorcer revocably in his death illness trying to escape, relying on what they established in its place and for brevity. Thus the intended meaning is only when he dies while she is in iddah, and then the intended transition to the iddah of death is apparent, so the claim that there is nothing in those expressions that conveys it is denied. And what he mentioned of aspects of invalidity when he is alive, and according to what we said... Then if she menstruated in its midst, she transfers to two menstruations; if she is freed before their completion, they become three menstruations; if her husband dies, she transfers to four months and ten. End. There is consideration because this case does not combine all quantities of iddah, i.e., its simple number, because the iddah of the post-menopausal woman is among the simple quantities of iddah, and he did not mention it. Hence it is said in al-Khaniyya: A woman may be obligated four iddahs. If he had mentioned it thus it would be fine. The summary of cases of iddah transfer: First: a minor who observes iddah then attains puberty in its midst, she starts anew with menstruation, whether it is final or revocable. Second: a post-menopausal woman who menstruates during the months or becomes pregnant, she starts anew with menstruation or with birth. Third: one who observes iddah with one or two menstruations then her menstruation ceases, she does not leave iddah until she becomes post-menopausal (i.e., reaches the age of despair), then when she despairs, she starts it with months. Fourth: a post-menopausal woman who observes iddah with months then menstruates, and it will come. Fifth: a slave woman freed after divorce or death, and we have presented it. Sixth: the husband of a free divorced woman dies during her iddah, and we have presented it in 'Zawjat al-Farr'.
Source pages p. 232

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the matn statement regarding the iddah of a slave woman who is emancipated during her waiting period. The author explains the core ruling: if she is in a revocable divorce, her iddah changes to that of a free woman—three menstrual cycles if she menstruates, or three months if she does not. However, if the divorce is final (ba'in) or due to death, her iddah does not change; she continues as a slave woman's iddah. The reasoning is that in revocable divorce the marriage is still intact, while in final divorce or death the marriage has ended. The author then addresses a specific issue regarding ila' (vow of abstinence): if a slave woman is under ila' and then freed, the period of her ila' transfers to that of a free woman, because the marriage is still present. Next, the passage critiques some mistaken expressions found in certain books of the madhhab that claim that if a man divorces his wife revocably during his death illness, her iddah changes to that of death while he is still alive, so that she can inherit. The author clarifies that the intended meaning is only if he dies while she is in iddah; then the transition is valid. The passage then provides several sub-cases of iddah transfer: a minor attaining puberty, a post-menopausal woman menstruating or becoming pregnant, a woman whose menstruation ceases, and others. These are examples where the iddah changes due to a change in the woman's status. The commentary emphasizes that these are the accepted Hanafi positions.

Key Terms
عدة (iddah): The waiting period a woman must observe after divorce or death of husband before remarrying
طلاق رجعي (talaq raj'i): Revocable divorce where the husband may take back his wife during the iddah without a new marriage contract
بائن (ba'in): Final divorce where the husband cannot take back his wife during iddah without a new contract
إيلاء (ila'): A vow of abstinence from marital relations for a period of at least four months

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the Hanafi ruling on the iddah of a slave woman who is emancipated during a revocable divorce?
A) Her iddah remains as a slave woman's iddah
B) Her iddah changes to that of a free woman
C) Her iddah becomes four months and ten days (death iddah)
D) Her iddah is invalid and she must start over

Key Takeaway
A slave woman's iddah upgrades to that of a free woman upon emancipation during a revocable divorce because the marriage bond persists; no such change occurs for final divorce or death.

Return of Menses After Months-Based Iddah: Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on invalidation of months-based iddah by return of menstruation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on whether return of menstruation invalidates months-based iddah, and identify the conditions for invalidation according to the preferred opinion.

Core Idea
Return of menstrual blood invalidates months-based iddah because it disproves menopause; condition is flowing blood.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq addresses a nuanced intra-Hanafi dispute: a woman in iddah with months due to assumed menopause sees blood again. Does that invalidate her iddah? The answer is not straightforward; scholars differed on the definition of menstrual blood and the timing of its appearance. This lesson unpacks the opinions and the preferred fatwa.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core ruling
The matn states that return of blood after months is menstruation, implying invalidation of the months-based iddah and requirement to restart with menstrual cycles.
This is the starting point of the dispute.
2
Understand the rationale
The return of blood contradicts the assumption of menopause. Months-based iddah is a substitute (khalaf) for menstrual iddah, valid only if menopause is permanent until death.
Analogy with fidyah for a very old man who cannot fast.
3
List the four opinions on blood definition
1) Copious flowing blood; 2) Red or black; 3) According to prior habit; 4) Any blood (absolute).
Each opinion has a different threshold for what counts as menstrual blood.
4
Analyze the timing dispute
Some say invalidation only if blood appears during the months, some only after, some absolutely (before, during, or after).
The timing affects marriages contracted after completion of months.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
Al-Mi'raj gives fatwa on the first opinion (copious flow). Al-Mujtaba considers the fourth opinion (absolute) correct. The absolute invalidation (al-Hidayah's choice) is also strong.
The fatwa is not monolithic; both views are relied upon.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (ومن عاد دمها بعد الأشهر الحيض) أي وعدة من اعتدت بالأشهر لإياسها ثم رأت دم الحيض فينتقض ما مضى من عدتها وعليها أن تستأنف العدة بالحيض، ومعناه إذا رأت الدم على العادة لأن عوده يبطل إياسها وهو الصحيح فظهر أنه لم يكن خلفاً، وهذا لأن شرط الخلفية تحقق اليأس وذلك باستدامة العجز إلى الممات كالفدية في حق الشيخ الفاني؛ كذا في الهداية. وظاهره فساد الأنكحة المباشرة قبل رؤية الدم وعبده وهو لازم الانتقاض كما في فتح القدير. واختلفوا في معنى قوله إذا رأت الدم على العادة فقيل معناه إذا كان سائلاً كثيراً احترازاً عما إذا رأت بلة يسيرة وقيل معناه ما ذكر وأن يكون أحمر أو أسود، كان أصفر أو أخضر أو تربية لا يكون حيضاً، وقيل معناه أن يكون على العادة الجارية فلو حتى لو كان عادتها قبل الإياس أصفر فرأته كذلك انتقض هكذا حكى الأقوال في فتح القدير من غير ترجيح وصرح في المعراج بأن الفتوى على القول الأول. وشمل إطلاق المصنف كالهداية ما إذا رأت قبل الحكم بإياسها أو بعده وهذا الإطلاق بجملته مختار صاحب الهداية وهو أحد الأقوال. وحاصله ينتقض مطلقاً، وسواء كان بعد الشهور أو في أثنائها، ولكن عبارة المصنف فيما إذا كان بعد الأشهر الثاني لا ينتقض مطلقاً واختاره الأسبيجابي. الثالث ينتقض إن رأته قبل تمام الشهور وإن كان بعدها فلا وبه أفتى الصدر الشهيد، وفي المجتبى وهو الصحيح المختار للفتوى الرابع تنتقض على رواية عدم التقدير للإياس التي هي ظاهر الرواية فإنما ثبت الأمر على ظنها فلما حاضت تبين خطؤها، ولا ينتقض على رواية
His saying: (And whoever's blood returns after the months, [it is] menstruation). That is, the iddah of one who observed iddah with months due to her menopause, then she sees menstrual blood: what has passed of her iddah is invalidated, and she must restart the iddah with menstruation. Its meaning is when she sees the blood in her usual manner, because its return negates her menopause, and this is the correct view. So it becomes clear that it was not a substitute (khalaf). This is because the condition for substitution is the realization of menopause, and that is by continuous inability until death, like the fidyah for a very old man; so in al-Hidayah. Its apparent implication is the invalidity of marriages contracted before seeing the blood and its servant? and it necessitates invalidation as in Fath al-Qadir. They differed on the meaning of his saying 'if she sees the blood in the usual manner.' Some said: its meaning is if it is flowing copiously, to avoid the case when she sees a slight moisture. Some said: its meaning is what is mentioned: that it be red or black, not yellow or green or dust-color (?) would not be menstruation. Some said: its meaning is that it be according to her ongoing habit, so if her habit before menopause was yellow and she sees it as such, it invalidates. Thus the opinions are reported in Fath al-Qadir without preference, and al-Mi'raj explicitly says the fatwa is on the first opinion. The absolute expression of the author (like al-Hidayah) includes whether she sees it before the ruling of her menopause or after, and this absolute expression in its entirety is the choice of the author of al-Hidayah and it is one of the opinions. Its summary: it invalidates absolutely, whether after the months or during them. However, the expression of the author [al-Kanz] is that if it is after the months, the second [opinion] does not invalidate absolutely, and al-Isbijabi chose it. Third: it invalidates if she sees it before completion of the months, but if after then not; and this is the fatwa of al-Sadr al-Shahid. In al-Mujtaba, it is the correct choice for fatwa. Fourth: it invalidates according to the narration of not specifying menopause, which is the apparent narration; because the matter was established based on her assumption, and when she menstruates, her error becomes clear; and it does not invalidate according to the narration [cut off].
Source pages p. 233

Commentary
This passage addresses the scenario of a woman who was observing her iddah with months because she was believed to be past menopause (a'isa). The matn says: 'whoever's blood returns after the months, it is menstruation.' The commentary explains that if she sees actual menstrual blood, the previous months-based iddah is invalidated, and she must restart with menstrual cycles. The reason is that her menopause was not confirmed; it was only assumed due to cessation. The condition for menopause to be established is the continuous cessation of menses until death, like the analogy of fidyah for an old man who cannot fast. The text then reports a dispute about the definition of 'seeing blood in the usual manner.' Four opinions are given: (1) it must be copious flow, not mere moisture; (2) it must be red or black, not yellow or green; (3) it must follow her previous habit (even if it was yellow); (4) it invalidates absolutely without condition. The author prefers the first opinion based on al-Mi'raj. There is further disagreement on timing: some say it invalidates only if seen during the months, some say only after, and some say absolutely. The 'absolute invalidation' view is held by the author of al-Hidayah. The mufta bihi according to al-Mi'raj and al-Mujtaba is that invalidation depends on seeing copious blood, and it invalidates the iddah whether seen before or after the months. This lesson is important for understanding how Hanafi scholars handle cases where a legal presumption (menopause) is contradicted by physical evidence (return of menses).

Key Terms
إياس (i'yas): Menopause; the permanent cessation of menstruation such that a woman is considered no longer capable of having menses
عدة بالأشهر (iddah bil ashhur): Iddah counted in months (three months) for women who do not menstruate (e.g., post-menopausal or young girls)
انتقاض العدة (intiqad al-iddah): Invalidation of the iddah that has already passed, requiring the woman to start the iddah over

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the preferred opinion (first opinion) in this passage, what condition must the blood meet to invalidate the months-based iddah?
A) Must be flowing copiously
B) Must be red or black
C) Must match her previous habit
D) Any blood suffices

Key Takeaway
The return of menstrual blood invalidates months-based iddah, but the definition of menstrual blood and the timing of its appearance are subject to scholarly disagreement within the Hanafi school.

Six Cases of Transition of 'Iddah
Enumerates six scenarios where 'iddah type changes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify each of the six cases where a woman's 'iddah type transitions in Hanafi law and state the correct new 'iddah for each.

Core Idea
A change in a woman's reproductive status during 'iddah requires a fresh start with the appropriate 'iddah type.

The waiting period ('iddah) is normally fixed based on the woman's status at the start: months for the menopausal or minor, three menses for the menstruating, and childbirth for a pregnant woman. However, status can change during the 'iddah, and Islamic law prescribes how to adjust. This lesson, drawn from al-Bahr al-Rāʾiq, presents six scenarios where the 'iddah must transition to a different type. Understanding these rules prevents errors in calculating the end of 'iddah and protects the woman's rights.

Guided Path
1
Parse the first two cases (minor and menopausal)
Identify the woman's initial status and the change. Case 1: a minor begins 'iddah with months, then reaches puberty → she must restart with three menses. Case 2: a menopausal woman begins 'iddah with months, then menstruates or becomes pregnant → she must restart with menses or childbirth.
Note that the first case applies to both irrevocable (bāʾin) and revocable (rajʿī) divorce.
2
Parse the third case (menses stop during 'iddah)
A woman who has had one or two menses, then her menses stop without known cause. She remains in 'iddah until she reaches definitive menopause (āʾisa), then she must restart with three months.
The key is that she does not exit 'iddah until menopause is certain.
3
Parse the fourth case (menopausal woman bleeds)
A menopausal woman observes 'iddah with months, but then she menstruates. The full ruling is given later in the text (the author promises a separate discussion). For now, note that the transition is to menses-based 'iddah.
This case is referenced but not elaborated here; it will be covered in the next lesson.
4
Parse the fifth and sixth cases (slave woman and death of husband)
Case 5: a slave woman is divorced or widowed, begins 'iddah with two menses (half of free woman's), then is emancipated during the 'iddah. Her 'iddah transitions to that of a free woman. Case 6: a woman in 'iddah from a revocable divorce when her husband dies; she must switch to the 'iddah of widowhood (four months and ten days).
These are covered in earlier sections of the text and are summarised here for completeness.
5
Consolidate the six cases
Review all six cases: (1) minor reaches puberty → menses; (2) menopausal woman menstruates or conceives → menses/delivery; (3) menses stop → stay until menopause then months; (4) menopausal woman bleeds → menses; (5) slave freed → free woman's 'iddah; (6) husband dies → widow's 'iddah.
The common thread: any change in reproductive capacity or legal status that would have altered the initial 'iddah requires a fresh start.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وحاصل مسائل انتقال العدة مسائل : الأولى صغيرة اعتدت فبلغت في خلالها تستقبل بالحيض، مبتوتة كانت أو رجعية الثانية آيسة حاضت في أثناء الشهور أو حبلت تستقبل بالحيض أو بالوضع . الثالثة اعتدت بحيضة أو حيضتين ثم ارتفع حيضها لا تخرج من العدة ما لم تيأس فإذا أيست استقبلتها بالأشهر الرابعة آيسة اعتدت بالأشهر ثم حاضت وستأتي. الخامسة أعتقت الأمة بعد الطلاق أو الموت وقد قدمناها السادسة مات زوج الحرة المطلقة في عدتها وقد قدمناها في زوجة الفار .
Summary of the cases of transition of 'iddah: The first: A minor who observes 'iddah, then reaches puberty during it, she starts anew with menses, whether the divorce was irrevocable or revocable. The second: A menopausal woman who menstruates during the months or becomes pregnant, she starts anew with menstruation or with childbirth. The third: One who observes 'iddah with one menses or two menses, then her menses stop, she does not exit 'iddah until she reaches menopause; when she reaches menopause, she starts anew with months. The fourth: A menopausal woman who observed 'iddah with months, then menstruates, and it will come later. The fifth: A slave woman who is emancipated after divorce or death, and we have previously mentioned it. The sixth: The husband of a freewoman who is divorced dies during her 'iddah, and we have previously mentioned it in the section on the wife of a missing person.
Source pages p. 233

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Rāʾiq enumerates six cases where a woman's 'iddah shifts from one type to another due to a change in her status. The first case: a minor (below puberty) who begins 'iddah with months (since she doesn't menstruate) but reaches puberty during the 'iddah; she must switch to waiting through three menstrual cycles, regardless of whether the divorce was revocable or irrevocable. The second: a woman past menopause who starts 'iddah with months but then menstruates or becomes pregnant; she switches to menses or delivery. The third: a woman who has had one or two menses during 'iddah, then her menses cease; she remains in 'iddah until she is definitively menopausal, then she starts months. The fourth: a menopausal woman who observed months but then menstruates; this is detailed later (the focus of the next lesson). The fifth and sixth refer to earlier discussions: a slave woman freed during 'iddah and a woman whose husband dies during her revocable divorce iddah. These are standard Hanafi rules. The teacher should note that each case assumes the woman was validly in iddah and a change occurs, requiring a fresh start with the appropriate iddah period.

Key Terms
عدة (iddah): The waiting period a woman must observe after divorce or death of husband before remarrying.
آيسة (āʾisa): A woman past menopause who no longer menstruates.
حيض (hayḍ): Menstruation, used to calculate iddah for women who menstruate.
صغيرة (ṣaghīra): A minor girl who has not yet reached puberty.
مبتوتة (mabtūta): A woman divorced with an irrevocable divorce (bāʾin).
رجعية (rajʿiyya): A woman divorced with a revocable divorce (rajʿī), where the husband may take her back during the iddah without a new contract.
أمة (ama): A female slave.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A woman begins her 'iddah with three menstrual cycles. After two menses, her menses stop completely, and it is later confirmed that she has entered menopause. What is her 'iddah ruling?
A) She completes the third menses as soon as it returns.
B) Her 'iddah is already complete because she had two menses.
C) She must switch to a months-based 'iddah of three months.
D) She must wait indefinitely until menopause is certain, then restart with three months.

Key Takeaway
A change in a woman's reproductive status or legal standing during 'iddah triggers a fresh start with the appropriate new 'iddah type.

Blood Returns After Months Nullifies Menopausal 'Iddah
Explains the ruling and condition for a menopausal woman who menstruates after completing months iddah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a menopausal woman who menstruates after completing months iddah, and identify the conditions under which her previous iddah is nullified.

Core Idea
If a menopausal woman sees real menstrual blood after months iddah, the months are void and she restarts with menses.

This lesson examines a critical scenario in Hanafi iddah law: a woman considered menopausal completes her iddah by three months, then menstrual blood returns. What happens to her iddah? The answer hinges on the authenticity of her menopause and the nature of the blood. We will explore the ruling, its proof, and the intra-Hanafi dispute on what constitutes menstrual blood for nullification.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and ruling
The woman was considered menopausal, so she observed iddah by three months instead of three menses. Then she sees menstrual blood. The Hanafi ruling: her previous iddah is nullified, and she must now observe iddah by menses.
This applies whether she sees blood before or after a formal ruling of menopause.
2
Understand the condition of the blood
The blood must be 'on her usual habit' – meaning it must be actual menstrual blood: flowing, red or black (not yellow/green), and consistent with her pre-menopausal pattern. Mere spots or non-menstrual discharge do not nullify.
There is an intra-Hanafi dispute on this condition; the mufta bihi requires flowing blood.
3
Analyze the proof (qiyas)
The ruling is based on analogy to a decrepit old man who pays ransom for inability to fast, then later becomes able: the ransom is invalid because the condition of permanent inability was not met. Similarly, the months iddah is a substitution (khalafiyyah) for menses, conditional on permanent menopause; return of blood proves the condition was false, so the substitution is void.
This reasoning is from al-Hidayah and is a dalil aqli (rational proof).
4
Note the consequences
The nullification is retroactive: any marriage contracted after the months iddah is invalid and must be dissolved. The woman must separate and observe a fresh iddah with menses from the original divorce.
This is a severe consequence, showing the importance of verifying menopause before issuing fatwas.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (ومن عاد دمها بعد الأشهر الحيض) أي وعدة من اعتدت بالأشهر لإياسها ثم رأت دم الحيض فينتقض ما مضى من عدتها وعليها أن تستأنف العدة بالحيض، ومعناه إذا رأت الدم على العادة لأن عوده يبطل إياسها وهو الصحيح فظهر أنه لم يكن خلفاً، وهذا لأن شرط الخلفية تحقق اليأس وذلك باستدامة العجز إلى الممات كالفدية في حق الشيخ الفاني؛ كذا في الهداية. وظاهره فساد الأنكحة المباشرة قبل رؤية الدم وعبده وهو لازم الانتقاض كما في فتح القدير. واختلفوا في معنى قوله إذا رأت الدم على العادة فقيل معناه إذا كان سائلاً كثيراً احترازاً عما إذا رأت بلة يسيرة وقيل معناه ما ذكر وأن يكون أحمر أو أسود لا أصفر أو أخضر أو تربية لا يكون حيضاً، وقيل معناه أن يكون على العادة الجارية فلو كان عادتها قبل الإياس أصغر فرأته كذلك انتقض هكذا حكى الأقوال في فتح القدير من غير ترجيح وصرح في المعراج بأن الفتوى على القول الأول. وشمل إطلاق المصنف كالهداية ما إذا رأت قبل الحكم بإياسها أو بعده وهذا الإطلاق بجملته مختار صاحب الهداية وهو أحد الأقوال.
His statement: 'And whoever's blood returns after the months, that is menstruation' meaning: the iddah of a woman who observed iddah by months due to her menopause, then she sees menstrual blood—then what has passed of her iddah is nullified and she must start the iddah anew with menstruation. And its meaning is if she sees blood in her usual habit, because its return invalidates her menopause, and this is the correct view. Thus it appears that it was not actual menopause. This is because the condition for substitution is certainty of menopause, and that is by the continuation of cessation until death, like the ransom in the case of the decrepit old man; as stated in al-Hidāyah. And its apparent meaning is the nullification of marriages contracted before seeing the blood... (continues). And they differed regarding the meaning of his statement 'if she sees blood in the usual habit': some said meaning if it is flowing abundantly, as a precaution against [the case where] she sees a little moisture; and some said the meaning is what was mentioned, that it be red or black, not yellow or green... etc. And some said meaning it be in accordance with the habitual cycle, so if her habit before menopause was yellow and she sees it thus, it nullifies. These opinions are reported in Fatḥ al-Qadīr without preference, and in al-Miʿrāj it is stated that the fatwa is on the first opinion. And the absolute expression of the author, like al-Hidāyah, includes whether she saw before the ruling of menopause or after; and this absolute expression in its entirety is the choice of the author of al-Hidāyah, and it is one of the opinions.
Source pages p. 233

Commentary
This passage discusses a key Hanafi ruling: if a woman who was considered menopausal (āʾisa) and had already observed her iddah by three months then sees menstrual blood, her previous iddah is invalid and she must start over with three menstrual cycles. The reason: the return of blood proves she was not truly menopausal. The condition ‘on the usual habit’ is explained: the blood must be such that it qualifies as menstruation (flowing, not just a spot; red/black, not yellow/green; and consistent with her pre-menopausal pattern). There are three interpretations, and the fatwa is on the first (that it must be flowing). Importantly, the ruling applies whether she sees the blood before or after a formal ruling of menopause. This absolute view is the choice of the author of al-Hidāyah. The commentary also notes that any marriages contracted after the months iddah (thinking it was valid) are nullified retroactively, which is a serious consequence.

Key Terms
إياس (i'yās): Menopause, the cessation of menstruation expected to be permanent.
خلفية (khalafiyyah): Substitution: the months iddah stands in place of menses iddah for menopausal women.
انتقاض (intiqāḍ): Nullification of a legal status or period.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A woman considered menopausal completes her iddah by three months. She then sees a small yellow spot that does not flow. According to the mufta bihi, what is the ruling?
A) Her iddah is nullified and she must restart with menses.
B) Her iddah remains valid because yellow non-flowing discharge is not menstrual blood.
C) She must wait to see if it turns red for three days.
D) She must repeat the months iddah.

Key Takeaway
A menopausal woman's months iddah is conditional on permanent menopause; if menstrual blood returns, the iddah is void and she must restart with menses.

Age of Menopause in Hanafi Law
Discusses opinions on menopause age and conditions for ruling a woman menopausal

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the mufta bihi age of menopause in the Hanafi school and explain the additional condition of prolonged cessation of menses.

Core Idea
Menopause is determined by age 55 plus cessation of menses for six months - not by age alone.

Determining when a woman is legally menopausal affects her 'iddah after divorce or death. The Hanafi school has a rich internal discussion on whether menopause is tied to a fixed age or left to ijtihad. In this lesson, we unpack the key opinions and the relied-upon position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Zahir al-Riwayah position
Recognize that the most authoritative transmission does not fix a numerical age; menopause is determined by ijtihad based on the woman's physical condition and norms.
This preserves flexibility.
2
Review the later estimations
List the various fixed ages proposed: 50 (Ibn Muqatil), 55 (majority), 60, 70 (Saffar). Note the fatwa is on 55 years without distinction between Roman and other women.
55 is the mufta bihi.
3
Identify the additional condition
Beyond age, a woman is only ruled menopausal if her menses have ceased for a 'long period,' defined as six months (al-ashah). This cessation can begin before age 55.
Age alone is insufficient.
4
Apply the ruling to a scenario
If a woman is 55 and has not menstruated for 6+ months, she is menopausal and observes 'iddah by months. If cessation is shorter, she must wait for three menstrual cycles.
Practical consequence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم اعلم أنه لا تقدير لسن الإياس في ظاهر الرواية . وإياسها على هذا أن تبلغ من السن ما لا يحيض فيه مثلها وذلك يعرف بالاجتهاد والمماثلة في تركيب البدن والسمن والهزال وفي رواية فيه تقدير . قال الصدر الشهيد المختار خمس وخمسون سنة وعليه أكثر المشايخ وفي المنافع وعليه الفتوى؛ كذا في المعراج . ثم قال بعده قال ابن مقاتل : حده خمسون سنة وهو مروي عن عائشة رضي الله عنها وعليه الفتوى. وقيل ستون، وقيل لا تلد لستين إلا قرشية . وقال الصفار : سبعون سنة. وقدر محمد في الروميات خمساً وخمسين سنة، وفي غيرهن ستين وعنه سبعين وفي الخانية : لا فرق بين الرومية وغيرها وهو خمس وخمسون سنة وعليه الفتوى. وفي الاختيار المرأة إذا لم تحض أبداً حتى بلغت مبلغاً لا يحيض فيه أمثالها غالباً حكم بإياسها . وذكر في الجامع الصغير إذا بلغت ثلاثين سنة ولم تحض حكم بإياسها . وفي القنية : طلق المدخول بها وعمرها خمس وخمسون سنة ثم مضى عليها أربعة أشهر لا تحيض ليس له أن يتزوج بنت أخيها حتى تنقضي مدة الحبل ثم ثلاثة أشهر للاحتياط ا هـ . ذكره في الحقائق شرح المنظومة النسفية في باب الإمام مالك ونصه : وعندنا ما لم تبلغ حد الإياس لا تعتد بالأشهر وحده خمس وخمسون سنة هو المختار لكنه يشترط للحكم بالإياس في هذه المدة أن ينقطع الدم عنها مدة طويلة وهي ستة أشهر في الأصح . ثم هل يشترط أن يكون انقطاع ستة أشهر بعد مدة الإياس؟ الأصح أنه ليس بشرط حتى لو كان منقطعاً قبل مدة الإياس ثم تمت مدة الإياس وطلقها زوجها يحكم بإياسها وتعتد بثلاثة أشهر . هذا هو المنصوص في الشفاء في الحيض وهذه دقيقة تحفظ ا هـ .
Then know: there is no estimation for the age of menopause in the apparent transmission (Zahir al-Riwayah). And her menopause on this view is that she reaches an age at which someone like her does not menstruate, and that is known by ijtihad and by similarity in body composition, plumpness, and emaciation. And in another transmission there is an estimation. Al-Sadr al-Shahid said: the chosen view is 55 years, and upon it are most of the mashayikh, and in al-Manafi' the fatwa is upon it; as in al-Mi'raj. Then he said afterward: Ibn Muqatil said: its limit is 50 years, and it is narrated from Aisha (may Allah be pleased with her), and upon it is fatwa. And it was said sixty; and it was said: a woman does not give birth at sixty except a Qurayshi woman. And al-Saffar said: seventy years. And Muhammad estimated for Roman women 55 years, and for other women sixty, and from him seventy. And in al-Khaniyyah: there is no difference between a Roman woman and other, and it is 55 years, and upon it is fatwa. And in al-Ikhtiyar: a woman who never menstruates until she reaches an age where her peers generally do not menstruate, she is ruled to be menopausal. And it is mentioned in al-Jami' al-Saghir: if she reaches 30 years and has not menstruated, she is ruled to be menopausal. And in al-Qunyah: if a man divorces his wife with whom he has consummated marriage and she is 55 years old, then four months pass without her menstruating, he may not marry her niece until the period of pregnancy passes, then three months as a precaution. End quote. Mentioned in al-Haqa'iq Sharh al-Manzumah al-Nasafiyyah in the chapter of Imam Malik: According to us, as long as she has not reached the limit of menopause, she does not observe iddah by months. Its limit is 55 years, which is the chosen view, but it is required for the ruling of menopause in this period that blood is cut off from her for a long period, which is six months according to the most correct view. Then: is it required that the cessation of six months be after the age of menopause? The most correct is that it is not a condition, so even if the cessation was before the age of menopause and then the age of menopause occurred and her husband divorces her, she is ruled to be menopausal and she observes iddah by three months. This is what is stated in al-Shifa' on menstruation, and this is a subtle point to be retained. End quote.
Source pages p. 234

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Rāʾiq addresses a crucial point: the age at which a woman is considered menopausal (āʾisa) in the Hanafi school. The Zahir al-Riwayah does not fix a specific age; rather, menopause is determined by ijtihad based on the woman's physical condition and the norm for women of her type. However, a later transmission (riwayat tasamuh) fixes an age. Most of the later scholars prefer 55 years as the age of menopause, and that is the fatwa. Other opinions exist: 50 (narrated from Aisha), 60, or 70 (al-Saffar). Imam Muhammad distinguished between Roman women (55) and others (60). But the Khaniyyah says no difference, and the fatwa is 55. Additionally, the text from al-Haqa'iq adds that to rule menopause, the cessation of menses must be for a long period, which is six months (the most correct view). This cessation can occur before the age of 55 and continue into it, and then she is ruled menopausal when she reaches 55. Important practical note from al-Qunyah: after a menopausal woman completes her iddah of months (for death, 4 months and 10 days? The text says 4 months then 3 months precaution — actually, the iddah for death is 4 months and 10 days, but here it mentions 4 months only, and then 3 months additional precaution. This needs careful handling. The teacher should emphasize that the age of menopause is not as simple as a fixed number; there are nuances, and the combination of age (55) and a long cessation (6 months) is the most reliable indicator.

Key Terms
سن الإياس (sin al-i'yās): The age at which a woman is legally considered menopausal.
اجتهاد (ijtihād): Independent reasoning to determine menopause based on physical signs and norms.
احتياط (iḥtiyāṭ): Precautionary measure; here, an extra three months added after the 4-month iddah for safety.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the most relied-upon age of menopause in the Hanafi school according to this text?
A) 50 years
B) 55 years
C) 60 years
D) 70 years

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi mufta bihi for menopause is 55 years plus cessation of menses for six months, combining a fixed age with a clinical observation.

Age of Menopause: Intra-Hanafi Dispute and the Mufta Bihi
Intra-Hanafi dispute on age of menopause with tarjih.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the range of Hanafi opinions on the age of menopause, identify the mufta bihi (55 years with six months amenorrhea), and apply it to 'iddah calculation.

Core Idea
The Hanafi school has no single fixed age for menopause in its core texts; the mufta bihi of 55 years is a later tarjih.

The age at which a woman becomes menopausal (iyas) directly affects her 'iddah: a premenopausal woman observes 'iddah by menstruations, while a menopausal woman observes three months. Hanafi scholars debated this age extensively, with opinions ranging from 50 to 70 years. This lesson unpacks that debate, the authority levels, and the relied-upon position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal issue
The core question: At what age is a woman considered menopausal so that her 'iddah becomes three months rather than menstrual cycles?
This affects marriage, divorce, and inheritance timelines.
2
Survey the opinions recorded in the text
List the six or more opinions: 50 (Ibn Muqatil from Aisha), 55 (al-Sadr al-Shahid, majority), 60, 70 (al-Saffar), and the Zahir al-Riwayah view that age is not fixed but determined by ijtihad.
Also note the 'no fixed age' view in al-Hidayah.
3
Analyze the Zahir al-Riwayah vs. later authorities
Zahir al-Riwayah (the most authoritative layer) does not specify an age; the mufta bihi (55) comes from fatawa mutakhkhirin. Understand how later scholars exercised tarjih.
Al-Sadr al-Shahid and the majority of mashayikh chose 55.
4
Understand the condition of six months amenorrhea
Even at age 55, a woman must have six consecutive months without menstruation to be ruled menopausal. This condition is mentioned in the text as a safeguard.
The cessation can occur before reaching 55; if so, menopause is retroactively confirmed.
5
Apply the mufta bihi to a scenario
Given a woman aged 55 who has not menstruated for six months, state her 'iddah ruling: three months. If she menstruates again after that, the previous 'iddah is valid (per the majority).
Refer to the sixth opinion in the text about future vs. past invalidity.

He claims the estimation for it, and it was chosen in al-Idah, and limited to it in al-Khaniyya, and al-Quduri and al-Jassas affirmed it, and supported it in al-Bada'i. Fifth: she becomes invalid if no judgment of her menopause was made, but if judgment was made, then such and such, as if one of them [claims] invalidity of marriage, so he judges its validity - this is the saying of Muhammad ibn Muqatil and he corrected it in al-Ikhtiyar. Sixth: she becomes invalid in the future, so she does not observe 'iddah except by menstruation for the divorce afterwards, not for the past, so the marriages contracted after observing 'iddah by months are not invalidated. And he corrected it in al-Nawazil. So it is established that there are six corrected opinions. So one must consider what is established from the author of the school, the greatest Imam, may Allah be pleased with him. And al-Aqtah' explicitly stated, and followed him in Ghayat al-Bayan, that the apparent transmission (zahir al-riwaya) is the opinion of invalidity absolutely. And it is the choice of the author of al-Hidaya, so it is determined to adopt it. But it is based on the condition of verifying menopause in the later [part] of the months by text, and that verification of menopause only occurs by continuity of cessation until death. And he weakened it in Fath al-Qadir by refuting his statement, that is, by continuity of inability until death, etc., based on the fact that menopause is actually a belief of never happening, not knowledge of its non-existence. And in al-Qamus: al-ya's is despair, which is the opposite of hope, and cutting off of hope. End quote. Its limit is fifty-five years, which is the chosen opinion. But for the judgment of menopause in this period, it is a condition that blood be cut off from her for a long period, which is six months, according to the more correct opinion.
