ʿIddah in Fāsid Marriage: Contract Establishes the Bed
Author refutes previous Hanafi works on ʿiddah obligation in fāsid marriage

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school holds that ʿiddah is obligatory in a fāsid marriage from the time of contract and evaluate the opposing intra-Hanafi view.

Core Idea
In a fāsid marriage, the contract alone establishes the marital bed, obligating ʿiddah before ratification.

The question of ʿiddah in a fāsid marriage touches on when the marital bond (firāsh) is formed. Some Hanafi works argued that ʿiddah only begins after ratification, but a stronger transmission from al-Aṣl shows that the contract itself establishes the bed. This lesson examines the evidence and the correction of a common intra-Hanafi error.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disputed ruling
Determine what exactly is contested: whether ʿiddah is obligatory in a fāsid marriage before the contract is ratified (e.g., by the master in a slave woman's marriage).
The opposing view claims ʿiddah is not required before ratification.
2
Present the correct Hanafi view and its evidence
State the position: ʿiddah is obligatory because the firāsh is established by the contract itself. Identify the key evidence: the case from al-Aṣl and al-Ḥalwānī's commentary.
The case involves a slave woman who marries without permission, gives birth six months after the contract, and the child is attributed to the husband from the time of the contract.
3
Present the opposing view and its evidence
Contrast the view found in al-Ikhtiyār and al-Muḥīṭ: ʿiddah is not obligatory before ratification because lineage is not established in a suspended marriage.
This view analogizes fāsid marriage to a suspended (fudūlī) marriage where firāsh is not formed until ratification.
4
Analyze the disproving counter-evidence
Examine the case from al-Aṣl: the ruling that the child belongs to the husband from the time of the contract proves that the firāsh is formed by the contract, even without ratification. Al-Ḥalwānī explicitly states this as evidence.
The author declares the opposing view a mistake (sahw) because it contradicts this direct transmission.
5
Determine the muftā bihī
Conclude that the relied-upon position in the Hanafi school is that ʿiddah is obligatory in a fāsid marriage from the contract, following the text from al-Aṣl and al-Khāniyya.
This is the position that must be applied in fatwa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هذا الفرس بسرجه. هو المراد وليس الوطء المحرم سبباً لانقضاء ولا آلة له. وقيد بالنكاح الفاسد لأن المنكوحة نكاحاً موقوفاً كنكاح الفضولي لا تجب فيه العدة قبل الإجازة لأن النسب لا يثبت فيه لأنه موقوف فلم ينعقد في حق حكمه فلا يؤثر شبهة الملك والحل والعدة وجبت صيانة للماء المحترم عن الخلط واحترازاً . عن اشتباه الانساب ؛ كذا في الاختيار والمحيط وهو مشكل مخالف للرواية فقد نقل الزيعلي في النكاح الفاسد ما نصه : وذكر في كتاب الدعوى من الأصل إذا تزوجت الأمة بغير إذن مولاها ودخل بها الزوج وولدت لستة أشهر منذ تزوجها فادعاه المولى والزوج فهو ابن الزوج فقد اعتبره من وقت النكاح لا من الدخول ولم يحك خلافاً. قال الحلواني : هذه المسألة دليل على أن الفراش ينعقد بنفس العقد في النكاح الفاسد خلافاً لما يقوله البعض إنه لا ينعقد إلا بالدخول اهـ. فهو صريح في ثبوت النسب فيه ويتبعه وجوب العدة فكان ما في المحيط والاختيار سهواً. وفي الخانية : أم ولد تزوجت بغير إذن المولى فولدت لستة أشهر فصاعداً من وقت النكاح فادعاه المولى والزوج فإن الولد يكون للزوج في قولهم جميعاً اهـ .
This horse with its saddle. That is the intended meaning. Unlawful intercourse is not a cause for expiration nor a tool for it. It is conditioned by fāsid marriage because a woman married with a suspended marriage like the marriage of a guardian (fudūlī) does not require ʻiddah before ratification because lineage is not established in it, as it is suspended; thus it is not concluded regarding its ruling, so the semblance of ownership and permissibility does not affect it. And ʻiddah is prescribed to preserve respected water from mixing and to avoid confusion of lineages. Thus it is in al-Ikhtiyār and al-Muḥīṭ, and it is problematic, contrary to the transmission. For al-Zaylaʻī transmitted in al-Nikāḥ al-Fāsid what verbatim: 'And it is mentioned in Kitāb al-Daʻwā from al-Aṣl: if a slave woman marries without her master's permission, and her husband has intercourse with her, and she gives birth after six months from the time of marriage, and the master and the husband both claim the child, the child belongs to the husband, and he considered him from the time of marriage, not from intercourse, and he did not report a disagreement.' Al-Ḥalwānī said: 'This case is evidence that the bed (firāsh) is established by the contract itself in fāsid marriage, contrary to what some say that it is only established by entry.' So it is explicit in establishing lineage therein, and it follows that ʻiddah is obligatory. Thus what is in al-Muḥīṭ and al-Ikhtiyār was a mistake. And in al-Khāniyya: 'An umm walad married without the master's permission and gave birth after six months or more from the time of marriage, and the master and husband both claim the child, then the child belongs to the husband in all of their statements.'
Source pages p. 236

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute and critical refinement. The author identifies an error in two major Hanafi works, al-Ikhtiyār and al-Muḥīṭ, which stated that ʻiddah is not obligatory in a fāsid marriage before ratification because lineage is not established. The author corrects this by citing a narration from al-Zaylaʻī regarding a case in Kitāb al-Daʻwā from al-Aṣl. In that case, a slave woman marries without her master's permission, and the husband has intercourse. She gives birth after six months from the marriage. Both the master and the husband claim the child. The ruling is that the child is attributed to the husband, and this attribution is based on the time of the marriage contract, not the time of intercourse. Al-Ḥalwānī explains that this proves that in fāsid marriage, the bed (firāsh) is established by the contract itself, contrary to the view of some that it is only established by entry. Since lineage is established, ʻiddah is also obligatory. The author then cites al-Khāniyya where a similar ruling for an umm walad confirms that the child belongs to the husband by the contract alone. Thus, the author declares that the statements in al-Muḥīṭ and al-Ikhtiyār were a mistake (sahwan). The correct Hanafi position is that in fāsid marriage, the firāsh is established by the contract, so ʻiddah is obligatory even before consummation if the marriage is later ratified.

Key Terms
النكاح الفاسد (al-nikāḥ al-fāsid): A marriage that is defective in some condition but not void (bāṭil); in Hanafi fiqh, it requires ʻiddah if consummated or if the contract is later ratified.
الفراش (al-firāsh): The legal bed or marital bond that establishes lineage and ʻiddah. In this passage, the firāsh is established by the contract itself in fāsid marriage.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the case from al-Aṣl, when is the child attributed to the husband?
A) From the time of consummation only
B) From the time of the marriage contract
C) From the time of the master's ratification
D) From the time the child is born

Key Takeaway
In a fāsid marriage, the marriage contract itself establishes the marital bed, making ʿiddah obligatory from the time of the contract, even in the absence of ratification.

The ʻIddah of an Umm Walad: Duration and Conditions
Ruling on umm walad's ʻiddah with conditions and exceptions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ʻiddah duration for an umm walad under different conditions (menstruating, non-menstruating, pregnant) and identify the exceptions when she is prohibited to the master.

Core Idea
An umm walad observes a three-cycle ʻiddah upon master's death or freedom, but only if she is not already prohibited.

In this passage, the author clarifies the ʻiddah of a special category of slave woman: the umm walad (mother of the master's child). Though her bond is weaker than marriage, Hanafi fiqh prescribes a waiting period to protect lineage and observe precaution. The ruling is based on the practice of Caliph ʻUmar and is detailed with conditions for menstruating, non-menstruating, and pregnant women, as well as exceptions when she is already prohibited to the master.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Determine what legal act is being discussed and its hukm level. The passage rules on the ʻiddah of an umm walad, which is wajib (obligatory) when her firash (bed) is removed.
The removal occurs by master's death or freeing her.
2
Locate the dalil
Find the proof cited. The author relies on the statement of Caliph ʻUmar (ra): 'The ʻiddah of an umm walad is three menstrual cycles.' This is a Qawl Sahabi, valid in Hanafi usul.
The dalil is mentioned explicitly: عدة أم الولد ثلاث حيض
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
Explain how the dalil proves the ruling. ʻUmar's explicit statement is used as authoritative, though it is not a direct Quranic or Prophetic command. Hanafis accept Qawl Sahabi as binding when there is no contradicting evidence.
The school completed the deficient right (of the umm walad) with the perfect one (of a free woman) as a precaution.
4
List the conditions and exceptions
Extract the conditions from the text: she must be menstruating (otherwise three months), not married or in another's ʻiddah, and not nursing the master's son (which creates prohibition). If prohibited, no ʻiddah is due from the master.
The text also notes that if she is pregnant, her ʻiddah is until delivery.
5
Note the practical application
Apply the ruling to a scenario: e.g., a woman who bears her master's child, then her master dies. She must observe iddah of three cycles unless she is nursing his son (which makes her like a milk-mother, thus prohibited).
The exception is from the principle: no ʻiddah if the woman is forbidden to the master at the time of the event.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقت وأما عدة أم الولد فلأنها وجبت بزوال الفراش فأشبه عدة النكاح، وفراش أم الولد وإن كان أضعف من فراش المنكوحة إلا أنهما يشتركان في أصل الفراش والمحل محل الاحتياط فالحق القاصر بالكامل احتياطاً. وفي كافي الحاكم : لو أعتق أم ولده لا نفقة لها في عدته وإمامنا فيه عمر رضي الله عنه فإنه قال : عدة أم الولد ثلاث حيض. ودخل تحت قوله وغيره عتقها وهو مقيد بأن تكون من ذوات الحيض، فإن كانت من ذوات الأشهر ومات مولاها أو أعتقها فعدتها ثلاثة أشهر كما ذكرناه، وإن كانت حاملاً فوضع الحمل كما في الخانية، وبأن لا تكون منكوحة ولا معتدة لزوج فإن كانت لا عدة عليها من المولى إجماعاً لأنه لا فراش لها من المولى ووجوب العدة بزواله. والتحقيق أن يقال : الشرط في وجوب عدة المولى أن لا تحرم عليه بسبب من الأسباب وأسباب الحرمة عليه ثلاثة : نكاح الغير وعدته والثالث تقبيل ابن المولى. فلا عدة عليها بموت المولى أو إعتاقه بعد تقبيل ابنه كما في الخانية قال : ولذا لو أتت بولد بعد حرمتها لستة أشهر لا يثبت نسبه ما لم يدعه اهـ.
Time. As for the ʻiddah of an umm walad, it is obligatory due to the removal of the bed (firāsh), so it resembles the ʻiddah of marriage. The bed of an umm walad, although weaker than the bed of a married woman, still shares the origin of the bed, and the place is a place of precaution, so the deficient right is completed by the perfect one as a precaution. In Kāfī al-Ḥākim: 'If a man frees his umm walad, she has no maintenance during her ʻiddah.' And our imam in this is ʻUmar (may Allah be pleased with him), for he said: 'The ʻiddah of an umm walad is three menstrual cycles.' And under his statement is included her freedom, and it is conditioned that she be among those who menstruate. If she is among those who have months (i.e., due to age or amenorrhea) and her master dies or frees her, her ʻiddah is three months, as we mentioned. And if she is pregnant, her ʻiddah is until delivery, as in al-Khāniyya. And it is conditioned that she is not married nor in the ʻiddah of a husband, for if she is, she has no ʻiddah upon the master by consensus, because she has no bed (firāsh) from the master, and the obligation of ʻiddah is due to the removal of the bed. The investigation is to say: The condition for the obligation of the master's ʻiddah is that she not be prohibited to him due to any of the causes. The causes of prohibition upon him are three: marriage to another, her ʻiddah, and the third is the nursing (kissing?) of the master's son. So there is no ʻiddah upon her due to the master's death or his freeing after the nursing of his son, as in al-Khāniyya. He said: 'For this reason, if she brings a child after her prohibition within six months, his lineage is not established unless he claims it.'
Source pages p. 236

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof on the ʻiddah of an umm walad (a slave woman who has borne a child to her master). The author explains that her ʻiddah becomes obligatory when her bond (firāsh) is removed, either by the master's death or freeing her. Although her relationship is weaker than that of a married woman, precaution requires treating it similarly. The proof is from Caliph ʻUmar (ra), who ruled that her ʻiddah is three menstrual cycles, like a free woman. However, this is conditional: she must be menstruating; if she is too young or old (so she counts months), her ʻiddah is three months; if pregnant, it is until delivery. Furthermore, she must not be currently married or in the ʻiddah of a husband, because then she is prohibited to the master, and there is no firāsh to remove. The author then investigates the conditions more deeply: the obligation of the master's ʻiddah only applies if she is not already prohibited to him due to marriage, her ʻiddah from another, or nursing of the master's son (which would make her like a milk-mother). If she is prohibited, then no ʻiddah is due from the master. This is supported by a quote from al-Khāniyya: after such prohibition, if she gives birth within six months, the child is not attributed to the master unless he claims it.

Key Terms
أم الولد (umm al-walad): A slave woman who has borne a child to her master; upon his death or freeing her, she has a certain ʻiddah.
عدة (ʻiddah): A waiting period after dissolution of marriage or bond to observe purity and avoid mixing of lineages.
فراش (firāsh): The marital or concubinal bed; the legal bond that gives a woman the right to a waiting period upon removal.
تقبيل ابن المولى (taqbīl ibn al-mawlā): Nursing (or literally kissing) the master's son; creates a milk-relationship prohibition.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Three menstrual cycles
B) Three months
C) Until delivery
D) No ʻiddah

Key Takeaway
The ʻiddah of an umm walad is three cycles by default, but adjusted for non-menstruating and pregnant women, and voided if she is already prohibited to the master.

Combining ʻIddahs for Umm Walad When Order of Deaths Unknown
Author calculates ʻiddah periods for umm walad when order of deaths unknown

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to calculate the combined ʻiddah for an umm walad whose master and husband die in an unknown order, apply the Hanafi principle of precaution (ihtiyāt), and identify the ikhtilaf between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on the third scenario.

Core Idea
When the order of deaths is unknown, the longer ʻiddah is taken as precaution — but the exact calculation depends on the known interval.

The death of both a woman's master and husband creates a complex ʻiddah scenario, especially when the order is unknown. The Hanafi school uses precaution (ihtiyāt) to ensure the obligation is fulfilled. In this lesson, we break down the three possible scenarios and the intra-madhhab difference on the third.

Guided Path
1
Identify the woman's status
The woman is an umm walad who was freed and married a free man. Her original master's death requires an istibrā' period (three menstrual cycles), while her husband's death requires a full ʻiddah of four months and ten days (since she is free).
Key distinction: istibrā' vs ʻiddah of marriage.
2
Recognize the two possible sequences
If the master died first, then when the husband dies later she is already free, so only the husband's ʻiddah applies (4m10d). If the husband died first, she was a slave at that time, so she observes his ʻiddah (2m5d), and then upon the master's death, she must observe the istibrā' (3 menstrual cycles).
The ʻiddah for a slave widow is two months and five days.
3
Apply the first scenario: known interval < 2m5d
If it is known that the two deaths are within 60 days or less, the longer ʻiddah (4m10d) is taken because it covers both possibilities. The first sequence would require 4m10d, the second only half that, so the maximum is observed as precaution.
No combination; only the longer marriage ʻiddah.
4
Apply the second scenario: known interval ≥ 2m5d
If the interval is at least 60 days, then in the second sequence (husband first), the husband's ʻiddah expires before the master dies, so the master's death triggers the istibrā' (3 hayḍ). Thus she must observe both: 4m10d (if master first) or 2m5d + 3 hayḍ (if husband first). Since the order is unknown, she observes the combination: 4m10d + 3 hayḍ as a precaution.
Here the combination is required because the master's ʻiddah is separate.
5
Understand the third scenario and ikhtilaf
If the interval is unknown, Abu Hanifa treats it like the first scenario (4m10d only), while Abu Yusuf and Muhammad treat it like the second (4m10d + 3 hayḍ). The author presents both views without explicit tarjih.
This is a legitimate intra-madhhab ikhtilaf; both views are transmitted.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فلو طلقها بعد الإعتاق عليها عدة الحرائر وبانقضاء عدة النكاح تعود عدة المولى ثلاث حيض، ولو مات المولى والزوج ولا يدرني الأول فهي على ثلاثة أوجه : الأول أن يعلم أن بين موتهما أقل من شهرين وخمسة أيام فعليها أن تعتد بأربعة أشهر وعشر لأن المولى إن كان مات أولاً ثم مات الزوج وهي حرة فلا يجب بموت المولى شيء وتعتد للوفاة عدة الحرة، وإن كان الزوج مات أولاً وهي أمة لزمها شهران وخمسة أيام ولا يلزمها بموت المولى شيء لأنها معتدة الزوج، ففي حال يلزمها أربعة أشهر وعشر وفي حال نصفها فلزمها الأكثر احتياطاً، ولا تنتقل عدتها على الاحتمال .. الثاني لما قدمنا أنها لا تنتقل في الموت الثاني أن يعلم أن بين موتيهما شهرين وخمسة أيام فعليها أن تعتد بأربعة أشهر وعشر فيها ثلاث حيض احتياطاً لأن المولى إن كان مات أولاً لم تلزمها عدته لأنها منكوحة وبعد موت الزوج يلزمها أربعة أشهر وعشر لأنها حرة، وإن مات الزوج أولاً لزمها شهران وخمسة أيام وقد انقضت عدتها منه لأنها مصورة أن بينهما هذه أو أكثر، فموت المولى بعده يوجب عليها ثلاث حيض فتجمع بينهما احتياطا . الثالث أن لا يعلم كم بين موتيهما ولا الأول منهما فكالأول عنده وكالثاني عندهما؛ كذا في المعراج وغيره. وقيد بأم الولد لأن المديرة والأمة إذا أعتقت أو مات سيدها لا عدة عليهما بالإجماع كما ذكره الاسبيجابي. وفي فروق الكرابيسي: المعتدة في عدة الزوج تغسل زوجها ولا تغسل مولاها في عدته إذا كانت أم ولد لأنها ليست عدة النكاح بل هي استبراء اهـ.
If he divorces her after the freeing, then upon her is the ʻiddah of free women, and when the ʻiddah of marriage expires, the ʻiddah of the master returns as three menstrual cycles. And if the master and husband die and one does not know which was first, then there are three aspects: First: that it is known that between their deaths is less than two months and five days; then she must observe ʻiddah of four months and ten days, because if the master died first and then the husband died while she was free, nothing is obligatory due to the master's death and she observes ʻiddah for the death of a free woman. And if the husband died first while she was an slave, she would observe two months and five days, and nothing is due due to the master's death because she is in the ʻiddah of the husband. So in one case she must observe four months and ten days, and in the other half of that, so the greater is obligatory as a precaution, and her ʻiddah does not transition based on probability... Second: as we stated that she does not transition in the second death; if it is known that between their deaths is two months and five days, then she must observe four months and ten days including three menstrual cycles as a precaution, because if the master died first, his ʻiddah is not due since she is married, and after the husband's death she must observe four months and ten days as a free woman. And if the husband died first, she must observe two months and five days, and her ʻiddah from him has expired because she is considered to have that interval or more; then the master's death after that obligates three menstrual cycles, so she combines both as a precaution. Third: that it is not known how much between their deaths nor which was first; then it is like the first according to him (Imam Abu Hanifa) and like the second according to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad). Thus in al-Miʻraj and others. And it is conditioned (muqayyad) by umm walad because a mudabbara and a regular slave woman have no ʻiddah if freed or master dies, by consensus, as al-Isbījābī mentioned. And in Furūq al-Karābīsī: The woman observing ʻiddah from her husband may wash her husband but not her master during his ʻiddah if she is an umm walad, because it is not an ʻiddah of marriage but rather a period of istibrāʼ. End.
Source pages p. 236

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof presenting a detailed legal calculation for an umm walad who is also married to a free husband, and both the master and husband die, but the order of deaths is unknown. The ruling is built on precaution (ihtiyāt). There are three scenarios: 1) If the interval between deaths is less than 60 days (2 months 5 days), the longer ʻiddah (4 months 10 days) is taken because it covers the possibility of the husband dying first (entailing 2 months 5 days) or the master dying first (then later husband dies when she is free, entailing 4 months 10 days). 2) If the interval is exactly 2 months 5 days or more, she must observe 4 months 10 days plus three menstrual cycles (combining both possibilities) because if the husband died first, by the time the master dies her husband's ʻiddah is already over, so she must start the master's ʻiddah of three menstrual cycles. 3) If the interval is unknown, there is a disagreement: according to Abu Hanifa, it is like the first case (just 4 months 10 days), while according to the Sahibayn, it is like the second (4 months 10 days plus three menstrual cycles). The author notes that this applies only to umm walad, not to other slaves (mudabbara or regular amah) who have no ʻiddah by consensus. He also quotes al-Karābīsī that the woman in her husband's ʻiddah can wash her dead husband but not her dead master because the master's ʻiddah is not a true ʻiddah but istibrāʼ (a period to verify purity).

Key Terms
استبراء (istibrāʼ): A period of waiting to verify that the womb is free from pregnancy; in the context of umm walad, her ʻiddah upon master's death is considered istibrāʼ rather than full ʻiddah of marriage.
احتياط (ihtiyāt): Precautionary measure taken when there is uncertainty; here, the longer ʻiddah is taken to ensure that the obligation is fulfilled.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the first scenario where the interval between the master's and husband's deaths is less than two months and five days, what ʻiddah does the umm walad observe?
A) Two months and five days
B) Four months and ten days
C) Three menstrual cycles
D) Four months and ten days with three menstrual cycles

Key Takeaway
When the order of deaths is unknown, the Hanafi school prescribes the maximum possible ʻiddah as a precaution, with the exact calculation depending on whether the interval between deaths is known.
